THE RED PAGE.
MOIRA O’NEILL
THESE verses of hers were here
reprinted when they appeared in
Blackwood ’s; yet since they recur in-
sistently they may be printed again for
addition to the 'treasure of a chance literary
wayfarer. The little book that holds
them, Songs of the Glens of Antrim, has
passed through ten impressions since 1900 :
they are simple verses, yet so rare in art and
accent that you may search twenty books of
the Irish school without finding an analogue.
The use of dialect adds a beauty. English
water seems hard and cold besides the soft
Irish wather:
When she’d come laughin’ ’twas the runnin’
wather...
The words are admirably chosen and placed,
and a strong rhythmical beat fixes their mes-
sage ; the subjects are familiar characters, old
instincts, homely longings, the tender voices
of the past heard through regretful years.
The author does not express passions, is
content with sentiments, yet her light shafts
linger in yearning hearts ; and indeed her
verses are a kind of religion.
CORRYMEELA.
Over here in England I ’m helpin’ wi’ the hay,
An’ I wislit I was in Ireland the livelong day ;
Weary on the English hay, an’ sorra take the
wheat!
Och ! Corrymeela an’ the blue ski/ over it!
There’s a deep dumb river flowin’ by beyont the
heavy trees,
This livin’ air is moithered wi’ the bummin’ o’
the bees;
I wisht I’d hear the Claddagh burn go runnin’
I through the heat
Past Corrymeela, wi’ the blue sky over it..
The people that’s in England is richer nor the
Jews,
There’s not the smallest young gossoon but thravels
in his shoes !
I *d give the pipe between me teeth to see a barefut
child !
Och / Corrymeela an’ the low south wind.
Here’s hands so full o’ money an’ hearts so full o’
care,
By the luck o’ love ! I’d still go light for all I did
go bare.
“ God save ye, colleen dhas,” I said : the girl she
thought me wild.
Far Corrymeela, an' the low south wind.
D’ ye mind me now, the song at night is mortial
hard to raise,
The girls are heavy goin’ here, the boys are hard
to plase ;
When ones’t I’m out this workin’ hive, ’tis I’ll
be back again—
Ay, Corrymeela, in the same soft rain.
The puff o’ smoke from one ould roof before an
English town !
For a shauyh wid Andy Feelan here I’d give a
silver crown,
For a curl o’ hair like Mollie’s ye ’ll ask the like
in vain,
Sweet Corrymeela, an’ the same soft rain.
“The Ould Lad.”
I mind meself a wee boy wi’ no plain talk,
An’ standin’ not the height o’ two peats ;
There was things meself consated ’or the time that
I could walk,
An* who’s to tell when wit and childer meets ?
’Twas the daisies down in the low grass,
The stars high up in the skies,
The first IJmowed of a mother’s face
IWi’ the kind love in her eyes,
Och, och!
The kind love in her eyes.
I went the way of other lads that’s neither good
nor bad,
An’ still, d’ ye see, a lad has far to go ;
But the things meself consated when I wasn’t
sick nor sad,
They ’re aisy told, an’ little use to know.
’Twas whiles a boat on the say beyont,
An’ whiles a girl on the shore,
An’ whiles a scrape o’ the fiddle-strings,
Or maybe an odd thing more.
In troth,
Or maybe an odd thing more.
A man, they say, in spite of all, is betther for a
wife,
In-undher this ould roof I live me lone ;
I never seen the woman yet I wanted all me life,
An’ I never made me pillow on a stone.
’T-is “fancy buys the ribbon” an’ all,
An" fancy sticks to the young ;
But the man of his years can do wi’ a pipe,
Gan smoke an’ hould his tongue,
D’ ye mind.
Smoke an’ hould his tongue.
Ye see me now an ould man, his work near done,
Sure the hair upon me head’s gone white;
But the things meself consated ’or the time that I
could run,
They ’re the nearest to me heart this night.
Just the daisies down in the low grass,
The stars high up in the skies,
The first I knowed of a mother’s face
Wi’ the kind love in her eyes,
Och, och!
The kind love in her eyes.
SAID THE MAN FROM JEWBURG
IT ’S not as bad a place as you make it out
to be—Johannesburg. I know there’s
lots have n’t done too well there; but
plenty has. You get the yarns of the
men who do badly : the men who do well
don’t write. I’ve done pretty well myself ;
I’m not denying it. I put in live years there
—carpenter, builder, contractor, and all
sorts of odd jobs : I admit a man has to be a
tradesman to get on. If you ’re not, you
starve, unless you can put on a silk hat and
a frock-coat and live up to them. I landed
here a fortnight ago, but there’s nothing to
do here. I’ve looked round Adelaide, Mel-
bourne, Sydney —they’re all dead; bad spirit,
no encouragement. So I’m off over to
Fr’isco next boat.
There’s not many Australians in Africa
when you add them up, but you’d think
they run the place, they make such a lot of
noise. That’s the fault of them :it gets
them disliked, they can’t keep their mouths
shut. Yes, I’m Australian myself, though I
do wander round. You know an Australian
at once over there: lie’s as different
from the Englishman as an American
is. The Australians do just as well
as anybody else, and a bit better
than most : they ’re too smart for the
Englishman, that’s why he dislikes them.
You ;sea, over here a man has plenty of
friends, and he takes it easy. If you lose
your job, you’ve grown up with Tom, Dick,
and Harry, and they ’ll find you a job. Over
there it’s different: you’ve no friend but
yourself, and everybody waiting to rob you.
So the Australian finds himself on the edge
of a hole, and he hits out, and no blame to
him. When he’s got to fight, he can fight
better than'most of them, and that’s why
they howl.
It’s a country of thieves and liars. I
reckon I was honest enough when I went
over there, but after I’d been robbed once
or twice I saw I had to turn to and do a bit
of robbing myself. It’s either that or clear
out. They’ve no use for honesty in Johan-
nesburg : it’s run by the Jews on the prin-
ciple of collar what you can. I found the
Jews all right—the best kind of them : I’ve
nothing to complain of. They ’ll have you
if they can, but it’s what I gall a fair have ;
and you know what to look out for. I
would n’t say they have better brains
than Australians ; but they’ve got
better energy. That’s what I admire
about them, their persistent energy. The
Australian takes a rest now and then : the
Jew goes all the time. Of course there’s a
terrible lot of the other sort over there—what
they call Russian and Peruvian Jews : they’d
rob you of threepence as soon as your back’s
turned. Nothing’s too bad for them.
I Once you know them, you know Russia is
the only country that deals with them
properly : they ’re only fit for wiping out.
I don’t know what’s going to become of
the country : nobody knows. It ’ll never be
an English country as long as you live. The
Englishman only goes there for what
Ihe can make, and clears out. The English
aren’t ten per cent, of the white popu-
lation, after all the fuss. It’s a Dutch-
man’s country, Dutchmen and natives.
Nobody knows what ’ll turn up. The Boers
won’t say anything, won’t say what they
want or what they ’re going to do : nobody
knows. I like the Boers : they ’re a bit
thick in the head, but they’re a simple,
decent crowd. You don’t find them in
Johannesburg. Mines ? Well, there you
are. They’ve got Chinese labour now, at a
bob a day, and still it costs them 255. a ton
to raise and crush the stone. And the best
stone they’ve got isn’t worth more than 655.
a ton, with a two-foot reef. Not much in
that when you come to chase it down, consider-
ing all the capital invested. They’ve got
pretty well all the surface stuff, and whether
deep sinking is going to pay, with all their
cheap labour, is pretty doubtful. The Chows
are just slaves, of course, and they’re not doing
too well, not as well as the Kaffirs : you can’t
drive ’em past a certain point. The Kaffir
was just as cheap, but he would n’t hire for
more than three months : that’s where the
trouble was. The Chows go there for three
years, live or die, and too many of them are
dying : bringing up the expenses.
Sydney seems slow after Johannesburg :
your people have n’t got their heads pro-
perly screwed. You try to make a trade
over here, and then .you kill it by not look-
ing after it. Part of my time I was in the
cold storage business—another swindle ; and
a consignment of fowls came over from
Sydney : the boss showed ’em to me : good
birds all right, and their heads hacked off
and the blood let drip all over them—
shippers had n’t the sense to keep them
clean and attractive. Down went the price
a bob—just on the looks of them, see ? All
the Australian stuff is more or less like that
—good stuff spoilt for want of nous in
handling, or else no reliance on the quality
—first shipment tip-top, next rotten. And
there you are !
Well, I called in for some American papers,
not to gas like this.
FLOTSAM
Tolstoy’s new play, as translated and per-
formed in London, is summarised by a Times
critic: — «
A peasant woman, with the encouragement of an old
hag, poisons her husband in order that she may marry
her lover, a farm labourer. So soon as the farm labourer
[who, by way of hors d'oeuvre, has already seduced and
abandoned a village girl) is married and in possession lie
squanders his wife’s money, takes to drink, and takes his
wife’s step-daughter for his new mistress. By-and-by the
nistress gives birth to a child, which the wife, the hag,
and the farm labourer bury alive. Then the man con-
fesses, is handed over to the police, and the play—or
rather the nightmare—is over.
It is pretty, but is it Art ? That is what, occupied
with their personal sensations, the critics fail to
;ell us. One receives fresli light on the remarkable
[Russian peasant, a new reflection of the Age of
[?aith, and the critics object to the “ crack-crack -
ng ” of the bones of the infant undergoingmurder.
Tolstoy’s retort might be inferred. You can’t
make a moral Russian omelette without breaking
infants’ bones.
Lond. Times, by the way, has been engaging its
readers in “heart-to-heart talks” regarding'the
best means of ridding Olympus of wnite ants.
Jove the thunderer lending an affable ear to the
prattle of a worsh ipper is a sweet spectacle. W here
will this democratic spirit end ? That not all the
worshippers have gropers’ souls is seen in their
attitude toward ad vts., so naif, so noble. One likes
the readers who say that ad vts. are nuisances, and
ask that they should be placed in a separate sup-
plement for convenience of throwing-away. Better
still is the reader who suggests that all advertise-
ments, except possibly Births, Marriages, and
Deaths, and theatres, should be refused by The
Times, as they are “ probably of little general
interest except to the advertiser.”
Prof. Lounsbury, discussing “honour,” points
out that the forms honour and honor sprang up
side by side in Anglo French, and have flourished
together from the fourteenth century till to-day.
The spelling honor is no late Americanism, but good
old English : honour is no less and no more legiti-
mate. And as to current usage, you pay your
pleasure and take your choice. One’s own pre-
ference of honour has this reason : that it looks
better. It yields to the eye a rounder, fuller
image than honor, which seems “tight” and
meagre by'comparison. Pronounce it “honnoor”
and it sounds better than “fionner'” or “ honhor,”
because we have a long vowel-sound in place of a
short; and we want to preserve rich vowel-sounds
in English for the sake of the music and dignity of
speech and poetry. Then there is a reason of associ-
tion: now that one has grown up with honoicr, how
barren appears
I could not love thee, dear, so much
Loved I not honor more.
The thought has lost the rich odour, the sweet
savour, that comes with honour. And, finally,
the tendency to throw back accent is giving us
too many clipped and ugly monosyllables: the
language will lose variety if we do not try to
preserve words with sonorous or fair-seeming
endings. A word, like a yacht, wants a clear
“run” if it is to cut a picturesque figure or swim
suavely in waves of melody. Honour sails past
the eye like a swan, and august advances to the
ear with the dignity of a grave monarch in his
coronation robes. Honor is a bird plucked of its
tail-feathers, au'gust is a vulgar little boy in a
hurry : do you not feel it ?
The Saturday Review praises a Belgian critic’s
conclusion that the philosophic value of Swin-
burne’s poetry resides not in content but in form :
“it is breathed from the accent of a verse,
launched like an arrow from the quivering of a
stanza, or shed like a halo from the-silence that
follows. It is by virtue of rhythm that Swin-
burne reaches the vision transcendent, achieves
the metaphysical, power of divination. In him
the Absolute is manifested in the shapes of Num-
ber and Music only: his God descends in
a song.” The Salwrdag accepts this dictum
as a new revelation: but does it Contain any-
thing more than the old testimony ? Swinburne
is a technician: there is value in technic: and
then? The point is whether technic holds all the
value of poetry, or the chief value ; and one says
No, of course. The emotion received from
Number and Music is not to be preferred to the
emotion received from Ideas, much less to the
emotion received from Ideas plus Number and
Music. Yield. Swinburne all the poetic crafts-
man’s laurels and he will still have to win the
crown of the poetic artist.
H. S. Power, an Adelaide painter, offered by
auction at Lawson’s Sydney robms on 23rd Feb.
a catalogue of 90 paintings, chiefly in <pil. The
bidding was so poor that the sale was
after some thirty lots had been withdrawn or
sold for a ridiculous guinea or two.\ The
work deserved a better-reception. Up to a per-
tain point it was uncommonly good. Power paints
with sympathy, and his studies of cows and
horses are especially clever; in pure land-
scape he does not reach the same value. A
genuine Australian sentiment inspires his work,
though the touch is usually modern French. But
there is no market for pictures in Sydney at
present, and the better the work the less the
chance of sale.

THE POPR, AND KELLY
!IHIS is purely a personal expression of
opinion regarding the Pope, and re-
- garding Kelly. In the matter of reli-
gion, I am not concerned with the
oe : I am not concerned with Kelly,
ery man has a right to his own re-
qh • ©very religion has a right to its
li man. Identity of religious beliefs is not
ential to good citizenship. If Kelly will
der to Cresar the things that are Ciesar’s,
•ill render to the Pope the things that are
Pope’s. That is partly scripture, and
•tly common sense.
Jut what is the Pope’s ? Is Australia the
pe’s? In effect, Kelly says so. Kelly
s a delegate to the recent Labour Confer-
■e in Sydney. He opposed Socialism not
the ground that it was theoretically un-
ind, not on 'the ground that it was lus-
ically indefensible, not on the ground
t it was practically impossible, but on the
mnd that the Pope had sent round a letter
linst it. He listened to Home, and
dined to listen to reason.
But Socialism is a plan of civil govern-
jnt. It is not a plan of ecclesiastical
vernment. It has nothing to do with
wen or hell, or ways and means of getting
3 ve. It is not a matter of religious belief,
does not deny liberty of conscience. Con-
piently it is Ccesar’s affair, not the Pope’s.
As Collectivism, I oppose Socialism too.
it I oppose it because I do not think it
11 pay Australia to adopt it. I believe the
ate can make just laws, and reform unjust
es. It can open and keep open to all the
enues of industry. It can protect the
;ak against the strong. It cannot make all
ual, but it can give to all equal opportuni-
>s. It can place the poor man’s child and
e rich man’s child on the same footing of
.tural ability. It can tax the inheritances
the wealthy for the benefit of the labourers
r whose efforts they are partly amassed. It
n prevent by legislation the evils arising
am the monopoly of laud. It can experi-
entally aid co-operative production. But
cannot ensure human happiness while
iman egoism remains a constant factor,
he world can only be bettered by better men.
My opposition to Collectivism may be
rong or’ right, but it is reasonable,
oily's opposition is unreasonable. In his
rblished statement, he says : “I humbly
;cept his [the Pope’s] pronouncement
gainst Socialism as an article of faith. ...
Whatever views I had on that fascinating
lbject anterior to the publication of the
ncyclical on labour and socialism,
ren I have been against socialism.” In
dier words, Kelly has abandoned his right
t private judgment on a civil question at
re bidding of the head of his religion. -On
civil question, Kelly refuses to think for
iinself: Ire permits the Pope to do his
kinking.
Clearly, Kelly is a bad citizen.
Why ? Because, in the matter of the
overnment of Australia!, a matter which is
ot a religious matter, lie does not put
Liistralia first. He puts the Pope first,
’hat is bad citizenship.
Kelly is a bad citizen because, in the
ratter of the- temporal government of his
ountry, he pays allegiance to a foreign
.ower. He accepts the commands of a-foreign
uthority as to how he shall or shall nofaper-
orm his duty of Australian, citizenship.
Kelly is a bad citizen, because he accepts
he principle of religious interference in civil
;overnment.. Experience proves that free-
Lonr of conscience and equality of citizenship
mder the law are incompatible with religious
nterference in civil government. Witness
Italy. ...
Kelly is a bad citizen because he dehb-
irately puts his mind in chains- Chained
ninds shrivel to the limits of their chains.
Almost inevitably the denial of civil liberty
follows the acceptance of religious tyranny,
rhe man who casts his vote as “an act ot
faithis presently found incapable of casting
his vote as an act of reason. Yet tne
principle of the government of Australia,
the principle of self-government by equal
citizens for the common benefit, depends
necessarily upon the exercise of
Democratic government by “ anaetot tan
in the Pope is unthinkable.
For" these reasons and others Kelly is a
bad citizen. Yet men of his creed have
been good citizens, men of his race have
been among the best citizens Australia ever
had. They have not found their civil duty
to Australia incompatible with their
duty to the **£**«£* &S 5
mouth of Father O I lynn, askeu . V.
the clargy be Irishmen too . An P
Kelly, Cannot the laity be Australians too-

Various Verses.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
My Loves.
Her eyes were brown as a deep rock pool,
Where golden sunbeams play ;
I dreamt I had found my love at last,
But I kissed her, and sailed away.
Her eyes were blue as the sky at noon,
She tried to say me nay;
I held her close and swore my vows;
I loved, but I rode away.
O, deep were her eyes as a moonless night,
She spurned my heart as clay;
But they haunt ine still, those sad, wet eyes.
When I lied, and came away.
O, wide were her wistful eyes in pain
(’Twas ’twixt the dawn and the day);
She thought my love a wondrous thing ;
I vowed, but I stole away.
O, grey were her eyes, and strangely sweet.
Humbled, I knelt to pray;
For love, my love, had bloomed at last!
But she smiled and went away.
N.s.w.
CARO.
Mariana.
Mariana ! thou hast seen me, standing in the
garden,
While a circling wheel of pigeons flew, at pleasure,
round the sun,
And the virgin daisy, like a tiny star of pardon,
Hung enjewelled in a cobweb which the loom of
Mab had spun.
Thou hast seen me, Mariana, at the fountain
linger,
Where the overbending loquat turned the bubllea
into gold ;
And the lily sent a slender silver-frosted finger
Thro’ her secret little window with a million leaves
enscr oiled.
Mariana! thou hast seen me laughing in the
orchard
While I freed the sparrow, fainting, on the twig
besmeared with lime,
And the shadow of the dial in the ancient court-
yard V .
Marked the happy, happy hour to the tall clock-
! tower's chime.
Thou hast seen me, Mariana, on the terrace
weeping,
As the stars, born in the sunlight, rose like vestals
to the moon ;
Arid the tiny Jack-o’-l m thorn showed the kraken
sleeping
’Mid the scarlet tufted rushes of the wonderful
lagoon.
Mariana! O, believe me, all my mirth and madness
Found their birthdays in the image of some fantasy
of thee.
Mariana ! tell me : Will there be as much of glad-
ness
In the lovely love thy lovely hands do
love of me ?
N.s.w.
HUGH MOCRAK.
Number Three.
This is a song of a grey-eyed maid
And an engine black and new—
They make life glide on an easy grade
And all the world seems true.
The girl lives out by the railway line
Where the Baldwin engine goes;
And, whoever may doubt that girl is mine,
That Baldwin engine knows.
I tell my friends I am studying
The style of the Yankee “ tank.”
They answer, “ Yes, she’s a dainty thing,”
And call me an engine-crank,
Saying, “Beware, she is luring you—
Great is her witchery.”
I wonder if they’re alluding to
The girl or to Number Three.
I take her out in the moonlight sweet—
The girl, I mean—and we
Plan pleasant ways till we hear the beat,
Afar, of the engine “3.”
Then I say good-night and she says good-bye—
I pause and ask what she said ;
“Nothing,” she answers, “you’ll have to fly,”
But I turn back instead.
And my heart flares up in a spurt of sparks
That would stagger a fire-brigade,
While Number Three makes coarse remarks
As she comes up the heavy grade.
I dream I’m adrift near a love-lit sea
On a river with grey-eyed banks,
Till there comes a clarion call for me
(The voice this time is the “tank’s”).
Then a voice, so sweet that it seems to flow
Like silver and rippling pearls,
Whispers to me that I must not go
(This time the voice is the girl’s).
I must not go and I must not stay—
Was ever man placed like me ?
Loth to leave those eyes of grey
And deafened by Number Three ?
Then I tear away from that girl of mine
(I’ve taken out patent rights),
And I hurl my feet for the railway line
And the twinkling station lights.
I never trained for a hundred yards,
And I’d much prefer to sit.
But if ever my name is on the cards,
You put your boots on it.
’Tis but a tale of a little maid
And a big, black Baldwin “ tank ” ;
They make life move on an easy grade,
Like a swinging engine-crank.
The girl is “one,” and I am “two,”
The engine is number “three”—
That three is a crowd isn’t always true—
With us ’tis a trinity.
M.L.
WILL LAWSON.

MEDICATOR.
Catarrh is inflammation of
the lining membrane of the
nose ami adjoining passages.
If this inflammation is not
arrested it invades the pas-
sages which lead from the
nose to the head, ears, throat
and lungs. It injures the
i sight and hearing, destroys
| ihe sense of taste and smell,
renders the breath offensive,
breaks down the affected tis-
sues, consumes the nasal car-
tileges, and rots away the
small frontal bones of the
skull. The discharge,passing
through the lungs and sto-
mach, causes dyspepsia, also
consumption. Do you want
relief and cure? If so, try
our great remedy.

The Bulletin
The National Australian Newspaper.
" Australia for the Australians.”
Printed and Published by William Macleod, of
Botany-street, Waverley, for The Bulletin
Newspaper Co., Ltd., at the Office of the Co.,
214 George-street North, Sydney, N.S. Wales.
PUBLISHED ONCE A WEEK.
TERMS TO SUBSCRIBERS'.
(In Advance, Only)
Per Annum ... ... ... £1 6 0
Half-yearly ... ... ... OIS 6
Quarterly ... ... ... 0 7 0
NOTICE.
By sending direct to The Bulletin Office Twenty-Six
Shillings sterling you can have The Bulletin posted
regularly to you every week for a year.
Cheques, Drafts, etc., and all Business Communications
to he addressed to William Macleod, Managing Director.
Literary communications to be addressed to the Editor.
Sow to A ddress Letters.
Correspondents to The Bulletin would oblige the paper
(and, to an extent, themselves ) by taking some care as to
methods of address.
Business letters (with regard to subscriptions, advertise-
ments, etc.) should in no case be addressed to the Editor,
but to the Manager. To send them to the Editor is to
invite inattention.
It is not a good thing to address editorial letters to
anyone by name. “ The Editor, Bulletin,” is the proper
superscription for “ copy." Delay, and even loss, is
sometimes caused by addressing to persons instead of
officials. The person may not be there; the official
always is.
Special Notice.
The contents of this paper are copyright by the pub-
lishers. The right to reproduce or translate any part of
the contents of this number is hereby expressly reserved by
the publishers.
The Editor will carefully read and, where practicable,
acknowledge in the “ Correspondence ” column all contri-
butions submitted, whether in the form of Political,
Social, or other Articles, Verse, Short Tales or Sketches
(those dealing, with Australian subjects, and not exceeding
two columns in length, or, say, 3000 words, are specially
acceptable), Paragraphs, Letters, or Newspaper Clippings.
All communications will be regarded as strictly confi-
dential.
The Bulletin will return all unsuitable MS. when the
address is specified and stamps are enclosed But no
MS. will be returned, or preserved, unless these conditions
are observed. When MS. is forwarded without stamps
for return it will be understood that the copy is to be
destroyed if not acceptable. Personal applications for
return of MS. cannot receive attention. The Bulletin
desires, further, those sending MS. to distinctly under-
stand that it is not responsible for the preservation or
transmission of MS.
To Amateurs in 111 itch and White.
Anyone supplying to The Bulletin original humorous
or other topical drawings in line or ivash, of sufficient
merit and interest and adapted for direct reproduction, or
any interesting photograph illustrative of a current event,
will be paid for same on acceptance. Any picture con-
taining a good idea, but unfitted for d irect reproduction
— i.e., any picture which has to be re-drawn —will be paid
for as a suggestion only. The conditions under which
such artistic contributions will be received are those
applying to MS.

THURSDAY, MARCH 2, 1905.
PUBLIC OPINION, LTD.
Journalism comes first to be a party weapon and then a
commercial speculation, carried on without conscience or
scruple, like other commercial speculations. ... A news-
paper is not supposed to enlighten its readers, but to
supply them with congenial opinions. . . . Napoleon’s
sublime aphorism, suggested by his study of the Conven-
tion, “No one individual is responsible for a crime com-
mitted collectively,” sums up the whole significance of a
phenomenon, moral or immoral, whichever you please.
However shamefully a newspaper may behave, the disgrace
attaches to no one person. . . . We shall see newspapers,
started in the first instance by men of honor, falling
sooner or later into the hands of men of abilities even
lower than the average, but endowed with the resistance
and flexibility of indiarubber, qualities denied to noble
genius; nay, perhaps the future- newspaper proprietor
will be the tradesman with the capital sufficient to buy
venal pens.— Balzac (prophetically ) in “On Grand
Homme de Province a, Paris.”
Expression of public opinion in England has
been strong enough to force Mr. Harmsworth,
the man who is ten daily “ we’s” and a score or so
of other monthly and weekly “we’s,” to publish
behind the shield of his chief “ we ” —the Daily
Mail— some attempt at justification of the News-
paper Trust. The man of many “ we’s ” argues :
Shallow-brained people with short memories and little
knowledge of the past history of journalism, are amusing
themselves a great deal at the present moment by the
suggestion that it is possible for some one individual to
gain a control of the greater portion of the press of the
United Kingdom.
The suggestion is not at all novel. In 1885 an immense
amount of alarmist gossip was directed at Mr. Andrew
Carnegie, who, with Mr. Samuel Storey, was then
absorbing a good many newspapers, and who did indeed
make quite a respectable little “combine.” Mr. Car-
negie has long ago had quite enough of newspapers, and
Mr. Storey, with all his great ability, is only one of
hundreds of newspaper owners.
As a matter of fact, the consummation of a newspaper
trust is as impossible as undesirable.
The rumors printed elsewhere are as baseless as mis-
chievous. Man;, of them emanate from manufacturers of
printing machinery, desirous of securing orders ; a good
many from writers of the ridiculous “ London Letters ”
which appear in some of the provincial journals, and
others from ambitious journalists, who wish to stir up
competition among newspaper owners. If people would
take the trouble to study the past of their own profession
instead of rehashing antiquated theories about it, much
anxiety and ink would be spared.
In effect: Mr. Harmsworth and his allied capi-
talists have not bought up all the newspapers of
Britain; therefore the talk of a Press Trust is
foolish. That is a very clumsy evasion of the
issue, and if Mr. HarmsworTh, with his many
“we’s,” never argued better than that, the danger
would be lessened to the public of one group of
capitalists controlling a large number of influential
papers, covering a wide area, and pursuing the
same object, whilst masking a common aim by the
pretence of difference of opinion. The fact
remains undenied, and without effort at palliation
of its essential political dishonesty, that Mr.
Hakmsworth controls several newspapers, most
of them professing to have different policies, but
all necessarily united in obedience to his banking
account. True, a complete Press Combine has not
yet materialised. The day has not come when
all the important newspapers are owned by the
one syndicate, and when a united press, at the
whisper of a little group of capitalists can, with-
out fear of check, swing the publio mind to or
from war, outrage, or folly. But a long step
has been taken in that direction.
* * * *
Balzao, writing half a century ago, foresaw the
time when “the newspaper proprietor will be the
tradesman with capital sufficient to buy venal
pens.” That time has come long since. And at
first in spite of its strictly commercial basis the
tradesman newspaper was reasonably honesty The
average man who put his capital into a printing
press had some sympathy with letters, and
put with his capital nis individual pride and
decency. The public-company newspaper de-
stroyed that, and paved the way for the news-
paper as a purely commercial and money-making
institution. Few men who invest in the stock of
a public company consider that they have em-
barked their honor with their allotment fee; the
clergyman will without a qualm take dividends
from a company which draws an income from the
sins of the slums; the man who would blush to
sweat his individual employee cannot see it as his
duty to concern himself as to the wages paid by
the ’bus co. or the coal-mining co. in which he has
invested a few pounds. So the journal owned
and controlled by a joint-stock co. loses the
restraint of individual ownership. It may by
chance, and for a time, be blessed with
directors who take a proper view of their respon-
sibilities, not merely as dividend-seekers, but as
leaders of public opinion. But the chance is a
poor one. A a rule the directors will study only
dividends, the dividends from the paper and the
dividends from their other enterprises which the
paper can assist. The joint-stock newspaper is, in
short, owned by the “tradesman with capital suffi-
cient to buy venal pens,” and without even the
check of having his name up over the door. He
mav sell light-weight news, adulterated news, or
plain lies, may withhold what should go
to the public, and not have to face even the
ignominy of personal association with his misdeeds.
It becomes simply matter of what pays best. And,
irresistibly, the joint-stock newspaper moves to
the next step —the combination of syndicates into
a Press Trust. There is no reason why half-a-dozen
newspaper companies, having but one real object
—the gathering in of money—should not combine
forces to reduce the costs of production, to enhance
the value of their product, and to agree “to
tell the same yarn” when their mutual inte-
rests are affected. Capitalism knows no
civic jealousies. It is cosmopolitan, almost
international. Whatever its city, its religion and
its patriotism is the Dividend. Public opinion
has not, as yet, been “ cornered ” as completely
as kerosene oil, or tobacco, or steel. But such a
corner is only a matter of time. Mr. Harms-
worth’s ten “ we’s ” point irresistibly to that end.
* ft ft *
Australia, in particular, is ripe for such a combi-
nation. At one time the “great daily newspapers ”
kept their individualities fairly distinct. But that
day is past. The Sydney Morning Herald once
despised the Daily Telegraph as a poor upstart.
It is related that when it was proposed by the Tele-
graph that the two morning papers should agree
together to drop an issue on Christmas Day and
Good Friday, the Herald haughtily replied that
it was its custom to manage its own affairs without
outside interference. Now the two papers are so
friendly that they can stand shoulder to shoulder
in begging free postage and rail-carriage subsidies,
and in taking precautions to crush out the possi-
bilities of competition. The Age and the Argus
at one time were as really hostile as they now pre-
tend to be. At present they are quite friendly
enough to manage together a cable syndicate, to
agree as to days of non-publication, and to
decide mutually what days the public is to
have more paper than usual for its money.
All the Australian dailies, big and little. Protec-
tionist and Freetrade, Tory or pseudo-Liberal,
are already in brotherly agreement in the
cable syndicate, pledged by the holy bond of divi-
dends to tell the same story of all happeningsoutside
of Australia, and not to add to it m any way by
extra cables. It is not difficult to imagine, if the
masterly hand of a big capitalist came upon the
scene, Public Opinion, Ltd.—a syndicate con-
trolling all the daily newspapers—becoming a
reality in Australia within a decade. Such a
Trust could save much money by eliminating
expensive competition, and it could make much
money since its control of public policy would be
so complete that no tobacco trust, steel trust, or
sugar trust could afford to be “bad friends.”
* * * *
“ Public Opinion, Ltd.,” will come, must come.
The logic of events decrees it. Given a demo-
cratic Government which is also largely a State
Socialistic Government, and Public Opinion be-
comes of great monetary value. It can, working
through Parliament, build a railroad, buy up an
unprofitable estate, allow a monopoly, divert
taxation to subsidies and bonuses. Its exploitable
value is hardly calculable. Given, then, daily
newspapers, which—since they are the sole source
of supply of news, which is a necessity of civilisa-
tion—are able to dictate public opinion and which,
because of the expense of their production are in the
hands either of syndicates of capitalists or of joint-
stock companies, and it is a natural development
that the capitalists owning the newspapers will
combine to exploit the rich field which the
moulding of Public Opinion to suit their views
offers. There is great profit in such a combina-
tion and practically no risk, since no poor man or
no possible association of poor men could threaten
competition. By no other means could Capitalism
plunge its hands into the pockets of the people
with such rich promise of profits, as by using the
power of the press to levy toll upon the
vast sums which are raised by Parliaments, or
in raising which legislation can assist. Is
Capitalism going to neglect the opportunity 1 Such
neglect would be foreign to its whole nature.
* * ft *
There has been, of late years, a conspicuous
example of the power of Capitalism, through its
control of the press to mould public opinion. The
S’African plutocracy, owning the diamond and
gold mines, was eager to destroy the Boer repub-
lics, which stood in the way of its obtaining cheap
labor and full political control of the exploited
territory. At first open violence was tried, though
even then a secondary thought was given to the
press, and the evidence before the Raid Commis-
sion permitted a glimpse of the methods employed
by Rhodes and his fellow-conspirators to influence
the Times and other papers. The Raid having
failed, the Rand plutocracy relied on the press
to effect a surer way. Gaining control of every
S’African paper, except one or two Boer organs of
small circulation, the plutocracy opened a cam-
paign of vilification against the Boers. Public
opinion in S’Africa declared unanimously through
half a score of “we’s” (just Mr. Harmsworth’s
number) that the Boer must be put down —the
tyrannous, dirty, cruel, and savage Boer. Further,
public opinion stated, with equal emphasis, that
the task would be an easy one, that England could
do the job with a “little war” —a battalion or
two, a picnic, with an occasional skirmish as an
appetiser, and then—Victory and Pretoria.
England listened (heard too, public opinion—
prompted by the same gang of rogues—in her own
country) and embarked on the war. The unhappy
event still bleeds freshly in the public mind. The
Boer nation almost exterminated, 20,000 British
lives lost, £220,000,000 in treasure wasted—to give
a greedy plutocracy, mostly of foreign origin,
cheap workers and freedom from Boer labor legis-
lation. It was the crime not so much of the
British nation as of the press combination which
deceived the British nation. Such crimes may be
at any time prompted, and may be committed
again and again. If half-a-dozen London news-
paper capitalists came to an agreement to-morrow
they could, probably, within a year, bring Eng-
land to war with France or Germany or Russia, if
such served their interests. If the Australian daily
newspapers found it worth their while to do so,
they could probably destroy the White Australia
policy within a couple of years. There is hardly
a limit to the power of a syndicate controlling
many newspapers and urging the same thing
with voices apparently conflicting, but secretly in
agreement. A democracy exposed to such a com-
bination is as an army ambushed on all sides by
masked batteries. Public Opinion, Ltd., could do
practically anything it found it profitable to do.
* • * *
The evil is more apparent than the remedy.
But when it is generally recognised how imminent
and how great is the danger, some remedy will be
sought, however desperate. Democracy having
achieved so much in council hall and on battle-
field, will not allow itself to be quietly
strangled in its adult age. Perhaps relief will be
sought in the Socialisation of the news industry.
The newspaper is a necessity in so far as it sup-
plies news, a luxury only in so far as it supplies
comments and opinions. Civilised man must
have news supplied to him, and that news can only
be gathered by a vastly expensive and complicated
organisation. Opinions he can make for himself.
If the State took upon itself the task of supplying
news—as it now does to a small extent in every
civilised country, and as it used to do to a very
large extent in previous epochs—the problem of
the press would be solved, and the danger to
free Government be averted. The “liberty of the
press” would not need to be interfered with.
Men and associations of men could still print
what they liked, and the citizen wishing to buy
ready-made opinion could still patronise his
Daily Jove or his Evening Satyr. But he
would have available to him, in a pure, unadul-
terated form, with no hint of comment or opinion,
the actual news of the day, in the State news-
paper. If then, from deliberate choice, he chose
to be misled, that would be his own affair, and a
misfortune which the State could hardly guard
him against. Such a remedy seems at first
thought too extreme : certaiidy it runs counter
to many traditions and dearly held ideas. But
the dilemma is inescapable. Modern democracy
turns every day more to a conviction that in the
Eublic interest all monopolies must be controlled
y the State. The proprietors of daily news-
papers are quickly moving towards a monopoly.
When that comes the issue will be the destruction
of democracy or the State newspaper.
THE MAN IN LONDON.
Dear Bulletin.— B. R. Wise’s presence in Lon-
don just now ought to make it easy for the
Commonwealth Government to pick a High Com-
missioner for all the Australias when the proper
time comes. True, he is under a ban ; for has he
not committed the unpardonable sin—he has
disobeyed the daily morning press ; and, treating
their riding orders with contempt, has ridden his
own race ? No man was ever a greater pet with
the morning dailies, once upon a time. The
Herald looked upon him as the “rising hope ” of
a perishing fiscal doctrine. The Telegraph,
newly converted, in those days, to the N.S.W.
fiscal orthodoxy, after a futile Protectionist career,
cherished him in its bosom. At that time, while
admitting Wise’s ability, I hated him for having
thrown his brilliant powers into the scale of a
policy which (I thought) meant an adjournment of
the Australian national ideal for generations. We
were younger then; and some of us condemned
Wise for advocating a fiscal policy which estranged
our native State from the other States, and set up
difficulties in the way of Union—Union, which we
thought was the nearest road to—well, to other
things, which seem as far off now as Federation ap-
peared then. We thought, and said, that Wise could
not be sincere, but was moving along the political
curve of least resistance—a cunning young man,
who saw clearly on which side his political bread
was buttered, who did not care to fight a losing or
an uncertain cause, but chose deliberately to take
a commission with the winning Freetrade-provin-
cial side, knowing that he was advocating an
economic fallacy. We think now that we were
mistaken. We forgot to take into consideration
the Ox ord equation in our estimate of Wise. He
was taken young into a school where the
CoBDEN-ite fallacy was regarded as economic
orthodoxy, and all other theories were treated as
blasphemy, to he condemned with the Oxford
equivalent for bell, book and candle. Protec-
tion was regarded as unarguable, nay wicked;
and the mental attitude of Oxford towards those
rising industrial portents, the U.S.A., and Ger-
many after 1878, was that of tearful prayer. Wise
was the victim of his intellectual environment.
We suspected him, too, of inconstancy to many
causes. We thought him emotional. He was a
dabbler (let us look at the worst side of him !) —
he was a dilettante. He flirted with single-tax ;
he had emotional moments towards Socialism;
but he always drew back in time. He once said
publicly that he didn’t know an oil painting from
a water-color, and a week later he became a trus-
tee of the National Gallery. He took the
Attorney-Generalship from Parkes, and as sud-
denly abandoned it. He wrote a book of Herbert
SpENCER-ian quasi-economics—full of sentimental
sophistry. In short, until we came to close grips
on the Commonwealth question, Wise was one of
the ablest and easily the most unsatisfactory of
native-born Australians.
» * * *
His regeneration may be dated from 1895. A
year before that he acted as sponsor for the Reid
Government in N.S.W., though he refused to join
it, owing to a chivalrous attachment to the old
warrior, Pakkes, who had been jockeyed out of
leadership and office by Reid, assisted by a Scotch
Governor and the Daily Telegraph. He finessed
when the Parkes-Dibbs agreement to oust Reid
was entered upon ; but later on he defended his
old chief with admirable fidelity. He was just
beginning to atone for a misspent political career ;
and his faithfulness to Parkes, whose star was
just dipping the horizon, and to a lost cause, was a
hopeful symptom that he had not altogether sunk
to the level of roads-and-bridgedom. His reward
was the honor of being conjoined with Barton,
Parkes and Dibbs in exclusion from publio life,
while Lyne, Jessep and Storey (the hatter) flour-
ished over them, and the press rejoiced. The next
act of the drama was the Federal fight. Just as
Wise’s star appeared to have gone out, he re-ap-
peared as the tenth delegate to that series of
conventions which devised the freest system of
Constitutional Government that exists in the
world to-day. In those conventions he stood in
the first flight. He had found himself. His dilet-
tantism was put to flight. A European journa-
list of great experience who was present at the
first convention told me that Barton, Deakin,
Wise, O’Connor, and Reid were equal in debating
calibre to the best in a House of Commons which
then contained Harcourt, Morley, Asquith,
Chamberlain, Balfour, Redmond, Lloyd Jones,
John Burns and Charles Dilke. So far as to
ability. But as to fibre. In those conventions the
cracked veneer of an Oxford education rubbed off;
and Wise the Australian stood revealed. How
much that Oxford veneer cost Wise ! Rhodes,
the arch-imperialist, knew what he was about
when he devised his scheme of scholarships, which
takes the best of provincial youth from the outer
rim of the Empire, stifles its originality, fits it out
with an Oxford accent, an overpowering idea of
Oxford superiority and of the universal outside-
Oxford inferiority, and perverts it with a distaste
for the ways, the thoughts and the aspirations of
its mother province ! In the convulsion of that
great Federal fight Wise threw off the prejudices
of education and youthful environment, became
a primitive Australian patriot, naked and un-
ashamed, and stood by Barton as his right-hand
man against the crusted Provincialism and “cab-
bage-tree hat ” ideas of the mother State. When
he had to make a choice between attachment to his
CoBDEN-ite superstitions and the hope of Aus-
tralian nationality, between the dead theories of
the past and the bright and alluring prospects of
the future, Wise chose the latter. Moreover,
he went Nap. For this the N.S.W. morning
press has never forgiven him. He saw the dangers
of hostile tariffs between friendly States on an
island continent, and he manfully exposed them.
He led the thousands of whole-souled Freetrade
electors who put Australian nationality first and
fiscalism nowhere; who were content to surrender
their chances of external freetrade for the advan-
tages of union, and its corollary, intercolonial
reciprocity. They yielded the right to erect
barriers against the world, in return for the
advantages of tearing down all trade barriers
between the people of one nation. They voted
to transfer custom-houses, and all the petty in-
conveniences thereof, from inland border towns
to seaports, so that through the length and breadth
of a vast continent we should have “ that repose
and peace which,” as Sergeant Buzfuz informs us,
“ a custom house can never afford.”
* * * *
Reid, guilty of unparalleled political vacillation
and poltroonery at this crucial stage of the nation’s
development, whose conduct had even made the
gorge of his own press rise against him—saw only
one way of regaining favor, viz., by raising the fiscal
ghost, which had been laid aside in the interests
of Australian nationality. The press came round
to him, the Freetrade party whip was cracked,
and the large and small dogs ran to heel. Wise
stuck to Barton, when men so reputedly strong
and independent as M ‘Millan and Bruce Smith
failed the Federal leader. Wise might easily
have come in out of the wet with those gentlemen,
who, no doubt, struggled long against the vortex
of the re-united Reid-Daily Telegraph crowd.
They succumbed eventually, and allied them-
selves to Reid against Barton and Wise—
M‘Millan, who had declared and proved that
Reid was the greatest financial charlatan N.S.W.
had ever seen, and had exposed his devious
methods of public finance; and Bruce Smith,
who had, from a high horse, threatened Reid
with exposure for attempting to secure a political
pledge from him as a condition precedent to
appointing him to the Upper House, and who was
beaten by a man whom he regarded as a nonentity,
owing to Reid’s influence, at the Glebe election,
when B. Smith stood as a Bartonite. In this
crisis, while others trimmed, B. R. Wise fought
with all the powers of a convinced mind to make
the Australian Commonwealth a going concern.
It is a going concern, though the same enemies,
who, under various names—“prudent Federalists,”
“Freetraders,” ‘ ‘anti-Victorians,”and what not! —
have tried to hamstring it, and are watching night
and day to give it a foul blow. One of their methods
of dealing a blow at the Commonwealth, and at the
advanced Democracy which it incarnates, is to
slander it before the nations; and, in order to
counteract these slanders, and defend this free,
selfireliant, progressive Commonwealth in the
face of the nations, we need a man who, whatever
his faults, was true to the Union from the start;
who made large sacrifices to advance its welfare
and guard its infant steps; who had a share, and
no small share, in giving it its shape; and who,
on all grounds of ability, brilliancy and compe-
tency with pen and tongue, is one of the most
capable of defending before the world the demo-
cratic ideals of its founders, and of explaining the
aspirations of the greatest democratic movement
(socialist, if you like) that has ever taken practical
and constitutional shape on earth.
* * * *
We want no Reids in London to acquiesce for
party advantages in detrimental stories which he
knows to be untrue ; we want no Forrests, who
frankly do not believe in Democracy. We want
no doubters in London. We want a believer
one who has faith in the Commonwealth; one
who knows that Australians will do right,
not perhaps, according to the ideals of
outsiders, but according to their own ; one
who believes in the destiny of Australia
and follows its star; not one who fears that, in
passing progressive legislation and trying social
experiments in an altruistic spirit, Australia is
committing a solecism, is offending against some
imaginary etiquette, or is running down a steep
place into the sea. We want a man "'ho will
show that we are not all national tuft-hunters,
title-seekers and toadies; who will do for Aus-
tralia what Benjamin Franklin did in Jraris for
the United States in its infancy ; and, in a smaller
sphere, what Reeves is doing for Maoriland,
explaining, expounding, defending, extolling,
praising, and, if you will —proudly boasting. We
want no propitiation of English opinion ;we want
our own view-point put before the nations. 1
know of no one more competent to do this than
B. R. Wise.— Yours, etc.,
JACQUES.

was the whole point of the argument, and the
point which G. Bron evades. A king of to-day
does nothing kingly; he doesn’t even believe in

paint a king ? Wnat Rubens, Vandyck and Hol-

The Frills of the Fighting Man.
Lord Roberts, ex-Commander-in-Chief of the
British Army, contributes a sensible article to the
Nineteenth Century on army matters, of which
the vital conclusion is this :
I maintain that it is the bounden duty of the State to
see that every able-bodied man in this country, no matter
to what grade of society he may belong, undergoes some
kind of military training- in his youth, sufficient to enable
him to shoot straight, and carry out simple orders if ever
his services are required for the national defence.
That, of course, is written to apply to England.
But there it is hardly likely to win acceptance.
The logical corollary of universal service in a
democratic country is the abolition of class dis-
tinctions in the army. If in England every voter
were a the system by which the army is
the playground of the wealthy young bloods
would have to fall ; and that system is
an essential of present British social life. The
British soldier is, as to the ranker, a sort of
helot, who must not appear in uniform in respect-
able public-houses, but can use his uniform as a
bait to wheedle nurse-girls into supplying him
with beer-money (there is a fixed tariff paid by
the London servant-girls for being allowed the
honor of walking with a gaudy soldier in
public). The British soldier is, as to the
officer, mainly the gilded youth filling in time
by being in the service, or a snob anxious
to court society from the vantage ground
of a fashionable regiment. That, withal, the
British soldier (and even the British officer) is
pretty good stuff when the firing-line crackles for
the fight, only shows that a good brand of
white men can show good in spite of a deal of
discouragement. It would take something of a
social revolution to sweep away this system and
give to Britain a citizen force enrolled by con-
scription. But in Australia—to which country
Lord Roberts’ conclusion is even more applicable
—no such great change would bo needed. There
is already a leaven of the democratic spirit
in the forces of the Commonwealth, and a universal
service scheme would have to fight no very strong
vested social interests. The only obstacle here
would be the tired disinclination of many to fulfil
their natural citizen-duties and to train to guard the
country which, one day, they will be only able to
hold by stern defence. And that disinclination
calls for no consideration.

State-made Pariahs.
The women of Goulburn (N.S.W.) and other parts
who want more lash for certain offences should
consider the case of Ellis, lately sentenced to
death in M.L. for the Collinson murder. Years
ago, Ellis was convicted of a sexual crime, the
victim being a little girl, and for this he was
sentenced to life imprisonment and two floggings
of 25 lashes each. The floggings were adminis-
tered, and, when 12 years of the gaoling had been
served, the unfortunate social misfit was released,
only to be shadowed by detectives and shunned
by all but the most depraved. His life, even in
the backblocks, was ‘ ‘hell on earth” ; every petty
squabble with a drunken shearer or rouseabout
ended with some taunt about the scars on
his back. Then, for shooting a sheep, Ellis
was ordered off Te Awaiti station, and the
‘hands” were given strict instructions not to
harbor him. Blaming Collin.son for “ peaching ”
on him, this hunted pariah, denied the right to
cut a bit of scrub for his bread, and refused per-
mission set his foot on the 65,000 acres of free*
hold in Te Awaiti station, did nothing very sur-
prising when the circumstances and the obvious
nature of the man are considered—he killed
Collinson. Morally the State has connived
(unwittingly, of course) at the murder for which he
awaits the gallows. Why did it release the man ?
If he deserved release, he also deserved a chance
to make good use of his freedom. What chance
did it afford him when it turned him ad-sift,- his
back scored wi th marks of brutalising punishment,
without a shilling in his pocket, his footsteps
shadowed,” and refused the right to work among
his fellows ?

The Little Australian.
No sort of immigrant that a country can get is,
in. general, likely to be as valuable as its own
child, born within its own bounds, and with no
other country to share his affections. Hence the
enormous importance of satisfactory birth and death
rates. And, since it is practically only the little
Australian who dies in Australia in his infancy,
the infant-mortality rate calls for a very special
watch. Mostly the figures in this connection are ex-
tremely erratic; one year measles are bad, and even
in the best districts the littie Australian dies in
frightful numbers. Thus, in 1902, in tile north-
west suburbs of Sydney, there were 133 infantile
deaths (under one year) to every 1000 births; next
year there were only 100. But still, in spite of
these irregularities, certain averages are constant.
Again and again, attention has thus been drawn
to the awful disproportion of child-deaths in
certain portions of Sydney. Of every 1000 chil-
dren born, 40 more die in the citv than in the
northern suburbs, on the average of the
last 10 years. But while the murder of
the city infant is being gradually re-
duced, partly because the matter has been so
constantly kept under notice, the even greater
disproportion in the infant death-rate in country
districts has had little attention j and we have
been driving our hopeless railways out into the
west, in spite of the fact that on the average one
infant in every six born there dies before it has
reached its 13th month, while often the proportion
exceeds one infant in every five. On the North
Coast, on the South Coast, and on the Alonaro,
Australia loses its most desirable of all immigrants
the young Australian—at the average rate' of
less than 80 per 1000 in infancy ; in the West it
loses him at the rate of 165 per 1000. Considering
how badly Australia needs population, this loss ol
the best population it can possibly get is a matter
of such importance that it ought to be reckoned
an important factor in any scheme of land settle-
ment. We want to settle our people not only
where they can grow wheat, but also where they
can grow Australians.

The Churches and Socialism.
Britishers who inveigh against Socialism on the
grounds that it is antagonistic to Christianity
seem ignorant of the attempt made in England,
about 1860, by Maurice, Kingsley, Ludlow, and
Hughes, with other fervid Anglicans, to found a
Christian Socialist Barty, which would usher in
the millennium by means of voluntary co-operation
for production and distribution. The Australian
Roman Catholic anti-Socialists are apparently
even less aware of the attempts made in Europe
by their Church, to either capture or divide the
Socialist movement. In 1863, Dr. Dollinger,
seeing in the Catholic Clubs of Germany an
organisation ready to .his hand, recommended
these, presumably with the sanction of the Roman
Curia, to adopt a modified scheme of socialism
and to work strenuously for its attainment. His
efforts were seconded by Baron Von Ket-
telek, Bishop of Mayence, whose pamphlet
on Christianity and the Labor Question
togely anticipated the reasoning of the 1891
Encyclical. Ketteler denied that the Catholic
Church has ever maintained that there is an abso-
lute right of property : argued that no such claim
can avail against the pressing needs of the poor,
and that no man is more than a feudal vassal,
holding property under God, on the conditions He
imposes, one of these being that “anyone who
finds himself in extreme need, is justified, when
all other means fail, in satisfying his immediate
wants how and where he may.” It is interesting
to remember that this doctrine was advanced by
Cardinal Manning —a firm believer in State
intervention—some 14 years ago, when protes-
ting against the severe punishment of one who
stole bread to relieve his children’s hunger.
Ketteler proposed that the Roman Church
should guide the movement and provide the
initial capital through the subscriptions of the’
faithful. In 1869, a German conference of Roman
Catholic Bishops lent modified approval to the
scheme, and the Catholic Laborers’ Associa-
tions were formed. They owned a journal, styled
the Christliohe Sooiale Blatter, and their
principles, as laid down in its columns,
singularly resemble those of the Australian
Labor Parties. But they waned as the Social
Democrats waxed, and now have, under the style
of Christian Social, or St. Joseph, Associations,
resumed their status quo ante Dollinger. France
has two schools of Roman Catholic Socialists—
the voluntarists who follow Le Play, and those
who march behind Count Albert de Mun and
believe in State intervention. These are sup-
ported by the Catholic Socialists of Switzerland
and Belgium, and their annual congresses, at-
tended in 1890 by Lord Ashburnham and the
Bishops of Salford and Nottingham (Eng.), are
considerable. German Protestantism has perhaps
gone even further towards Socialism, if not,
indeed, the whole way in theory. Todt pub-
lished his German Socialism and Christian
Society, and had the support of Dr. Stocker,
a Court preacher. Todt adopted all the premises
of modern Socialism and most of its conclusions,
and argued that nothing but the atheism of
Continental Socialism prevented its realisation.
He quoted the New Testament to prove that
Communism harmonised with Christianity, re-
fused to advocate the abolition of private pro-
perty by force, and declared that the triumph
of Socialism must be postponed until
selfishness was swallowed up by love. The
Christian Social German Workingmen’s Party at
first included 700 pastors and owned a paper, the
Staats-Socialist, but the socialism of the cassock
could not prevail against that of the blouse, and
though Stocker, losing his Court pulpit, has
worked hard, the association has lately made little
or no headway against the attacks of the Social-
Democrats. No doubt the Australian Labor
Parties will have to meet from time to time
similar church attempts to conquer or divide them.

Yes, a Tozer!
Attorney-General Josiah Symon has taken the
trouble to show up thoroughly (in the Adelaide
Register) Mr. Horace Tozisr’s stinking fish tale
(m the Brisbane Courier) about his wife’s maid,
who came with her from London. Mr. Tozer’s
story was that the girl was only allowed to land
on his making a declaration that she was a tem-
porary visitor, and on his undertaking that she
should not run away and become a permanent
resident of the Commonwealth ; and now she
wants to get married, but “will have to leave
Australia at the end of the stipulated period. ’
Mr. Symon puts it delicately when he says that
Mr. lozer “should have been a writer of comic
opera.” He then affirms officially that: (1) a lady’s
maid does not come within thelmmigrationßestric-
Act; (2) the Brisbane officials took no action
whatever in the matter ; (3) Mr. Tozer, being
entitled, as Agent-General for Queensland, to
issue exemption certificates, gave the girl one for
six momma, thus himself stamping her as an other-
wise prohibited person. “ Upon my word,” says
Mr. Symon finally, “ I do not see why, even now,
in spite of that blundering exemption certificate
the marriage should not come off, and the happy
bride remain in Australia, unless Mr. Tozer, to
live up to his certificate, forbids the banns and
insists on packing her off home.”

Catering for Stupidity.
From Melbourne, in regard to the folly of some
people:—
, r 4, reeen t exhibition of Australian manufactures in
Melbourne led to some newspaper correspondence which
threw further light on the question of how to commend
Australian-made goods to a foolishly ant pathetic public.
I lie local firm of Robert Hurst, boot manufacturer and
ooofc shopkeeper, was accused of displaying' imported
boots as goods of local manufacture. To this charge the
hrin made an interesting reply—“ A few odd boots may
have been branded ‘ Made expressly for Robkrt Hurst
Melbourne,’ but we positively assert that these, as well
Hi the rest of the contents of the case, are of Australian
manufacture. We feel flattered that goods of our manu-
facture should have been mistaken for the best English
and American, seeing that they were ordinary lines taken
“‘oni stock, and were not specially made for show pur-
poses.” It is only an indirect deception that Hurst,
the manufacturer, practises upon some of the customers.
Public prejudice—largely British-born, but partly
repiesented by the Australian snobocracy—in favor of
imported goods compels Hurst to buy some of his
boots abroad and have them branded accordingly ;
also it accounts for the misleading orand on some
locally-made goods “Made expressly for R.H.
Melbourne.” The manufacturing-retailing firm makes
those boots expressly for its own shops, as a
matter of policy. Prejudice must be catered for as
long as it insists upon paying the price of its foolishness.
Every Australian firm in the
re tailing business finds the same difficulty in
forcing the merits of “ colonial ” goods upon the appre-
ciation of an unreasonable class. The manager for
Buckley and Nunn, Melbourne, talks instructively about
the local shirt trade, and how it is affected, to a slowly-
diminishing extent, by an ancient reverence for the
English brand. At the Buckley and Nuxx work-rooms
men’s shirts are made under far better labor'cond.tions
than exist in old-world factories, yet the higher wa»v
paid to the more educated and alert Australian girls
doesn’t prevent the local article from being better
value than the imported at all prices. Linen and
calico imported here pay only a 5 per cent, duty •
the sewing machine is the best in the world ■’
the workmanship is as near perfection as can be’
Nevertheless the demand for foreign-branded shirts is not
dead. Lnteachable persons ask for “ Welch Margetson ”
shirts at the shop counter in spite of the fact that they
could get a superior article for their outlay by encourag-
ing- an Australian manufacture in the work rooms up-
stairs. And so it is with more substantial and costly
things in body apparel than men’s shirts. The manufac-
turer, who is also a shopkeeper, has nothing to gain
financially by proclaiming the truth. He speaks in a
tone of patriotic cynicism. The public pay their money
and take their choice, and when their choice falls upon
the imported as against the local manufactured article it
is usually unwise. That’s all.
Effect and Cause.
Saul Joshua, of Port Melbourne, distiller, has
been giving evidence before the Tariff Commis-
sion. He said that his factory had been closed
down, and £lBB,OOO of capital lost through the
inadequate tariff. He implored the Commission
to examine his books in proof of his statements
that prior to the disastrous tariff above-mentioned
his firm had spent over £lOO a week for labor
direct; had spent £2OOO to £3OOO annually with
Australian bottle-makers; from £15,000 to £20,000
with Australian farmers ; and from £3OOO to £4OOO
annually for Australian coal, besides other sun-
dries. He further pointed out that the Australian
distilling industry could be protected by raising
the.customs duty to 15s. per gallon, and fixing the
excise duty at from 9s. to 135., and by this
the revenue too would be increased by £85,963.
The tariff as at present existing not only ruins the
Australian industry, but damages the Australian
revenue. There are work and wages for Aus-
tralian citizens and coin for the Australian
Treasury in bringing the tariff to a sensible stan-
dard. Why was not that done from the first?
To answer the question it is only necessary to
refer to any Australian daily paper of Nov. 15th,
“1902, or to The Bulletin of Nov. 29th, 1902,
where will be found the report of the proceedings
taken by C. Aloysius Fox against certain Mel-
bourne spirit-importing firms. This man Fox
swore that he was employed as an agent by the
spirit-importers to “lobby” the Federal Parlia-
ment to have the spirit duties so fixed as to crush
the local distilleries. For this he was to receive
50 guineas, and in case of success, a substantial
bonus as well. In all he got £llO, and was not
satisfied, for, as he represented to his employers—
The agitation has been successful beyond our best
hopes, so that distilleries are irretrievably crippled, and
everyone knows it. Surely results so patent, and affecting’
importing interests so favorably from one end of Australia
to another—interests represented by spirit imports of
close on £1,000,000 annually—will be considered as merit-
ing more than a beggarly pittance.
Saul Joshua’s sworn evidence before the Tariff
Commission is the second chapter of the interest-
ing industrial story, which began with 0. Aloysius
Fox’s sworn evidence before the Melbourne
County Court. One is the effect, the other the
cause.
** Talafekau ** writes some mixed treason from
M.L. :
Evidently our British self-complacency is now much as
it was in the beginning, and as, I imagine, it ever shall
be, for so long as the Bull strain shall endure. “The
Australian child is taught blasphemously to apotheosise
the English flag, country and King with his God” (8.,
2/2/’O5). Flashlike, that par. bridged for me more years
than X can stop to count, and I remembered sundry pro-
fane gratulations in the teaching of my own BuLL-pup
days. Here is a bit of one of our little hymns :
1 1 thank the Goodness and the Grace Which on my birth
have smiled ;
And made me in these Christian days a happy English
child.”
An indifferent Almighty might have made me a Russian
or a Prussian, or perhaps an Italian, or of some such
inferior and dubious brand of Christianity; but He
“smiled on my birth,” and made me of the Elect, a
memberof that specially-selected, hall-marked, safe-for-the-
Kingdom band which is composed of Britons, and of Britons
only. Great teaching this for the soaring British brat,
and small wonder that he develops into the swelling Bull
who brags about “My Earth,” or “My mission to
reform the earth,” which amounts to much the same
thing. We had a Catechism also in those Christian child-
days, which, after dwelling on “ the particular mercies ”
attendant on birth in “happy England,” went on to ask
why “ heathen children ” are so much less “ happy ”
than “English children,” the answer being that the
• heathen children “ are neglected and left in ignorance
and vice! ” Fancy the hide-bound insularity of the
Pecksniff who could pen this precious piffle with his
ears closed to the shrieks of the wretched, beaten,
starving child-slaves all around him ; with his eyes shut
on their ignorance, vice, dirt, and shivering ragged-
ness ; with his mind all innocent of any know-
ledge whatever of the lucky savage children who
‘ never know how to starve in the midst of plenty till
we show them the way.” Between the Christian
babe of the black country and the pagan babe of the sun-
lands, there is indeed a great gulf fixed, but I feel that it
is upon the savage side only that real happiness is to be
found—the heathen man wants for nothing till the Rev.
Oily Gammon comes in an eager pursuit of proselytes or
of pelf, with the curse of Ada.m fast upon his footsteps.
Then the contented heathen is “ converted ” into the dis-
contented Christian ; but he has to be chary of exhibiting
a discontent which is “ truculence ” when displayed by
natives blessed with the ministrations of the mission man.
So the converted one soon learns to assume contentment
e y?J\ as hot, and, like the Australian school
children, he is taught to apotheosise the English with
their God. And thus I leave him, singing his “ emanci-
pated ” Doxology :
Praise God who sent the English here ;
Bless them, their Bibles and their Beer;
Work for their gain with restless hands;
Give them our daughters and our lands.
When you glance at him introspectively, what a curious
mixture is J. Bull 1

Ex-Minister Capt. Miles, of Tasmania, has been
measuring the Monkey’s head, and finds it more
swollen than ever. He didn’t see any difference
in ns treatment of Englishmen and Australians.
Ihe Monkey has watched the Englishman crawl
to it so much that it is quite satisfied he ought
to go on crawling on his belly. Miles had a
characteristic experience at Tokio, where he had
to buy a silk hat before he was permitted to see a
sixth-rate commissariat officer on business in
business hours. His soft-felt wasn’t sufficiently
respectful to Class VI. Monkey in charge of the
salt fish and rice department. The Englishman
has to keep crawling now even to the rickshaw
coolie. Miles’ testimony:—
Up to some months ago the rickshaw boys in Japan
were very civil and obliging to all white people visiting
their country. Now, if you remonstrate with one of
them he becomes very insulting and offensive, and if you
rnn h? r ? t , ort f a J»P anese policeman is called up, and you
run the r.sk of being taken to gaol for offending a rfck
shaw man, whilst no notice is taken of the insolence of
that individual towards yourself.
Captain Miles went East with the usual Russo-
phobe ideas about the war; he comes back satisfied
that, if the Monkey wins, not only will Japan be
made intolerable for any white man, but that
Australia will have its turn next. But, then he
says, the Monkey won’t win, though the Bear’s
chances don t look rosy j ust now.

[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
For her ten thousand had voyaged forth,
Sailed in their ships from the lusty North.
Not with their swords had they fought for her,
But with pick and ploughshare and spade and spur.
Rich were the spoils their tools had won,
Her heritage good to look upon.
Four Seas flowed to her outer gates,
Four Seas girdled her wide estates.
Within the walls where the Four Seas rolled,
Were flocks and forests and mines of gold.
And when she had come to be woman grown,
“ Now,” they said, “you must move alone.
“ Heretofore thou hast not passed this way,
For counsel call on whom you may.”
She called for counsel; a sword blood-red
1 hey brought her. “ Thy lovers send forth, ” they
said.
And she who had thought to be a shield
Sent forth her lovers the sword to wield.
liie edge passed over her brow so fair,
And furrowed it deep with a line of care.
Yet as she mourned for her lovers dead,
She saw that others lacked for bread.
But her counsel answered : “Fair maid; ’tis well.
With ships of commerce thy harbors swell.
Their holds are filled from the farthest lands.”
“ And m y lovers,” she said, “ wring idle hands.”
“Thy lovers,” they counselled, “are fair of face ;
And others are waiting for thy embrace.
“True, thy fathers who won thee thy domains,
Kept pure the blood that fills thy veins.
“ ® ut fathers are dead, and thou art free.
Welcome thy wooers what race they be.
“ This yellow wooer he hath his worth—
Mark how with patience he tills the earth ;
Mark how he maketh the fruits to grow ;
The harvests thy garners shall overflow.”
“.Shall I murk the blood in my children’s veins
For bulging b irns and creaking wains ?
“Shall I mark my brood with the half-breed’s
curse
For the harlot’s price of a heavy purse ?
“Many things,” she said, “are to me unknown ;
l here are others that come to a woman grown.
“And blood as pure as my fathers gave
To me, from me shall my children have. ’
S.H.P.

■*
As a riot of color, “The Orchid,” staged
at Her Majesty’s for the first time in Sydney on
Saturday night, is as clamorous as “Florodora”
and “The Darling of the Gods.” The web of
plot that the librettist has woven is not much
tangled, the lyrics and orchestral numbers rise to
the occasion—but, then, the occasion itself does
not once knock the stars. Sometimes the songs
and the comedy sink to basement regions, but
that is just where the audience rocks most
wildly with laughter. Sometimes “ The Orchid ”
sprouts a petal of epigram. “ I thought you
liked my speech in the House. You said it
was like champagne,” says the silly Hon. Guy
Scrymgeour to his noble uncle. “So I did,” was
the reply ; “all froth and very dry.” Comedy
gets the better of the music, and with the fun the
audience was satisfied. There is a lot of child-
nature in a hairy, adiposey audience, and it yells
with laughter when someone shakes a rattle
at it, and tickles it with a straw, and thumps
the other fellow with a bladder. Scene-
Eainters and costumiers and ballet-mistresses
ave been given a free hand in the pro-
duction, and have proved their possession
of master hands (and feet) backed by artistic eyes.
The thin thread of the story winds round the
getting of a rare orchid from Peru, the subsequent
loss of the specimen, and its recapture. There is
an orchid maniac (Mr. Bantock) who is supposed
to burlesque Mr. Chamberlain. The piece opens
in the Countess of Barwick’s Horticultural College.
A comic gardener (Mr. Lauri), a comic lady
who wants to marry him (Miss Clifton), a
pupil at the College (Miss Evelyn Scott), a
teacher (Miss Alexia Bassian), and a huge
cast of other characters i lake up a brilliant
whole. Mr. Lauri, got-up in one phase after
the style of Mark Foy’s picture “The
Golfer,” has a great share in the fun-making.
Miss Alexia Bassian and Mr. Haigh Jackson
have not much singing to do, but they
mostly assist the comedians. Miss Clifton as
Caroline Vokins promised much in her topical
songs, and a minor promise was that “if anything
pinched her waist she’d loosen it with a spanner.”
Miss Evelyn Scott, who has a good acting and
singing part, gave very demurely the song,
“Little Mary,” a ditty that the Britishers know
as well as their prayers, so great is its vogue in
London. It is profoundly and rabbitly silly, but
no doubt Sydney will grow to like it when Sydney
gets down to its intellectual level. A new musical
director —Mr. Andrew MacCunn—waved his stick
in the orchestra.
** * *
Innovations at the Tivoli for this week are
the Mahers and the Reseda Sextette. The former,
Alice and Mike, are Irish specialty artists, with
new comic duets and four soles that beat as one
in lightning jig time. Mike bears a humorous
resemblance to a young local barrister of repute ;
Alice is a fair, fluffy shred of thistledown, wh®
has probably never seen aught of Ireland but its
map. They got a good reception. The Mignon-
ette Sextette (sounds prettier than “Reseda,”
and means the same) produced an Asiatic
melody, “She’s My Chrysanthemum,” with
catchy, whistlesome bars. If Sydney wants
cold shivers and hot thrills it may have ’em by
seeing “Cleopatra” and her snakes—pythons,
boa constrictors, anacondas, and the two croco-
diles. Eve herself wasn’t so versed in snake-
charming as Cleopatra, but Cleo. has something
to learn about crocs., for one of them nipped her
hand on Monday. “ Little Tich,” a noted come-
dian from London, makes a first appearance this
week.
