About the new singers in Australia :
Undoubtedly, for all-round excellence, the Parkina-
Foldesy co. has substantial claims to the patronage of
music lovers. But so far as Miss Elizabeth Parkina, the
American soprano, is concerned, anticipations were
raised just a little too high by the preliminary puffing, in
which she was described as “la petite Melba.” People
were prepared to hear a vocalist of prime magnitude
instead of one who, though highly cultured, has hardly
reached the level of the popular Mdlle. Dolores. They
have been proportionately disappointed, and unneces-
sarily so, for on her own merits she would have scored.
Her voice is a soprano leggiero, petite if you like, but
trained beyond average purity, of extended range, and
utilised with high artistic sense. To couple her name
with Melba’s was too absurd. There is only one Melba,
who stands majestically high on the musical horizon.
Alongside her artists of the Parkina level rank compara-
tively as pigmies. Of this fact those who have heard
Parkina have only become more convinced than ever.
Miss Margaret Thomas, the fair Welsh contralto, cap-
tured Sydney by her clear enunciation and expressive
rendering of simple airs, whether in English, Irish,
Scotch, Italian or German. She rolls her “r’s” rather
and emphasises her German gutturals, but not offen-
sively. Her punctilious particularity in this respect is
rather a good lesson for young Australians. The voice is
light, almost verging on the mezzo-soprano, but is exceed-
ingly well managed.
Some of the silvery quality of Mr. Henry Lloyd is
noticeable in the sweet tenor, Mr. Whitworth Mitton,
who is especially good in declamatory passages. More
uniformity of production would be an improvement, but
time and persevering study ought to accomplish this.
A pleasing feature of Mr. Mitton’s repertory is that he
breaks new ground to which Australians are quite
unaccustomed from tenors. At one concert his
“ Onaway Awake ” (Hiawatha’s Wedding Feast) by
Coleridge Taylor was peculiarly acceptable.
A most accomplished ’cellist is Mr. Arnold Foldesy.
While in such pieces as Svensden’s Romance and Max
Bruch’s “ Kol Nidrei ” he elevates his instrument to a lofty
sphere of eloquence, he shows dazzling brilliancy in high
shift-fingering and deft intrepid bowing while playing
Popper’s “ Spinnerlied ” and Paganini’s transcription of
the popular air from “ Moses in Egypt.” The latter was
written for the G string of the violin and on the ’cello the
performer limits himself to the A string which is specially
lowered one tone for the piece.

Mr. Watkin Mills’ big English quartette
party will come to Australia during the winter.
Following are names of the artists : Miss Edith
Kirkwood (soprano), Miss Gertrude Lonsdale
(contralto), Mr. Harold Wilde (tenor), Mr. Watkin
Mills (basso); piano and accompanist, Mr. M.
Edouard Parlovitz. Mr. Spencer Jones directs
the tour.
** * *
W. T. (bed) Stead has been to see a play—
the “ Tempest,” and his reflections fill seven pages
of the Review of Reviews (20/1/’05.) They’re
mostly of the bed-Stead type :
At this moment when I sit watching the result of the
merciless enslavement inflicted upon Caliban for attempt*
ing the virtue of a maid, there are sitting doleful in felon; *
cells throughout the English speaking world men serving
out sentences of imprisonment with hard labor for
offences against the virtue of maids, who but for my
action 19 years ago would have been free from prison
bars. The Criminal Law Amendment Act, which begot
many similar measures in the Colonies and in the United
States, made it an offence punishable with two years’
hard labor, to commit or attempt to commit the offence of
which Caliban was guilty under the plea of the consent of
the victim so long as she had not attained the age of 16. . . .
When Prospero reminds Caliban of his attack upon
Miranda’s honor, the hairy brute replies with an excla-
mation which implies more of a craving for paternity
than the satisfaction of a brute instinct. Poor Caliban !
Ferdinand and Miranda nowadays would have one child,
or perhaps two, leaving the task of perpetuating the race
almost entirely to Caliban. The cultured, the wealthy
and refined shrink from the duty of replenishing the
earth.
Interesting subject for a competition :
find a subject in writing about which Mr. Stead
cannot drag in sex.

A six weeks’ run at this dull season of the
year shows how hard Bland Holt hit the public
taste with “The Best of Friends.” But the end
comes Friday night, after which “Women and
Wine”; and then with “The Betting Book”
B.H. closes his long run at Sydney Royal.

So many people wanted to see Nellie
Stewart’s toes peep out from beneath “Camille’s”
bedgown that, at the last moment, the Musgrove
management decided to keep “Sweet Nell” wait-
ing on the Lyceum doorstep for another week.

#* * *
Adelaide Royal has opened with the
Anderson pantomime, “ Sinbad the Sailor,” and
there has been the customary rush of extra ladies
to the stage door in the hope of becoming a fairy
or a flower for a fortnight. The massive Miss
Ravensberg is principal boy. Bates, Graham, and
Bert Bailey have the fat comic parts. At the
Tivoli the Andrew Hodge co. has finishi a
brief season. A crowd that can play “ ist
Lynne,” then show its agility in pantomime 1 : a
week, and afterwards put on its skirts and < ler
things, and go back to “The Kelly Gang, ’ is
entitled to credit for being at least versatile.
“A Royal Divorce,” refreshed in public
interest by the recent production of “L’Aiglon,”
had an overflowing audience at Her Majesty’s
last Saturday. In the light of existing compari-
son, the doleful tale of Buonaparte’s downfall
calls for approval rather than otherwise. It is
nothing for the author to feel very proud of, but
it might have been something very much worse.
It treats history with utter contempt—but history
deserves no pity on that account. And it has the
excellent effect of cheapening kings and queens.
The alleged greatness of Emperor Julius Knight
is not perceptible, his only triumphant achieve-
ment during the drama being an infant son, who
subsequently coughed himself into an early grave;
Marie Louise’s family pride collapses when she
most needs it; the incidental King of Naples is an
unmajestic person who talks through his nose; and
ex-Empress Josephine has a plebeian habit of pulling
a wry mouth. Royalty wins no reverence anywhere.
It is always coming to grief. As a matter of fact,
all the people in “ A Royal Divorce,” from Napo-
leon down to the deplorable British admiral with
a sham-nose, are sorrowful creatures. Julius
Knight has come back to Melbourne with a reno-
vated voice, and his heavy picture of the discom-
fited Napoleon is perhaps a little more finished
than of yore. Miss Maud Jeffries is a handsome,
low-spirited heroine. Miss Mabel Lane, as Marie
Louise, forms an effective red-haired link between
this drama and “L’Aiglon” (in which she played
the same Marie in the same colored wig), and
Miss Unie Russell relieves the gloom as much as
Eossible by her gay and dainty performance of
tephanie, the light-comedy girl who frivols when
empires are falling. The bulk of the cast has
some weak spots in it.
** * *
The 'cello-drama at the Princess’s con-
tinues to have music in its soul and foolishness in
its body ; also the appalling secret Russian police-
man has thus far defeated Van Borinski’s laud-
able intention to kill him, and the wicked
duchess gets no nearer than before to the
accomplishment of her amorous designs. A
change of cast resulted in a slight tonmg-down of
“ The Broken Melody.” The tall comedian who
appeared as a noisy cockney theatrical agent
has given place to Horace Lingard the Horace
Lingard of former Australian days. Horace draws
the character rather mild, and sets one thinking
of what George Lauri may be in the distant
future when George shall have grown elderly.
One of Van Biene’s solos is now dropped into the
first act, and this is an improvement. Van Biene’s
’cello-playing atones for many absurdities in his
drama. But his efforts at original composition
and “ fantasia” arrangement are not so effective
as his attacks upon the big fiddle.
** * *
Manager Anderson is offering 5 guineas
reward for the three best letters, written by ladies
only, showing the moral merits of “ A Girl’s Cross
Roads, ’ and lie will doubtless expect long letters
for his money. The drama at the Melb. Royal
seems so hazy in its moral merits that some of
them will be hard matters to explain. In the cases
of bibulous Barbara and naughty Nellie, the
wages of sin is death, which would be a moral
lesson of the finest quality if the drama
made any endeavor to reform their charac-
ters. But it doesn’t. Barbara’s husband allows
her to dally with a decanter when he ought to be
leading her out of temptation, and Nellie’s
rural aged father permits the young spinster to
prowl around in a palpably loose manner rather
than trouble himself to make personal enquiries
concerning her business in the city. One thing
wanting in “A Girl’s Cross Roads ” is a fine, able-
bodied missionary who would talk seriously to
Barbara and Nellie before it was too late, and
bring out their moral merits, if they had any. If
they were hopelessly bad they would possibly ruin
the missionary, and serve as a warning to the
Y.M.C.A.

Walter Sanford and his somewhat Ameri-
can Players are changing their joke weekly at
Melb. Bijou. The present whirlwind, described
as an intense drama of London life, contains more
incident to the square minute than a conflagration
in a cartridge factory, but the programme next
Saturday will be “ Uncle Tom’s Cabin,” which is
not usually played at a furious rate of interest.
The Sanfordian treatment of de ole niggadrama is
awaited with curiosity, not to say anticipated with
dread.

muscular item on the following night.

Melbourne is to get the Parkina-Foldesy
Concert Co. at the Town Hall, on Saturday next,
and five subsequent dates.
Four dramas raged in Melbourne at one time,
And two were nightmares, full of gin and crime ;
The third was musical in name and deed—
A long-haired ’cellist played the tuneful lead.
More rich than these in scenical resource,
The fourth play dealt with battle and divorce.
From show to show the toiling critic crept,
Then shook his head, and wept, and wept, and wept.
The Wild Cat Column.

This is one of the concerns which, it is alleged, Australia
“ treats like criminals ” ! In four years it has paid divi-
dends amounting to £520,000, getting back in this short
period more than one-third of its whole paid capital.
Besides that, it has written off £267,000, mostly a legacy
of bad debts left over from the time when the country
was run by the crowd which is now saying that Australia
is “going to the dogs.” And, in addition to paying
£520,000 in divs. in four years and clearing up £267,000 of
the mess of the past, it has increased its reserves by
£44,944. Five years ago the Union Bank showed, on the
whole of its assets, a profit of 15s. 6d. per cent. ; now they
earn for the bank £1 2s. 4d. per cent.—which is a pretty
good proof that “stiff” accounts aren’t as common as
they used to be. Ten years ago its own funds (capital
and reserves) earned £3 9s. 6d. per cent. ; five
years ago (1899) £5 11s. 3d. per cent. ; last half-year,
£9 Is. 7d. per cent, per annum. If this is treating the
foreign capitalist like a criminal, the criminal is a mighty
lucky person. Comparing the bank’s position at August
31st last with that of 12 months previously, deposits had
increased from £15,525,725 to £15,699,612, but, partly on
account of the more prosperous times last year, advances
fell from £12,497,141 to £11,968,017. None of the move-
ments were, however, of much consequence.

A new co. was formed as at July Ist; of the old reserves
£75,000 was capitalised, and the balance was apparently
all written off. Steamships, etc., which stood (at cost) in
the old balance-sheet at £169,884 (June 30th) stand now,
with half-year’s additions, at £127,118. New co. evidently
intends to adopt the old co.’s plan with regard to provi-
sion for wear and tear, writing nothing off shipping
assets, but showing the provision on the other side as a
depreciation reserve. The reserves will, therefore, con-
tinue to be to that extent nominal, while the profits
shown will not be net profits. This half-year £3OOO is set
aside for depreciation, which is rather less than 5 percent,
per annum on fixed assets. On the old capital (£75,000)
the net profit (£10,374) is at the rate of £27 13s. per cent,
per annum ; on the total, including the £75,000 capitalised,
£l3 16s.—a very substantial improvement on late records.
Besides its ships, &c., co. has £30,905 in public stocks,
cash, &c., in addition to stores and trade debts, which
amount to £5352 in excess of the total owing to trade
creditors. The new co., which offers its employees some-
thing in the way of profit-sharing, starts off, therefore,
unusually well.

The writing-off this half-year is really a part of the former
writing-off, when the matter doesn’t appear to have been
completed. Even now the total of £4BOO is shown under
“ accumulated profits account,” and might easily be
mistaken for a reserve, as ships and other properties
remain at approximately their old figure, in spite of
admitted depreciation. In addition to this special
writing-off there is a regular charge (recently of £938 per
half-year) for wear and tear; and as this amounts to
about 10 p.c. p.a. on all properties (after the special
writing-off), the assets ought not now to be over-valued.
An all-round 10 p.c. writing-off is more liberal than one
usually meets, anyhow. Moreover, on the whole assets
the disclosed profit was equal to about 8J per cent, last
year, which also seems to show that the assets are sound,
the writings-off sufficient, and the net profits as stated
fully earned. The position shows solid improvement.

The position is hardly as good as usual. The increase in
book debts is not a satisfactory feature. Gross profits on
goods have certainly moved up in the 12 months, but,
against that, expenses (which include directors’ fees,
salaries, bad debts, insurance, &c.) have increased in a
larger proportion ; and interest is increasing rather rapidly.
Considering the co.’s very heavy indebtedness, amounting
to very nearly 255. for every £i of capital, the surplus of
profits over dividends is very small, and it looks even
smaller when the insignificance of the reserves is taken
into account.

The co. carries £500,000 of 4J p.c. debentures ; and that
is where the shareholders’ troubles lie. Shareholders
have, in fact, had a horribly bad time. The foregoing
table shows what capital they have lost ; and there have
been only four years in the co.’s life in which they have
handled a div. For years it looked as if the whole con-
cern must go to the debenture-holders ; but this year
there has been a turn, and a trifle over the debenture-
holders’ bite of £22,500 is earned. It isn’t anything like
enough to distribute ; but it is £11,557 better than the
previous year. This shows how matters have been moving
since the big writing-down (there was another writing-
do wrj in 1901, when £115,453 came off freeholds, etc.) :

Between 1897 and 1902 heavy calls were made, amounting
in all to £214,098, most of which was written off as shown
above. “ Freeholds, &c.” seem to be the immovable quan-
tity ; and, as all receipts are bunched, whether from rents
or brewings, it is impossible to tell how much these assets
are really worth. In almost all other respects an im-
proving position is disclosed. The best new movement of
all is, of course, in profit; but, unfortunately, the figures
shown are less depreciation, and whether more is written
off one year than in another is unknown. But, assuming
the thing is done on some system, the shareholders have
now a little hope to go on with.

This co. sells land on time payment, puts part of the
profit to contingent account to provide for bad debts,
and when it is more or less sure that the bad debts won’t
happen it reduces the account by paying large divs ;
hence the erratic movements of the latter. Recently,
however, the contingent account hasn’t been growing,
notwithstanding that no big divs. have been paid. At
Dee., 1903, it was £4324; at June, 1904, £3930 ; at Dec.,
1904, £3592. Some of the possible bad debts have
thus happened ; evidently the stoppage of the
Owe’-See loan boom has had its effect, and the fictitious
market values of “buildingblocks” have disappeared more
or less. Still, even at the present rate last year showed
a return of just about 7 per cent, on capital (£6842) and
reserves ; that is, provided enough was put to contingent
fund to cover possible losses on current business. Besides
its own funds, co. has £26,767 of the public’s money ; its
assets are freeholds £11,124 and balances due and ad-
vances £30,204. No doubt, with the erratic property
market which is the natural result of periodical loan
drunks and dependence upon industries which are liable
to be knocked out by droughts, the business is risky (a
76* per cent. div. is a pretty good hint of that) ; but the
co.’s own £14,928 is a substantial cover for £41,328 worth
(book value) of properties, balances, &c.

offer somebody else 6 per cent., if the concern could earn
it, rather than take any more risk themselves. Besides
the debentures, the co. owes £13,915, and has liquid assets
(including book debts) amounting to £11,379.

The true profits are somewhat less than is shown here.
The reserves consist of formal reserve fund £ll,OOO, con-
tingency account £4013, and undivided profit £998. The
contingency fund has an unexplained accession of £250
in the half-year, and this has therefore to be shown as
profit; but this fund is drawn upon, among other things,
to pay rates, and practically the addition is the half-year’s
contribution to the year ’t rates. Still, it stands in the
balance sheet as a reserve for the presen c, and must
be so regarded till it is paid away. Few changes of
any note have occurred. Deposits are £29,338 against
£28,582 twelve months ago, and are still in the safe pro-
portion of less than half of the society’s own funds. Loans
outstanding amount to £26,013, against £25,805 ; proper-
ties in possession £60,984 against £62,360, which is a move-
ment the right way. Net rents show a slight reduction,
but still give a return of over £5 19s. p.c. p.a., so the pro-
perties are probably very little, if at all, overvalued. There
are, in fact, very few “ cleaner ” building-society balance-
sheets issued.

This society sells land on time-payment, and part of the
profit shown is in respect of transactions not yet com-
pleted ; so that the profit may or may not be fully
realised eventually. The Mortgagors’ Contingent Reserve,
which bears the drain of the sale of properties at a loss,
now stands at £3232; and defaulting mortgages
stand in the books at £10,463, against £16,599 in 1903 and
£19,559 in 1902. Provided the transactions are satis-
factorily completed, this is a very satisfactory reduction
of an always doubtful asset—£9o96 in two years, at a cost
apparently of £1632. The co. owes, in one way and another,
£91,670, rather more than half of which is covered by deben-
tures. Against this, in addition to the comparatively small
amount of foreclosed properties mentioned, the society’s
assets are £13,463 balances on land sales, mortgages, &c. ;
£115,756 in various properties ; £16,737 in fixed deposits,
bank balances, &c. The properties are being sold as fast
as possible ; but, though £20,790 is represented by farming
property, the land-selling business these times is painfully
slow, and meantime the interest-return on the invest-
ment is, as usual, poor. The vacant land that lies waiting
for some one to come along and buy it hasn’t a habit of
yielding much in the way of dividends. It is only when a
boom happens, and the enthusiastic buyer is discovered,
that this kind of speculating society smiles. And the pros-
pect of any rush of enthusiastic buyers isn’t extra brilliant
at present.

* « * *
Perseverance, Kalgoorlie, is going to pay a div.
of 9d., or £52,500. Some of those who control the show
are well-known on the London market, and it is just as
well for the garden variety of gambler to keep awake
w hen they touch the scrip. Recently it was reported that
a good find had been made at 700 ft., but from inquiries
made, it looks as if this find were largely, if not wholly, a
myth. Below 700 ft. the lode is, so far as tested, valueless,
and the estimated ore reserves will only last nine months.
Still this need not be taken too seriously, as ore reserves
have a habit of bobbing up serenely, if not in values, at
least in tonnage. Ralph Nichols has beaten down all oppo-
sition, and is coming back to take personal oontroL
* * * *
Boulder Main Reef (W.A.) has decided to re-
construct, with an assessment of 25. , equivalent to £26,000.
Co.’s leases are in a good position, but position in mining
counts for little ; and, as the southern portion of the
Great Boulder leases is, apparently, no good, prospects of
main reef don’t look healthy.
■* * * *
Golden Pole (W.A.) reef in all three levels
ranges from 3ft. 6in. to 6ft. in width, and from 2oz. 15dwt.
to 6oz. Bdwt. in value. But winze below 3 level is in ore
6ft. wide, worth only 12dwt., the low grade being blamed
to a calcite intrusion. No. 4 level is to be started shortly,
and, as mine is now market-valued at £300,000, holders are
anxiously waiting to see how reef will pan out, as future
of mine depends on the result. The extra 10 heads are
now working, and March return should show a material
increase, and April div. will be 9d. The 6d. div. hitherto
paid has been cutting things very fine, but there should
now be a margin over and above 9d.
* * » *
Recent collapse of Mt. Morgans (W.A.) is now
explained. Arbitration Court was about to give its
award, and mine-owners wanted a reduction of 2s. 6d. a
week in Menzies district and ss. on northern fields. A.
E. Morgans says the mill will be doubled to 120-head,
capable of treating 14,000 tons of 7 or Bdwt. ore monthly
at a profit. In fact, all the big mines will get down to
that average before long, and will have to reduce costs
accordingly. A new IOOOh.p. engine is being erected,
so it must have been ordered long before the collapse.
ff TT W *
Golden Horseshoe, Kalgoorlie, for the first time,
publishes its return in fine ounces, and gives total costs
but no details. Total treated was 18,000 tons for 14,5200 z.,
worth £58,400, at a cost of £33,900, or 395. Bd. per
ton. On the average of the past few months 2000 tons
were smelted for 82500 z., worth £33,000, aud 16,000 tons
milled for 6,0000 z., worth £25,400. Taking costs of the 2000
tons smelted at the high figure of £6 a ton, or £12,000, the
costs of the 16,000 tons milled works out at £23,900. As
the gross value is only £25,400, the net profit on these
16,000 tons pans out at £l5OO only, or less than 2s. a ton.
Shareholders in co. are sitting on a volcano, and will be
blown sky high some day, When the rich shoot peters out,
as did that in the L.V. Consols, Boulder Perseverance, <fcc.
• * * •
Hidden Secret, Kalgoorlie, has paid a first div.
of £l2 10s. a share on its 800 shares, equivalent to £lO,OOO.
There are six shareholders, four of whom are Italians, and
their divs. range from £3375 down to £312 10s. Last
parcel for which returns have been received from smelters
panned out 26 tons for 2240 z. gold and 10670 z. silver ;
total to date (that is since October), 202 tons for 25920 z.
gold and 13,9850 z. silver; worth £ll,BOO, or over £5B a
ton. A further 28-ton lot has been despatched, and is ex-
pected to pan out at least £4O a ton. The lode at 200 ft.
is 9ft. wide, and as rich as at 150 ft. Recently the sam*
lode was picked up in an old drive at 100 ft. This drive
ran nearly parallel to and only 4ft. from the lode. It was
put in a year ago, and only just missed bringing the mine
to the front then. The ore, even at 100 ft., is a dense sul-
phide, and gives very poor results by panning. In fact,
a sample tested by panning to be worth 20z., assayed
440 z. gold and 300 oz. silver, and no gold was visible in the
stone. This lode might have been cut through in the
earlj- days, and regarded as worthless.
• * • •
East Murchison United, Lawler’s (W.A.), one
of Bewick, Moreing and Co.’s mines, has been auctioned
on behalf of the Union Bank, to whom concern was
mortgaged for £20,000 odd. Co. owned a 40-head mill, half
of which has been in use since 1896, and half since 1901;
but, as mine is 100 miles from a railway, the whole
concern brought only £19,500. Co. was rather a
flourishing concern in the early days, and paid £60,000 in
divs. between 1897 and 1900. Shareholders were recently
invited to put up more cash, but, for once, Bull buttoned
up his pockets. It is only 18 months since Long Reef,
another of 8.,M. and Co.’s unfortunates, was auctioned off
and shareholders left in the mud.
• * * «
In the past two years B.H. South has made
£137,271, which has been cut up this way:—Divs.,
£60,000; written off, > £32,110; carried to reserves,
£45,161. Writings-off were unusually heavy—no less than
£20,300 in the first half of last year, £15,000 coming off
the concentrating mill. Latter now stands as an asset
for only £21,148, though it cost more than three times
that and is still serviceable ; but it will be replaced pre-
sently by a new mammoth mill. The big increase of
reserves is to pay for the new plant. Co. had nearly
£70,000 in cash or public securities on Dec. 31st, while its
excess of all liquid assets over liabilities was £81,224,
equal to about Bs. 3d. per share. Net profits for past two
years were at the rate of 33J per cent, per annum on pre-
sent capital(£lsB,7B2). For size of co., the South has the
biggest ore reserves at Broken Hill, and its ore is cheaply
mined and of good concentrating quality. As a zinc pro-
position it wouldn’t show up as well as some of the others,
for its ore only runs about 15 per cent, zinc ; but, spite of
all that’s said about zinc, there is mighty little profit in
that business so far.
• * • •
' Broken Hill shareholders own about 50,000
shares in Junction North, and, disgusted with past con-
trol, want to put a couple of directors on the Board.
Complaint is that the mine hitherto has played seconds
to the market; and the absurdly dilatory way in which
work has been carried on seems to point to something of
the sort. Holders who want to see the mine pay divs.
couldn’t do better than give the Broken Hill movement
their help.
* * « *
B. H. Junction is still doing rather poor work,
turning out less than 1 ton of concentrates to 7 of ore.
The average of the ore (under 11 oz. silver and about 15
p.c. of lead) is not rich enough to stand this, especially
as the recoveries are only about 47 p.c. of the silver and
58 p.c. of the lead, and mining is expensive. Things may
improve as the mill gets further to work, but at present
there can’t be much, if any, profit in the business.

Since Tom Harvey and Herbert J. Daly found
Jubilee, and since Daly’s English crowd refused it, the
Melbourne co. has netted £65,700 in divs., representing
profits on 43,1610 z. won from 93,682 tons milled.
Actual value of output Of gold, £173,978 ; so that

BJdwt. Thanks to Alcock (formerly of Glenfine South),

Christinas bonus, and so raised the proportion of divs. -
disbursed to total expenses to 46 per cent. Mine is well-
handled, and, as the board is not frightened to try wfiat
is below, there is a fair quantity of crushing dirt in sight

• * • *
Mt. Lyell Blocks (Tas.) half-yearly report tells
a poor tale, but not so bad a one as if the January ran of
the mill had come within the term. Co. started the sbt
months with a cash credit of £1095, and “sundry
debtors” £1074, and ended the half-year with cash re-
duced to £564, and “sundry debtors” £l5O. Not only
that, but at end of half-year it owed “ sundry creditors ”
£1361 as against £971 on June 30, and had built up a nice
little overdraft of £6042. To meet the debts_it had
“ estimated surpluses ” from concentrates, £4973, and
the aforesaid £564 cash. So drift all along the line
had taken place, rendering recent call imperative.
Directors made a great yarn about working account
showing a profit. It does—£79o—but that is only possible
by taking credit for the “ estimated surpluses,” and these
very often are an unknown quantity. Co. got over £7l per
ton for its copper from the Wallaroo Co., with which it
has a six months’ contract. Wallaroo paid a top rate
because it specially wanted such concentrates, and it will
not be surprising if the next contract is less favorable.
Manager Nicholas holds out that the mill is O.K.
Tested on the basis of his sampling, it handled 2‘37 per
cent, stuff, and recovered 55"24 of the metal contents!
Loss is put down mainly to copper glance. If so, a new
vista appears ; for it is one thing to concentrate native
copper, and quite another to save copper-glance. What
is the percentage of glance? If unknown, it ought to be
determined, for the factor involved by its presence may
mean a re-valuation of the whole venture. On the top of
the adverse balance-sheet comes the news of a “creep”
at the mine, so that generally there is a shivery sensation
down the back of the Blocks crowd.
Mining men with concentrating problems to
work out will be interested to know that A. R. Wilfley, of
Denver, patentee of the world renowned Wilfley Concen-
trator, which has proved itself superior to all other types,
has been devoting his attention to the production of a
machine to concentrate extremely fine mineral and shines,
and has brought out and perfected an entirely new table-
using new mechanical principles for the separation of
mineral from its gangue. The new table has been
thoroughly tested and proved before placing it on the
market and has already been adopted by many leading
mines in America and Mexico. It is adaptable to any
kind of fine mineral and will fill a want long felt in muling
problems. It is of large capacity, being designed to take
the entire tailings and washwater from one Wilfley table.
Full particulars of the Wilfley Slime Table can be obtained
from the Austral Otis Engineering Company, Limited, of
South Melbourne, who have been appointed sole manu-
facturers in Australasia.*

The new electric light installation of the Mel-
bourne Exhibition Cycle Track, formed a great attraction
of the recent “ Austral ” meeting. The main feature of
the system is the “ Gams ” brilliant arc lamp, forty of
which are distributed round the track, suspended on iron
brackets. The light is absolutely steady, soft and
diffused, doing away entirely with the injurious glare
common with other lamps and overcoming the objection-
able shadows in and under the globe. Special globes are
used which, while diffusing the light, do not cut off an
undue quantity and the effect is _ a light known
scientifically to be the best for all illuminating purposes.
The candle power is more than four times that furnished
by other lamps at the same current consumption. Electric
lighting by the “ Ganz ” arc lamp is cheaper than any
other form of illuminant, both in first cost, in fixing and
in maintenance. The contractors for this installation
were the Austral Otis Engineering Co., Ltd., corner
Hannah and Kavanagh streets, South Melbourne, Sole
Agents for Ganz and Co., Budapest, Hungary, who will
gladly furnish estimates and expert advice upon similai
or any electrical schemes.*
* * * *
Of the several rook drills used in Australasia,
each is claimed by its maker to be as good, or better,
than its rivals. Despite the varied opinions of various
makers, the fact stands out clear that the bidk of Aus-
tralasia’s mining folk consider the Rand Co.’s Slugger and
Little Giant types the foremost drills selling, for there are
a far greater number in use than of any other known
make. A drill’s excellence is not demonstrated by an
“ alleged record,” neither can its cost be judged by initia)
price. Superior effectiveness in the long run, freedom
from repairs and consequent idleness, cost of mainten-
ance, economy of air-consumption, are all points which
mining managers consider. The opinion of the average
mine manager here as to the best types of drills has been
packed up to a startling extent by the_ report of a special
and independent Johannesburg committee, whose object
was to have exhaustive tests of every rock drill in use op
the Rand. Th* Slugger and Little Giant cpme first
and second respectively, their superiority over other
machines heing extraordinary. Complete details of these
S. African tests will be furnished on application to N.
Guthridge Ltd., Sydney, Melbourne, and Wellington
(N.Z.).*
* p * *
The strength, lightness, durability and low cost
of spiral rivited pipe have given ft a place of superiority
above any .other pipe. This pipe possesses enormous
bursting strength, and is protected, inside and out, with a
perfect coating manufactured from asphalt. Asphalt
coated steel pipes have lasted from 25 to 40 years without
any sign of decay, and may last as long again. Spiral
pipe is manufactured by Mephan Ferguson, at Wanganui,
Sew Zealand, and at his engineering and pipe works,
Melbourne, where the latest up-to-date machinery is also
used for making boilers, fluming, refrigerators, bolts, nuts,
and every discription of cast o.r wrought iron work.*

Charters Towers, —Mining speculators would

• * * *
Economical Gold Mining. — Low grade ores
and high cost in fuel and wages have made it imperative
that managers and engineers should reconstruct upon
more economical lines. Heretofore the boiler plant was
given little consideration, but the introduction of the
Hornsby “upright” water tube boiler having demons-
trated great economy, and with safety under indifferent
conditions of feed water, it is now superseding other types.
In W.A. these boilers are worked with an economy not
heretofore known on those fields, and in Queensland over
2500 h.p. is on order for the Mount Morgan Gold Mining
Co. It is cleaned in one fourth the time of other boilers
and has no tube troubles. Boilers may be seen in opera-
tion in Sydney and Melbourne. R. Hornsby and Sons,
Ltd., Sussex-street, Sydney, and Spencer-street, Mel-
bourne.*
* * -t *
The Babcock Boiler seems to be favored by
mining engineers, over 50 mines in Australia using these
boilers. Mount Morgan has 20, Waihi Gold Mining Co,
24, Mount Lyell 12, Tasmanian Gold Mining Company 7,
and at Broken Hill there are 30 distributed amongst the
mines, water works, and electric supply, while in
Western Australia they are looked upon as a necessity,
there being now 68 in the principal mines and Kalgoorlie
Water Supply. It will be noted that coal is expensive at
Broken Hill, and wood fuel is expensive at W.A. These
figures speak for themselves. Evidently _ the ease of
transportation, the economy in fuel and maintenance and
the adaptability to any class of fuel, that are such con-
spicuous points in the Babcock Boiler, are much
appreciated in Australian mines.*

“ Take the tide at the flood ” is good advice in
mining, and the promoters of the Gladstone Tin Develop-
ment Co. are popping off a side-show at the Dorset Flats,
near the Pioneer tin mine (Tas.), while the public is on
the feed. They ask for and have been given—lucky
people—a half-interest for £7500, to be used in placing a
dredge on the lease. The rally is entirely a Long Tunnel-
Gladstone rush, with Ward, the broker, working up the Ade-
laide connection. The name that charms isL. W. Grayson’s ;
Doubters want to know whether cheek bores have been put
down, what reliance can be put on bores as an index of
the worth of the ground, and what surety is there that
the tin can be saved in the short length of a dredge ?

been appointed a lay bishop of the Church of

tory act of respect.” But why not commission a

what as follows:

bourne folk. _ A man offers for sixpence, with a
airt-insinuating leer, a sealed' envelope—“ Real
good cards in it, mister.” The unwholesome-

A band of burglars who broke into a Melbourne warehouse played
cards and smoked many cigars on the premises. They also arranged
a quantity of Persian cushions from the stock, and had evidently slept
upon them.
THE LAW: “ Hello! Hello! What’s the matter?”
THE CITIZEN : “ Confound it, man, don’t snore so loud;
you may waken the burglars.”

to give up whisky in order to flourish as husband

Various fathers are comparing notes in
Melb. Age as to their weekly expenses in connec-
tion with the “free” education system. Chip
man’s yarn :
At one school in Collingwood, when the instructor
arrives, the children who pay are ordered to stand and
welcome him by repeating the words, “ We are your
friends, Mr. ,” whilst the other poor little ones sit
with downcast heads until the fortunate ones are seated,
when they in turn are forced to rise and repeat, “ We are
nobody’s friends.”
Reads like a grim lie ! But it is vouched for as a
fact. What cruelty—dull, humorless cruelty !
* * * *
An up-country ball committee, which had
been pursued by bush-fires for a week, got
a bad shock at a hop which it had arranged by
way of making friends with 1905. The weather
was frizzling, so it settled that the supper was to be
light above all things ; also, as most of the dads had
had their grass burnt, and were grumbling about
expenses, it decided to call for tenders. An
unknown person at the next township offered to
do it cheapest, and got the job; and, as he told
the sweet country things that he’d been cooking
and catering all his life, they left everything to
him, and talked of the just-lovely time they were
going to have. At midnight they wandered out,
with the ices already melting on their lips ; and at
the supper-room a man wreathed in matted
whiskers and strange smells met them at the door,
and led them on—to barracouta and something he
said was Irish stew. They found out then that
he’d been rousers’ cook at Jumbuck station, and
had thrown in the barracouta for style.
* • • •
N.S. W. Marine Board’s inquiry into the
recent foundering, near Bellinger Heads, of the
schooner Violet Doe pel, threw suspicion upon the
seaworthiness of boats of thb fiat-bottomed type.
Many of the boats that trade between Sydney and
the various rivers are “flat-bottoms,” which possess
advantages in navigating the shifting sand bars at
the river entrances. Properly loaded, they sail
well before the wind, but beating against V head
they rise on the waves, and the impact on
striking the water in falling is such that the
starting of a “ leak ” is always among the
possibilities.

“ Mahor ” : Be the contention that white
men could not endure the temperatures in which
the colored firemen of the mail-boats work, this
summer I have taken the tem-
perature in one of the furnace-
rooms of a mine on the Golden
Mile, Kalgoorlie. The hottest
week averaged about 105 degrees*
the hottest day was 110 and the
coolest 98. The heat inside the
furnace-room ranged during the
week from *lO5 to 125* the 105
being taken about 12 o’clock at
night. Close in to the furnaces
when fire-doors were opened and
men were firing, the heat would
go to 130 or 140. The men have
four roasters to fire and look after.
If a raddle breaks they have to
assist to put in a new one, and this
is probably the hottest work of all.
Several of the firemen recently
went for a sea-trip and got leave
to {put in a shift in the stoke-
hole. They assure me that the
work is far easier than firing
roasters. The men on roasters
have to work a longer shift and
have always a dusty and, at times,
a stinking atmosphere to work in.
The best fireman are not total
abstainers but moderate drinkers.
Total labstainers drink too much
water, which soon knocks them
out. Throughout the summer
there lias only been one fireman
knocked hp and he was a total
abstainer. Numbers of marine
firemen have had a trial on the
roasters but few of them ever
stayi they always strike back
for the sea.

Orient vessels to fly the mail flag) on the Wes-

f inding himself in shallow water and miles away
from where he should have been, prudence de~

uaited for. clearer weather,. 33ut his owners were-
under a heavy penalty if the mail was delayed,

IT WAS SUCH A NICE DAY, TOO!
“Where every prospect pleases
And only man is vile."
A sympathetic Taranaki (M.L.) paper con-
doles with an old subscriber in a domestic
affliction :
It will be a sad blow to Mr. and Mrs. , for their
bright little daughter used to milk eleven cows night and
morning.
Suggested epitaph :
Our Daisy’s happier far, we know,
For she’s in Heaven ;
But still we sorely feel the blow—
She milked eleven.
But Polly’s chances would be poor
At Heaven’s Gate;
St. Peter fast would hold the door—
Her tally’s eight.
This would combine a business-like warning to
Polly with due sympathy for Daisy.

A vivid flash of warning on the race ques-
tion showed in St, Kilda road (Melb.), the other
day, when nine four-in-hand drags, loaded with
Chows and white women, bowled along, Beaumaris
bound. It was the Chinese cabinet-makers’ picnic.
Later on, the motley gathering was photographed,
and the heavy weeklies printed the piebald picture
in their distinguished-citizen series.

The advisability of choosing one’s summer
girl with discretion was brought home to a Mel-
bourne man who went into the
country Seeking rest and recu-
peration about a month back.
I t-happened that he put up at
the same establishment as the
lady- in charge of the telegraph
office there, and to wear away
th.o time began getting in his
best work with her. She re-
ciprocated warily, with
thoughts of matrimony. He
allowed that he considered
Miss Dot the most appetising
specimen of her sex. She heard
wedding bells in the air. Then
came one day a hasty message up
from Melbourne, addressed to him
as father, and mentioning mother.
Miss Dot took the wire up herself,
and stood with a cold, .malignant
eye fixed-on him as he read it; and
then commenced the oratorio. He
caught the next train for Melbourne,
although he was going lame, and despite
the fact that he had an impediment in his
eyesight caused by a blow from a fruit-plate.
Melbourne coroner Candler lately com-
mitted a man for manslaughter under unusual
circumstances. The victim was the bed-mate of a
stranger in a cheap city lodging-house, and was
found dead in the morning—the mate having gone
out early—with bruises and scratches on his
throat and a fractured skull. Immediate cause of
death, suffocation. Dr. Mollison, who post-
mortemed, gave his opinion :
The two men were sleeping in a narrow bed ; one of
them had an injury to his brain (the fractured skull)
which, after a time, would cause him to become uncon-
scious, and might produce convulsive movements. The .
other man, thinking he was attacked, might struggle .
with him and choke him in that way, unconsciously aryii ■
inadvertently ; and, being drunk at the time, he might.*
not remember anything about it. %

Victoria’s amazing “honorariums” had a
field day, last week. At Footscray, a baby-girl of
seven had stolen a pair of shoes from a shop. The
Bench decided that a good birching” would
meet the case, and looked at the sergeant. The
sergeant’s face was stone. The J’sP. turned to
the kiddy s father. “ I have already given her a
good flogging.” “ You don’t look after her. You
will have to give her a good flogging before the
-bench. “ All right, ” said he; and taking off his
belt, was about to baste seven devils out of the
unfortunate youngster, when it dawned on the
honorariums that a Temple of Justice was hardly
the place for. that sort of thing, and the parent was
asked to adjourn operations till the court was
over, when the street outside would afford more
scope for the young lady’s proper chastisement.
When the pair did get outside, the crowd was so
large and interested as to threaten a riot, and the
performance was adjourned sine die.
Rejoicing’ in our monarch’s love,
We fain would prostrate fall,
And sing our loyal praises of
The Gov’nor-General.
W th heart and voice in sweet accord,
Whilst angels on the wing
Proclaim the goodness of the lord,
Who represents our king.
One Throne there is above the sky,
And one beyond the wave,
The King whose name we magnify
Our earthly king can save.
Then let us call a blessing down
From Him, the King of all,
Upon the lord who serves the Crown
As Gov’nor-General.
Ah 1 men!
IN THE NAME OF THE PROFIT—PIGS!
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Halliwell I knew in our early days,
A writer of stories and dainty lays.
There is joy to me for a whole life long
In the verve and grace of his Morning Song.
And the tales he fashioned of love and strife
Were writ with a pen in the blood of life.
But a sore, sad man was the bard to see,
He was bare and poor as a bard might be.
Right grateful, too, for a roll to munch
With the famished crew at a counter-lunch.
Halliwell I met in the street to-day,
And his ribs were fat and his garments gay.
He had cronies now—an admiring throng’—
And his pockets clanked as he moved along.
“ Old friend," said I, “ have you prospered then
With the product rich of your fruitful pen ? ”
And he grinned awry as he said : “ It’s so ;
I am breeding pigs up at Bendigo ! ”

A quaint, gay little procession, headed by
a sturdy band, was come upon at various spots in
Melbourne city a few days ago. Bent had been
tickled on his soft side for free railway passes, and
other people had assisted, so 150 orphans from
the Ballarat District Orphan Asylum, the boys in
blue sailor suits, and the girls in red dresses and
white pinnies, had been landed in Melbourne, in a
state of wild excitement, for a couple of days’
visit. First of all they marched to the Zoo,
where they spent three wonderful hours in the
smellful abode of the bear and the monkey.
Then they were taken in vans to the Exhibition
building, entertained at lunch by the Mayor and
Mayoress, and afterwards let loose amongst the
finny monsters in the Aquarium until 5 p.m.
Thence the open-eyed, open-mouthed youngsters
were marched through the giddy, bustling streets
to the Town Hall, where a tea was waiting for
them, and afterwards an entertainment whiled
away the hours until 8 o’clock. Time then
to march down to Princes Court, to put
in three wild and woolly hours chuting
the chute, tobogganing, etc., etc., before
they headed for the Federal Coffee Palace, happy
and tired, and glad to be tucked into their little
beds. Next day they filled in the time with visits
to St. Kilda and Kenny’s Baths, the Museum,
and the ships in the river, and other delights. At
6 of that evening a tired but very contented little
company started on the return journey home, with
hazy recollections of the, elephant chuting the
chute, lions and tigers roaming around Collins-
street in cable trams, fishes and whales swim-
ming in the Town Hall, the Exhibition as a
gigantic cake-shop, and a firmly-rooted conviction
that Melbourne was Paradise, and Mr. Bent, who
gave the free railway passes, a bizarre brand of
archangel.

“Artesian” : In view of certain recent
advertisements, let me state that there is no
opening of any kind for young women in Noumea,
other than that afforded by the cafes. Some
unscrupulous registry offices obtain the names of
“patrons” of cafes in Noumea, and send girls
over, who pay their own fares on being assured
that the money will be refunded ; on arrival, the
girls find that no one has advertised. Some well-
known New Caledonians, though, meet young
women at the wharf, and escort them—whither ?
* * « #
Vie. Justice Hodges recently condemned
the police custom of charging suspected persons
with vagrancy in order to hold them pending the
fixing of some more serious charge. The judge
held that persons so arrested, without summons or
warrant, had ground of action for illegal imprison-
ment.

The block of land at the corner of Collins
and William streets (Melb.), where the A.M.P. is
building its new offices, measures 80ft. by 93ft.,
and is worth to-day from £20,000 to £25,000. It
occupies just one-third of the half-acre bought by
John Batman, in ’37, for £65. Batman put up a
two-story brick building, part of which he let to
auctioneer Williams. In this auction-room La-
trobe was proclaimed Superintendent of Port
Phillip, William Webb, Commissioner of Customs
and father of T. Prout Webb, reading the pro-
clamation. Upstairs, Rev. J. C. Grylls, first
Anglican clergyman in Melbourne, fixed himself.
SUSY'S WISDOM.
TEACHER : “ Now, Robert, tell me, what is bacon
when its alive? ”
ROBERT : “ Pigs, mum.”
TEACHER : “ Right. Now, Susy, what is mutton
when its alive? ”
SUSY : “High, mum.”
The Maori tohungas, who curse a man
for fun and cure him for a fee, continue to make
trouble in the North Island. At Kuiti (Auckland
district) there was an exciting time one day in
January, owing to a young woman believing she
was under a tohunga’s malison. Having got “into
holts” with the prophet, she clawed off sections
of his face before she went mad, whereupon three
of her relatives became similarly afflicted. At
the end of the scene practically the whole of the
pah was rushing round, howling incantations,
attacking alleged friends and imaginary foes,
yelling, singing, and praying by turns. At latest
the tohunga was making fast time for the heights
of Tongariro mountain, where- the climate hasn’t
so much scratch in it.

In the days when Melbourne was only an
unnamed village, a primitive arbitration tribunal
delivered a judgment which will,' perhaps, de-
finitely set at rest a vexed question. It is dated
May 2nd, 1830, and reads :
We award, in the disputes between Mr. Henry Batman
and Mr. John Fascoe Fawkner—on the first claim, thirty
shillings ; on the second, nothing, although a strong pre-
sumption is in our minds that some hasty expressions of
Mr. Batman’s may have led Bullet to destroy the rabbits.
On the third claim, damages five shillings, and a fine of
twenty shillings, in consideration of it being an act of
unauthorised aggression ; and on the fourth claim,
nothing, as it does not appear that Mr. Batman set the
dogs on the calf. —Signed, A. Thomson, John Aitkbn,
James Simpson.
It would thus appear to be Fawkner who was
really responsible for introducing bunny to Vic-
toria. Had those arbitrators possessed the gift of
prescience, they would have hailed Bullet (one of
Batman’s Sydney blacks) as a public benefactor,
and allowed him a few shillings to spend in Eawk-
ner’s unlicensed grog-shop.
London Daily Mail gives the London
poor a few hints as to how to get along “during
the present distress.” Skim-milk, it points out,
is almost as nourishing as milk with the cream in
it, and very much cheaper. Dried peas, lentils,
and haricot beans are also good foods, and can be
got cheaply from the corn merchants; also whole-
meal from the same source (crushed maize or oats)
is a food fit for the Freetrade working-man, and
can be bought at donkey-forage rates. For vege-
tables there are recommended the outside leaves of
lettuces, tops of turnips, leaves of carrots, and so
on, “ which can be obtained for nothing from the
kitchens of the rich.” Thus the working man can
live in London on skim-milk, cow-peas, and horse-
meal, flavored with the refuse pig-greens from the
houses of the rich (the rich probably would be
thoughtful enough to leave the grubs with the
cabbage stalks and lettuce refuse) —grubs are very
nourishing. But will the English working-man
consent to so live, or rather will he not, one day,
hang his landlords and rob his palaces, as the
Frenchman did a century or so ago?

“Genung”: Was at Melb. panto., the
other night. In one topical song, “ When Cap-
tain Cook first landed in Australia,” reference
was made to the Chows on the Rand. The lines,
We’ll make laws more stringent
- To send no more contingent,
broke the house up. Stalls and “ gods ” cheered.
Not a dissent. I shut my eyes and re-saw an inci-
dent of four years back. My hat was bashed, and
I was dragged towards a water-trough, alongside
this very theatre, because I didn’t cheer a passing
contingent, but quietly said, “ It’s a pity to lose
such a fine crowd of men.” The crowd has been
in the water-trough since.
The interfering small boy and the average
woman’s inability to load a two-wheeled convey-
ance on a proper balance were responsible for a
humorous incident at a busy Melbourne street-
crossing the other day. The conveyance, a light
cart drawn by a small pony, con
tained the driver and her small
son, in front, and two sub-
stantial lady friends behind.
As it came along Collins-
street, the tilt of the shafts
and the pressure of the
bellv-band showed that
the pony had but very
little control over the
trend of circumstances.
As the machine entered
on the slope up the hill
the pony’s grip of things
lessened even more, and
it was just there that a
perky, and beefy, small
boy sprang on to the
tail-board in a spirit of
joyous effervescence,
crying “whip behind.”
He immediately sat
down on the ground,
while two large ladies,
of great tonnage, flopped
on top of him, and a
diminutive youth turned
head over heels over the
lot. The pony also rose
heavenward for a space,
then sank down across
the shafts. The little
woman who was driving
stuck to the reins while
the cart gradually settled
down. The large ladies were
escorted into a shop to be dusted ; the trap
was set straight again, the balance of weight
adjusted properly, and a disgusted pony
tackled the hill once more. Then a small boy,
who had been feeling his ribs and the rest of his
anatomy to see how much was still sound, crept
solemnly away. He had had enough “whip
behind.”
Some of the subjects which ‘it is proposed
to add to Victoria’s State educational curri-
culum—already 20 subjects : Evils qf tobacco-
smoking ; astronomy; soils and their improvement;
book-keeping ; horticulture ; Bible history ; me-
chanical training in wood and iron ; moral lessons;
agriculture ; elocution ; dress-cutting. Teachers
now have to be artists with brush and pencil,
musicians, lightning-calculators, naturalists, gram-
marians, drill-sergeants ; and, top of all this, keep
to good hours and short drinks.
“Hexham”: SydneyD.T.
says the flood of books on the
Slav-Jap war further illus-
trates how the brain-tax on
modern civilisation is growing.
But does the average reader of
such literature —novels, maga-
zines and newspapers—really
“tax” his brains at all? The
reasoning faculty is more exer-
cised in a game of billiards
or draughts, and much more in
chess or whist, than in devour-
ing lewt. of war-news or cur-
rent fiction. Except with one
reader in a thousand, the stra-
tegy of Marshal This or G eneral
That no more appeals to reason
than the passing of a circus
procession. School-systems (in
English-speaking countries, at
any rate) almost entirely en-
courage memory at the expense
of reasoning powers, except for
mathematics. Children are not
taught to distinguish between
fact and inference. Hence
quacks and impostors, press and
otherwise, flourish. “I was
sick, and took So-and-so’s pills
and recovered; therefore the
medicine cured me.” A spar-
row has this much logic, so has
a cow. Instead of the brain-
tax growing, it is rather les-
sening, chiefly through present
methods of education and news-
paper reading.

Ex - Governor Ranf urly
bequeathed to M.L. a loyal
white elephant. The Veterans’
Home in Auckland, opened
nine months ago with much
pomp and male millinery, was
quite a fashionable object of
patronage while Ranfurly was
in the neighborhood ; but with
his departure came the slump,
and as the institution will cost
£lOOO a year to keep going,
and as there is no provision for its maintenance,
the burden will probably fall upon the general
Government.

The affairs of one family at a N.S.W.
coastal town. A timber-splitter met a girl of 17,
and there was soon necessity for a hasty marriage.
Some bitterness was caused at the wedding owing
to groom’s mother accusing him of stealing the
blankets off her bed for his new household. After
a honeymoon of two days at a boarding house
the couple returned to live with the bride’s people.
In the course of a few months the axeman fell
violently in love with his mother-in-law. Finally
the wood-cutter and his newly discovered affinity
fled to Sydney. A fortnight after they quarrelled
fiercely, and the lady returned to the farm, was
“forgiven,’’ and ate of the fatted goose. Then
the axeman drifted back from Sydney and took
his own little wife to his mother’s. The next ship-
load cf Australian missionaries for China might
call in on that district en route.
A cheerful thing iu the way of pamphlets
is being scattered by the “ Flutterers ” —the sect
that broke out at Bendigo a while ago, and has so
much faith in the grocer that he won’t let them
starve, that they scorn to work for a living. The
sect isn't numerous, so far, but it is too numerous
for Bendigo to keep going on its own, wherefore
contingents are wandering around Victoria ; and
when the grocer gives them something to go on
with they pray for him at the counter, and then
hand him a leaflet telling him that it’s GGI to 1
that he’ll be damned. Besides this habit of doing
nothing and promising free and general damnation,
the “Flutterers”’ religion seems to consist mostly
of the belief that the world is flat; they aim two
dozen texts at the man who won’t believe that,
and promise him something extra-special in the
way of Tophet.

So-called surprise inspections by the new
Inspector-General of Military Forces, on ordinary
parades, are now adopted to test the efficiency of
the various corps. The idea is good, but its object
is defeated, as Commanding Officers receive
private notice of the dates of these inspect : ons,
and preparations can be made, as in the days of
the late G.0.C., to have everything ship-shape.

report
Senator Higgs : “ You say you have no market for your
by-productaV ” “ Practically none. But a large propor-
tion of poisonous drugs, such as fusel oil, is used in the
manufacture of essences and teetotallers’ drinks.
So Cold Tea’s temperance drinks are compounded
with the deadliness subtracted from the alcohol
he ravesabout!

IN THE SHADOW.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Bless me before I sleep ;
Lay your sweet hand,
Lightly, upon my hair ;
Close to my bedside stand
(Yourself a prayer !).
Heart’s dearest, do not weep,
Do not demand
Fate to prolong- my share
Of time. Oh, understand,
Death’s life, you there !
VIC.
DOROTHY M'CRAE.
THE LOCAL MAYOR.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
He stood upon his balcony and glowed
With large, contented ox-eyes on the street;
The passing crowds, beholding him, bestowed
Salutes official and good wishes meet.
His broad, fat hand he waved when someone passed
Whose rank detached him from the common fold
Or who by luck or labor had amassed
An extra walletful of yellow gold.
But though he waved a hand or bowed his head
When rank and wealth passed on beneath his gaze
Ilis rosy smiles he genially shed
Upon the humblest in the busy maze.
And then he entered in and sat him down
Among his family, and sighing said,
“ It must be so, that he who wears the crown
Shall feel the weight of it upon his head.
“ I owe it to my people, high and low,
That I shall let them feast their eyes on me ,
The ruler and the ruled thus nearer grow
Till all become one civic family.
“ The trouble personal I reckon not—•
The wearied limbs and head distressed with pain
I’ve giv’n my people food for happy thought,
And that is first and last and greatest gain ! ”
O.

“Ern ” writes, and The Bulletin prints
for what it is worth :—
On Monday next, March 6th, an annular eclipse of the
sun takes place. Speaking astrologically, and before the
event, the position of this solar eclipse in the second
decanate of Pisces signifies, according to the old astrolo-
gers, “ death of famous and excellent men (have we
any?), destruction of fish, and earthquakes.” Applying
this, I would infer that Australia is not likely
to suffer from drought for the next two or
three years; rather from an excess of rain, causing
floods, etc., and possibly irruption of the sea on
the south and west coasts. Earthquakes are probable in
or near Melbourne, especially about the 6th and ‘21st inst.,
also April 20th, and a strange suit in the Divorce Court.
Adelaide is likely to have a panic in one of its public
buildings, and the position of the sun and moon in
conjunction, on March 6, at the time of new moon,
presages immense and continuous commercial prosperity
to the Commonwealth citizens.
These predictions are close enough to hand to
make it amusing, if not profitable, to check them
by actual happenings.
