“THE VARNISHED BOARD
A WOMAN’S LETTER.
_ _ Sydney, February 28, 1905.
My Dear Arini.
A startling marriage is that of Mr. Ebenezer
Vickery, a very rich Sydney citizen, who owns,
amongst other properties, Vickery’s chambers in
Pitt-street. His private house is out Waverley
way. Mr. Vickery’s first wife died a year or so
ago. I don’t know how old he is ; but this week
at a Sydney Methodist meeting, which he
“chaired,” he owned up to having taken an
interest in missions for 70 years. His eldest son
is past 50 ; and he has a son-in-law who is one of
the greybeards of the Methodist conference. The
new wife, who was his typewriter, is in the neigh-
borhood of 21.
Miss Enid Whiting, the lily maid of “ Astolat ”
(her parental home is “ Astolat, ” and she fits the
quotation nearly as well as if she had been named
Elaine), was married last week to a Melbourne
merchant named Waters, who fills in his spare
time at soldiering. They started off on their
honeymoon in a motor car.
What a superb dress-show is “The Orchid,”
that bloomed in Sydney for the first time on
Saturday night ! The orchid effect of making the
girls look like masses of human exotics has
been ingeniously carried out. Principals, ballets,
and choruses again and yet again recall varieties
of the cypripedium, cattleya, and other bizarre
blooms too difficult to spell. Mauve, purple and
white; pale green, mauve and brown, flushed
purples, pure whites with orange veinings, crim-
son, yellow and white, ochre and rose. When you
want a new evening, or race, frock or fancy dress,
consider the orchids. Miss Alexia Bassian, in a
non-orchidian gown, scores with a pale amber
overdress, with mandarin-yellow pipings and tiny
mandarins growing on the bodice; white-lace
underskirt, big hat of white and amber. What
funny summings-up of a play you hear on its first
night! “ Bassian’s petticoats are the best—all laced
with pink velvet baby-ribbon, and Laurie wears
wedding-garters and—and—aren’t men a bold
lot? All ears when anything about lingerie is
mentioned. Did you ever notice anything like
the silence of the house while Clare Clifton was
reading out the corset advertisement? Really
you almost hear yourself think ,” was what one
young woman had to say of the piece. When it
was insinuated that women must have aided the
great silence her logic did not fail: “Oh, yes;
but the men were quiet because they expected
something slightly improper.”
Audience at first night of “Orchid” showed
ever so many poodle-haired girls. They are
trying to copy Miss Nellie Stewart’s hair-dressing,
leaving only a little wedge of face as a guarantee
of good sight, an appetite, end a capacity to kiss.
I don it’s a pretty custom. I know men
don’t. They reckon that if a face is worth seeing
it should be shown, and if it is not worth seeing it
should still flaunt itself, as a danger-signal.
Mrs. Bland Holt, in a meteor-flag-of-England
dress, burned a, glowing red spot in the circle.
Mrs. .T. C. Williamson back from Melbourne was
present. Mrs. “Ted” Simpson in the stalls
glittered like a star system with a lot of cut steel
work and what looked like beetles’ wings in her
hair «ud over her shoulders. Mrs. Brasier de
Thuy in a scarlet gown and with a seventeen inch
French waist was another looker on. Some people
must have thought the play to be a flower show,
and so brought their children. Mrs. Philip
Morton had two large infants with her, and there
was another bread-and-honey group in a box.
Mr. and Mrs. Frank Connor represented
Westralia in the gathering. Mr. Connor is one of
a firm of pastoralists and merchants, and member
for Kimberley. Mrs. Connor is a tall brunette ;
she was quietly dressed in pansy-blue and was
wearing a few splendid diamonds.
That high-toned amateur actors’ club “The
Players” is capering, in a dignified way (to
caper m a dignified way is to frivol staidly like an
elderly goat that belongs to a club and has a seat
in the Upper House), over the success of one of its
members—Mr. H. H. Wallace-in recent Gilbert
and Sullivan revivals. Mr. Wallace joined Mr.
J. C. Williamson’s Comic Opera Co. a few months
since and travels with the co. to M.L. Howard
Vernon’s illness gave him a chance as Bunthorne
and be is now studying the part of the Jester in
‘ -lhe Yeomen of the Guard.” “The Players”
club gave its ex-member a swag of silver-backed
brushes, dressing bag and the like before his
departure ; and they ought to prove very handy
for the chorus girls when Mr. Wallace gets to
appreciate the rough-and-ready communism of a
theatrical tour.
Talking of theatricals. That fine-looking girl
with the beautiful red hair, Adela Kerr, who was
fcxr many years with the Bland Holt eo., died on
Friday week of pneumonia, following on some
slight brain trouble. One of the late Miss Kerr’s
sisters recently married Signor de Beaupuis.
Another is a rather noted theatrical dress-
designer in Sydney.
Mr, Thompson, one of the partners in the .pri-
vately-owned Aberdeen line of steamers, and
grandson of the founder of the line, arrives In
Sydney this week by the Miltiades. His wife is
with him, and a Melb. correspondent, comments
warmly upon the lady’s good looks and taste in
gowns. Other steamship notables and new arri-
vals are the Frank Houlders (Mr. and Mrs ) of
m, e H° ulder line. They are at the Australia.
1 he Thompsons camp with their relative, Captain
Charles Taylor, up the North Shore line, where
t. i ere are wild bees’ nests, and where you can be
lost in the bush if you go to. call on your next-door
neighbor after nightfall. It is a great place for
the young folk, the North shore line.
Prince Alfred Hospital is that worrited ! It is
all over the discovery that Professor Anderson-
Stuart’s imported pearl is not as highly-qualified
as some of the nurses on her staff. Until the year
1900 any nurse with a two years certificate was
registered ; since then a three years certificate
has been required for the highest grade. Now
the old rule which admitted to this grade with
two years certificates has been revived. Imagine
the exasperation of those who had toiled up the
Alps by the three years' route ! They are making
great efforts to have the obnoxious revival killed.
The imported head-nurse is most competent and
energetic, only her hall marks are not technically
equal to those of some who work under her. The
Professor went to London for a title and a head-
nurse. He didn’t get the title, and it’s hard lines
that now exception—even technical exception—
should be taken to the nurse.
Mr. and Mrs. F. E. Winchcombe did not leave
for Europe in one of the German mail steamers ;
but in the P. and O. steamer Mongolia.
Say “Hats” to the average woman and she
starts, alert with interest, as does the terrier when
you exclaim “Rats.” Well, all this week the
greater part of long-haired Sydney will be crowd-
ing to see the new season’s millinery. All the
shops and the various Madames are showing sweet
horticultural and avicultural master-pieces made
in London, Paris and Sydney. Picture hats,
Romney, Cavalier, and the like seem to be as stead-
fast in wear as the halo with the angel. The
Breton sailor shape is still in the front windows
and has an awful lot of trimming under
the back of the brim. One, I noticed par-
ticularly, was covered crown and brim, under
and over, with tiny, dark ruby feathers fastened
on scale-wise. There must have been many thou-
sands of them, and a garland of larger feather-
tips encircled the crown. Chiffon-velvet hats,
toques of a kind of furry cloth like beaver, with
silk roses and ripe and unripe currants and grapes,
languish to be loved—and worn. Some of the
new silk and velvet roses and poppies will remind
you of bush fires. Outer petals are of the soft
black of a charred log, then smoky-edged petals
and a fiery heart. One of the beaver-cloth toques
was shaded from baby-gum-red to the sombre
green of the mature leaf.
Regarding a paragraph in last week’s letter, I
am informed that Mrs. Arthur Weigall (Miss M.
Barnes) inherited her money from her father, the
late Mr. Henry Barnes, owner of several stations
in northern N.S.W. and Queensland.
From Paling and Co. comes a brilliant little
ballet, “ Marjorie,” with a capital reproduction of
the portrait by Longstaff on the cover. The
ballet, which is in the key of A minor, is by that
clever composer. May Summerbelle.
Another piece of music—one which raises a
smile—comes from the same firm. “ Siege, Bom-
bardment and Fall of Port Arthur” is its name.
In this fantasia for the piano you get among
other diverting movements “ the adoration of the
Icon,” and “ the Russians saluting their General,”
the. “attack by Japanese torpedo boats and the
laying of submarine mines.” Naturally there is
considerable “Agitato” about the laying of the
mines, and they are laid with the help of a fusillade
of graceful notes. One passage is a “telegram from
the Czar to hold the town at all costs.” Very
staccato. The striking of the Russian and the
raising of the Jap flags. The Jap National
Anthem and final occupation of the fort will
present no difficulties to the student. Everything
is expressed but the Japanese smile. That
mysterious thing waB not attempted by the
composer.
Writes my Melbourne correspondent:—
“ This southern capital was agog last week over
the almost sensational arrival, at the Grand, of an
English couple—Captain and Mrs. Lewis-Hill—
with a swagger retinue. When the rumor got
abroad that the dame is one of the millionairesses
of the Cold Country, the curious and casual city
diners and frilly gossips rushed the Grand Hotel
for tiffin to look on the lady coming or going to her
daily drive. Of course, the moneyed pair dined in
their own suite of rooms ; even the valet and maids
also executed their fine gastronomic feats without
an audience and with fully as much fuss as their
employers. In fact, the travelling staff live in
luxury, and helped to take up half the accommo-
dation of the Grand with their luggage and mag-
nificence.
“ The history of the Lewis-Hill match is inter-
esting. Mr. Sam Lewis made a mint of bullion
out of the London borrowing sprigs of nobility,
and he died worth some millions. After certain
thousands were doled out to the charities, the
widow (there were no children) came in for the
millions. In due course, when her expensive
weeds were shed, she married a young officer of
a Guards regiment—Captain Hill. ’Tis said she
settled £20,000 a year on her new mate. This
trip is costing thousands. On Monday the mag-
nificent party occupied most of the Loongana, en
route to Tasmania. They motor some there, then
go on to Maoriland.
“Society in the gross and several recruiting
Urdlfits mustered at Saturday’s Caulfield meeting.
L'mgV bottle-shouldered viscounts and a swarm of
aides were packed into the pen of most importance.
Most of these wore striped tweeds and walnut-
shaped boxers. The little G.-G. himself clung to
the tall beaver, but even then he was lost to sight
in an excessive body-guard. Politicians took up
lots of the Northcote time. Mr. William Knox
(just back from a gad) was one of the first to
swing his Advance Australia figure across the
path of his Ex. Toorak johnnies came out simul-
taneously in a new grey bowler—the kind of hat
a man may only balance on his cranium in his
severely sober moments.
“ilt was a Quaker-grey meeting. Miss Maud
Jeffries, Mrs. Ernest Raleigh, and Mrs. J. A.
Wallace all appeared in the color of melan-
choly. Mrs. Fred M‘Evoy—another of the
corps sported a coy specimen of ’tween-season
millinery wi th chin strap and Salvarmy inclina-
tions, in which she resembled recent pictures of
Edna May.
“Next to the visiting Viscounts (Society couldn’t
tell t’other from which), fattest interest accumu-
lated about Captain and Mrs. Lewis-Hill. The
officer has graceful length and the girls liked his
saucy straw decker flaunting the Guards’ colors.
But when they saw him swinging an air-cushion
and settling his millionairess on the stand,
feeling was Ja bit reversed. The lady, who is of
generous size, had a fortune in her raiment—all
fawn sheeny stuff and wonderful lace hangings,
but there was greater wealth in the gems she wore.
Pearls the size of small eggs studded her ears and she
wore a necklet like a grand procession of door-
knobs. As the day wore on and a chill was in the air,
the millionairess was helped into an indigo blue silk
coat with immense plain sleeves. Then on the lawn
seat, her air-cushion was spread and the stripling
spouse showed a grand command over his features,
as he placed it, and presided ceremoniously at the
seating of his lady. Every time the air-cushion
was moved the stand showed profound sympathy.
“A good-looking Father Tim taking his fizz
and oysters candidly the other noon, in a Collins-
street caf4, caused one of the smart-girl contingent
to remark pensively, ‘ Isn’t he nice ? And what
a pity we can’t marry them. I’m sure they’ve got
lots of money.’
“ The decks of the Mongolia, last week, were a
scene of confusion and crush. The Williamson-
Wallaces had the grain and wool department of
Melbourne to say good-bye (the loud pedal was
used all round). The medical fraternity bustled
about Dr. M. IJ. O’Sullivan. There were as many
priests as at an Amy Castles matinee to wave a
hand to the Essendon, East Melbourne and St.
Kilda pastors, who are off for a holiday. The
Argus office was down to offer its paw to chief
L. C. Mackinnon. Toorak had come to say good-
bye to Admiral Lord Charles Scott and his wife.
Young Rupert Ryan, with his lordly relatives,
was wished further victories at Aldershot. Every-
body was going away, except the people who had
come to see them off.
“A touriste compelled to drop her band-box in
the Holy City of Adelaide wails to me as follows :
* Oh, this Adelaide ! Imagine a city of the Com-
monwealth without one attraction on a Saturday
afternoon, and at night worse than nothing. The
people are actually waiting with the money in
their fists to book for Anderson’s pantomime. I
have roamed the town disconsolate day after day.
The shops are sleepy ; the customers drone in and
out; no one is in a hurry or thinks anything worth
a hop, step, or a jump. Out of pure spitq and
annoyance, yesterday I mounted the silly old
tram that seems to be tottering with age, and
smoked. Some women clutched each other and
moved away from me. It was horrible, but I
enjoyed the shock it gave people. Yet I had
heard that Adelaide is so wicked ! The public
takes to the parks and seems to make as little
noise as possible so as not to wake the town. The
upper 300 are never seen driving or shopping.
They are are so many oysters unless the Governor,
or the Chief Justice, or the Mayor entertains;
then they wrench themselves from their shells.
No mind can realise the awful dreariness of this
city.’
“The return of the G.-G. Northcotes to their
St. Kilda road quarters was the signal for the
same tiresome old crawl up the long drive to write
the family’s name in the Book of Fate. The
immobile Tommies doing sentry steps at the big
gates gaze on the procession with tired eyes.
Same old faces, same old crawl, same old scratch.
“ Dr. Balls-Headley has returned from foreign
parts, and his impressive dame is dispensing much
hospitality in his honor. Another interesting
medico back from a continental gad is Dr. Eccles,
who went along some time ago to see his musical
wife and daughters after their long term of piano-
studies in Berlin. The two girls have returned
with their enthusiastic dad, and mama follows
in a, few weeks. Musical people are somewhat
excited about her return. She was once the
admired of all the Conservatorium strugglers.
Two or three years of study abroad for a married
amateur who is something of a beauty puzzles the
commonplace person. An enthusiasm that isn’t
single and frumpish and pince-nez-e d is rather a
rarity.
“ One of the most inveterate theatre-gadders in
Melbourne is Mr. Porter—the Elsternwick sport
who owns Her Majesty’s Theatre and appears to
be keenly sensitive to that prosperous fact. With
fine features, the silvered thatch of an Anglican
Bishop and an Advance Australia drop chest, the
rich property-owner cuts a notable figure. There’s
a growing family of Porters, the eldest of whom,
not long ‘out,’ is a beauty—an uncommon beauty
boasting a radiant pallor, a rich red mouth, and
dark, sultry, meditative eyes.
“ Edward Morey, M.L.C., of Ballarat, is bend-
ing under a load of cares. In fact, most think he
is Bent. Hence the trouble. His ’brella and his
‘ little Mary ’ are identical with Premier Bent’s.
They have the same fat, old, bearded faces and tha
same absent-minded way of reading heavy Reform
leaders in the daily press with their specs, stranded
high on the place where their intellects ought to be.
And good-natured old M.L.O. Morey is the suf-
ferer. The man in the street casts opprobrium at
him as he waddles along. ‘ Garn, Tommy,’ he is
adjured time and again. Even the women mistake
his inoffensive smile for the smug smirk of Thomas.
All these misunderstandings happen in Melbourne.
Mr. Morey is glad to get home now and again to
Ballarat, where he isn’t erroneously upbraided for
looking like the other person.
“A globe-trotter writes this mail of Randolph
Bedford: * The Whirlwind arrived here last week,
and as soon as the fog lifted I saw him in a silk
hat and a flogger. For some days that state of
things lasted, then he became grievously ill. On
recovering he ’verted to the slouch hat and the
travel-worn sac, and was ajoying of himself very
much. Besides mines, he has a novel, an opera
libretto and other wares to dispose of.’
“The drama of the ’cello strum introduces a
novelty—a stage manageress. Miss Lena Burn-
leigh (the wife of Van) is in command of the situa-
tion. She doesn’t look it. She is excessively
feminine in speech, gesture, movement —the sort
of purry charmer who looks more the dreamer
than the doer.
‘“The Christian Israelites,’ writes a Rhine
passenger, ‘ keep us amused. Some of the women
have beards, most of them are married, and all of
them are going to North America. The men with
the hanging hair leave the poor wives there, and
go off to Jerusalem to look for the spot where
Christ was buried. Strangely enough, this
Quixotic tribe have musical tastes, and can’t be
torn from the pianos. They are funny figures on
deck when there’s any kind of a blow or sea on.
Their unkempt, unbound, and uncut hair is all
over the place.’
“There has lately developed in Melbourne a
new type of bore. He is an aggravation of the
tea-room johnnie. But whereas that mighty atom
only bothered himself and the girl who served up
the scone, the new bore inflicts a whole room. He
wants certain tunes played, and hands along a
list of ‘ classical ’ selections for the string-band to
administer. If the band knows the desired airs,
it dispenses them, and all the tea-drinkers who
had been absorbing ‘ Little Mary ’ or ‘ Egypt,’
and ordering more toasted muffin in sheer joyous-
ness, frown upon the autocrat and endeavor to
blast him with a look. But it’s no use. He is
serene in his perverted love for ‘good, sound,
classical music ’ with his cream tart and his tea.
Now, for my part, I like good music in its place,
but I don’t want to eat ice-cream to music by
Beethoven, or sweep the crumbs off my frock to a
Mozart selection. For eating-house purposes
music should be gay and light as a good piece of
puff pastry. The tea-rooms will be losing their
scone-nibblers if the ‘ good-music bore ’ is encour-
aged further.
“ When a certain squatter married his first wife
—a distinguished society beauty—he compelled
the lady to come over to his (R.C.) church for the
union. Later, when, as a widower, he conferred
himself and his dollars on a chorus chick, he was
led like a lamb to the rival rails.
“Retired Judge Casey has issued cards for a
garden party on Thursday next (4th March), at
‘lbricane,’ St. Kilda, the domicile that flies a flag
what time the festive host is entertaining a shoal
of friends beneath his roof. The flag that braves
the bottle and the breeze, so to speak.
“ Early March is to see a course of polo matches
—with a jolly Gymkhana to finish. The woolly
dames from the opulent Western district are
helping the polo carnival by a grand dance at
the Prahran Town Hall. Kooyong is having
a special feast on Friday, the 10th, when the lady
sports, or poloeens, will show their want of skill,
ponies being lent by their reckless admirers.
“ A tennis champion, who used to fascinate
with his looks the Block charmers, is now
promenading the passages of his dad-in-law’s big

“ The motor-accident is becoming a gag. The
dame who is taken out for an afternoon whirr by
hubby’s best pal, and only returns at the aft-end
of supper-time, tells a breathless and wholly un-
convincing story of a collision with a bunny on a
rural road, or an impromptu sort of spill at a
critical turning-point. Last week, a great dinner
in the Kew district had to go on without the
hostess. She arrived at the same good old time, in
the honored state of breathlessness, with the
venerable yarn of a broken-down motor.

INDIGESTION.
DR. WILLIAMS’ PINK PILLS CURE IT WITH RICH RED BLOOD.
EVERY case of Indigestion, no matter how
bad, can be cured by Dr. Williams’ Pink
Pills. Not only cured—but cured for good.
That’s a sweeping statement —and you are
quite right in demanding evidence to back it.
And it is backed by evidence in plenty—living
evidence among your own neighbors.
Ask them, and they will tell you of dozens in
your own district who have been cured by
Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills of dizziness, palpitation,
biliousness, sour stomach, sick headaches, and the
internal pains of Indigestion.
Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills cure because they
strike straight at the root of all stomach trouble.
They actually make new pure red blood. And
new blood is just what the stomach needs to set it
right and give it strength for its work. The first
box or two will give you a wonderful appetite.
“Five years ago I was a martyr to a bad
stomach,” said Mr. A. T. Porteus, 6 Napier-street,
Paddington, Sydney. “My illness started with
a thumping, throbbing pain in my head, right
between the eyes. Sometimes it nearly blinded
me. If I took a mouthful of food, a cutting pain
soon caught me at the end of the breast-bone. My
head used to ache by the hour, and every morning
I got up heavy and drowsy, with a horrible brassy
taste in my mouth.
“Of course, I couldn't eat, and I lost weight.
My clothes were like bags on me,” Mr. Porteus
went on to say. “When I stood up after lying
down, a strange feeling would come over me, and
my head would swim with dizziness. Every little
while I had a bilious attack. Just before it came
on, I would grow cold all over, and great heavy
beads of perspiration would stand out on my fore-
head. After it was over, I would shiver and
shake and feel as weak as a kitten.
“I felt so miserable and downhearted that I
didn’t care whether I lived or died. Still I went
on taking all sorts of medicines. I wasted many
a pound this way before I read about other cases
like mine, that were cured by Dr. Williams’ Pink
Pills. I made up my mind to give them a fair
trial—so I got a good supply. To my delight the
first box gave me a wonderful appetite. From
that on, every dose did me good. My stomach
gradually got back its strength. Soon I was feel-
ing as healthy and strong as any man. Now
my digestion is perfect thanks entirely to
Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills.”
Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills cured Mr. Porteus’
Indigestion because they went down to the root
of ine trouble in his blood. That is why they
never fail. Bad blood is the cause of all common-
diseases like anaemia (bloodlessness), eczema, pale-
ness, headaches, neuralgia, sciatica, rheumatism,
lumbago, general weakness, and the special ail-
ments that only women folk know. Bad blood is-
the one cause—and Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills ar®
the one cure, because they actually make new
blood. They do just one thing—but they do it
don’t bother with mere symptoms.
JLhey don’t cure for a day—they cure for good.
Do not take any pills without the full name.
‘Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills for Pale People,” on
the wrapper round the box —and remember that
they are always in boxes, never in bottles. Sold
by all chemists and storekeepers, or sent post free
by the Dr. Williams’ Medicine Co., Queen’s-
Place, Sydney 7, at 2s. 9d. a box, or six boxes for
15s. 3d. Medical advice given free.

neers, is now settled at Lancefield (Vic.) as a land

Said now, in circles that claim to be well-
informed, that Mr. George Reid will succeed Mr.
Justice O’Connor (gone to the Arbitration Court)
on the High Court Bench. Tn that case Mr.

Mrs. Eliza Stead, of Medindie (S.A.), is
over 100 years of age. Born in 1804. she remem-
bers the excitement caused by the Battle of
Waterloo.
Dr. Bevan’s last sermon in Melbourne,
before his departure on a gorgeous holiday-trip to
Europe, was on the subject, “Do I need more
than to be saved?” Well, he takes some more,
anyhow.
“Johnny” Barber, a well-known Sackville
{N.S.W.) aboriginal who died, cetat 80, in Windsor
Hospital last week, married three times, and was
the proud father of 29 full-blood aboriginal olive
branches. He was a genuine “ Hawkesbury
native.”
There is friction already over the new
appointments to the N.S.W. Education Depart-
ment. One of the authors of the recent joint
report on education may resign.
Mr. Walter Edmunds, acting temporarily
as N.S.W. District Court Judge, made a good
start when, in declining to allow the benefit of the
First Offenders Act to a man who had robbed his
trade-union, he commented : “Fora man to rob
his mates is much worse than for him to rob his
employer.”
Letters in the Sydney press complain that
N.S.W. acting Agent-General Coghlan went to
Europe in a German boat. But surely, in 1905,
the Australian has the right to travel by what
vessel he pleases ; a century ago our ancestors
certainly had to take a British Government boat
in coming to Australia—no refusals allowed—but
circumstances have since changed.
Reid’s political future is so dark that any-
thing may happen. The anti-socialist legions he
called on won’t rise, for the socialism they have
been asked to fight doesn’t exist. The capitalistic
party, pure and simple, is prepared to put up a
show of fighting, but Reid is wise enough not to
take the field with them alone. He’s in the
tightest place of his life, and a somersault to a
cushion somewhere is a natural way out.
Mr. Murray, Chief Judicial Officer of New
Guinea, is reported to be in indifferent health.
No wonder. On his first circuit he had to sen-
tence 2G Papuans to be hanged.
WRESTLER HACKENSCHMIDT, OF
RUSSIA.
Just died at Carlton (Vic.), cetat 79, W. H
Manwaring. old-time police detective. First of
London police, later attached to Scotland Yard,
he afterwards served in the Crimea, getting the
Alma medal and clasp. Reached Victoria in the
fifties, joined the detective force in ’57, and there
remained till 1880, when he retired. He was
present at shooting of bushranger Morgan, and
took an active part in chasing Power, Stanford
and other bush free-lances of the time.
Mrs. Willis, formerly an Adelaide Hospi-
tal nurse, recently travelled overland from Oodna-
datta to Port Darwin with her husband. She did
400 miles of the journey on foot, and says she
enjoyed it.
. V certain well-advertised Brisbane parson,
in years gone by, filled in his spare time o’
nights as a public park visitor with a mission.
Armed with a bull’s-eye lantern, he used
to cast a ray on the couples who sat in dark
corners. Even if they did not belong to his con-
gregation he strongly advised them to lose no
time in visiting his study with the necessary wit-
nesses. Whether he so acted for business or for
pleasure, or from high moral motives, was never
exactly determined.

Wealthy Adelaide brewer W 11. Beagle-
hole is off to London. He was a stonemason in
the early days, and, after building Kapunda rail-
way station, invested his profits in an hotel, found
the mining folk thirsty, and made a fortune.

Sydney Trades Hall will put up a marble
bust to Henry Copeland, a generous remembrance
of a staunch democrat of the days when demo-
crats were few. A bust is fitting, for Henry
tailed off in his latter days, and that tail would
not look well in the Trades Hall.
Mr. Charles Todd, who has just retired
from the S. A. post-office, after 50 years’ service,
lias got so much into the habit of turning up to
his Adelaide office at a certain time each morning
that he found himself there on each of several
days after his retirement. He then decided to go
off with his daughter on a trip to Melbourne, to
break himself of the routine.
A young N.S. YV. dentist, who had been
reckoned good enough at the business to act as
demonstrator at Sydney Dental College, some
time back hung out his shingle in a Vic. town just
over the border. After working up a decent prac-
tice, he was flattened out by the Vic. authorities.
He had been two years on the N.S.W. rolls ; but
nothing less than seven years’ N.S.W. registration
would answer. Yet the Vic. tooth-tugger can
screw up his signboard in N.S.W. on a 10-minutes
Vic. registration.
Bigjohn Forrest is having a great time
amongst his Gropers. At a meeting, the other
day, he boasted that during his 10 years’ Premier-
ship he never made a mistake, and there was not
a single administrative action he regretted. Not
one mistake ! Now, didn’t he mistake his friend’s
umbrella prod for the stab of an assassin what time
he invaded the goldfields—and was already scoot-
ing back to civilisation and safety while friend
Maitland Brown was wiring, “My God, I am
proud of you ! ”
In the Melbourne Divorce Court, Kate
Horwitz, wife of Louis Horwitz, has obtained a
divorce on the grounds of his adultery. When
Horwitz was served in gaol with the divorce
papers he took the matter quietly, and just
remarked that they were all in order, and all he
desired was the suppression of the “ lady’s name.”
The late Frank Scarr, who recently died
at Neutral Bay, was in the old days a sturdy ex-
plorer of the Northern Territory. In 1878 he
tried to cross from the Georgina to the Overland
Line on a more southern course than that followed
by Nat. Buchanan. The. season, however, was too
dry, and he had to return to Buchanan’s Creek
and make for Tennant’s Creek that way. While
trying to cross via Pituri Creek he found some of
the horses of the ill-fated Prout Brothers, who
met their deaths in the dry country west of the
Queensland border. Scarr was both a good bush-
man and surveyor, two things which are not
always found in conjunction.
Mrs. Margaret Durack, one of the mothers
of the North Queensland and North Westralian
families of pioneering Duracks, died, last week, at
North Sydney, aged 83. She came of healthy,
hearty, handsome stock, and left behind genera-
tions who are doing their utmost to justify the
Commonwealth policy of “ a white Australia.”
N.S.W. Law Almanac for this year deigns
to notice slightly the existence of the High Court
of Australia—printing the list of solicitors who
are commissioned to take affidavits in its juris-
diction. Otherwise the High Court is ignored.
And the other day Justices Darley and Owen sat
heavily on a barrister who referred to Justice
Griffith as “Chief Justice of Australia.” There
is no such office, said Mr. Darley. Certainly
Mr. Griffith is rightly “ Chief Justice of the High
Court of Australia.” But does Mr. Darley ever
object to the ellipsis which makes him “ Chief
Justice of N.S. Wales” instead of “Chief Justice
of the Supreme Court of N.S. Wales ” ?
The man with brains and doubts generally
works life in broken shifts. J. Newton-Wood,
who, with Paris Nesbit, started the Adelaide
weekly, Morning, began as a Wesleyan parson
in the Cold Country. Beginning to question the
belief he was paid to teach, he signed off and
started a local rag. He worked up in journalism
till he became a sub-editor of London Times.
Gave that up to edit the Vegetarian, London
organ of the apples-and-water crowd. An agent
of Chaffey Bros.’ Mildura boom dragged him out
to Australia, where he took up 10 acres of salt-
bush at Mildura. Lost what little he brought
out, and edited the local Irrigationist for a crust.
Got in with a group of visionaries, who wanted to
form a communistic settlement, and was appointed
leader. Founded the Nyah (originally called
Tyntynder) village settlement on the Murray,
from Swan Hill, Vic. Sold out and went to Ade-
laide, and ran the printing office attached to a
missionary training college. Eventually got into
the editorial chair of Adelaide Labor Herald,
but did not stay long. Out of the pieces of
Nesbit’s Morning he raised Century, a fort-
nightly devoted to mental science, spiritualism,
etc. He writes most of and sets and machines an
8-page paper himself, though he is over 70 years.

Mr. Henry Willis, M.H.R. for Robertson,

Attorney-General Homburg’s grab at a
seat on the Supreme Court bench of S. Australia
is something of a scandal. Had he not been an
accidental Attorney-General he would never have
been even mentioned for the place. Mr. JTomburg
was only drafted into the Jenkins Ministry a few
months ago, when Iscariot was called upon by
Darling to make room fora'couple of reactionaries.
Probably he will be painstaking enough in a dull
way ; but, even if he promised to be twice as
brilliant, that would not justify the manner of the
appointment.
The long-tlireatened retirement of Justice
Boucaut, of South Australia, came pretty suddenly
at the end. He was a vigorous man in his day,
and one of the most strenuous Ministers S.A. has
known, though there was a good deal of Bang-and-
Borrow about his locally-famous public-works
policy. These days he would have been known as
Owecaut—or Owe-Cohen. He was a good hater
of shams, and “Society” was a good hater of him,
but when he accepted a knighthood the toadies
came round a bit, though he still moved little their
way. Starting as a confirmed despiser of the
plutocrat, he wound up by being a good deal of a
plutocrat himself—married into an old-time
squatting family, and became Australia’s best-
known breeder of Arab blood-stock. The Judge,
who looked like a bushman, paddling down the
jetty in his bare feet, used to be one of the sights
of Glenelg, where he lived most of his life and was
head of the local hard-grafting yachting crowd.
Maitre Labori, who is acting as counsel
for Carrie Swain in the Gardner matrimonial
cause, summarised the case for his client before
the Paris civil court :
Mr; Gardner in 1866, when he was a theatrical manager,
married'his client; who wag then a singer known as Mrs.
Oarrie Wisler. She had been divorced from her former
husband, whose name was Swain. The record of the
marriage between Gardner and Swain has disappeared.
The pair went on a theatrical tour in Australia, where
Mrs. Gardner earned considerable sums. They invested
in gold mines, and, as the result of these speculations,
realised a large fortune. In 1901 Mr. Gardner fell in love
with a friend of his wife’s, Madame Le Glay, who had
been divorced from her husband. He then left his wife,
alleging that her marriage with him was null and void,
he having been married previously. He omitted to add
that he had been divorced from his former wife, whose
name was Anna Graham. Instead, he sent a copy of the
divorce decree which had terminated his union with
Madame Graham to Mrs. Swain, informing her that it was
really a decree dissolving her own marriag-e with him.
Mr. Gardner subsequently married Madame Le Glay in
London. On learning this Mrs. Swain sued Mr. Gardner
in the Philadelphia court, which had granted the divorce
between him and Madame Graham. She sought leave to
plead the nullity of this divorce, but the American court
rejected her claim. Mrs. Swain then brought the present
action before the Paris court, affirming the validity of her
marriage with Mr. Gardner.

Leith Napier, one of the two doctors
whom C. C. Kingston brought out to run the
Adelaide Hospital what time the local sawbones
were on strike, found himself the other day a.
victim of the Boer backwash. Napier took to
mafficking like the rest of them, only in a heavy,
sober way—took to putting on gorgeous uniform,
and capering on strange horses fed on oats.
Going down to Marino, where a contingent was-
being instructed in the ways of farm-burning,
Napier’s strange animal somehow got from under
him. For many months he wasn’t expected to-
live ; and, though he did live, he can never be the
same Napier again. For four years he wasn’t
able to make much move in the matter of a claim
for compensation ; and when he did move, he met
the backwash. The Government drove him to-
the courts, and there the S. Aus. Chief Justice
ruled that he wasn’t actually on service ; and the-
claim for £6500 collapsed.
About Joseph Banks :
Banks is the latest man to claim a statue in Sydney,
Many good things may be said of that culler of simples,
and most of them have been said, but there are other
things. If Banks had had his way there never would have
been a White Australia. The race would have been part
gaol-bird, part Polynesian, part Chinkey. He patterned
the settlement on the Dutch possessions, and was of
opinion that any number of useful inhabitants might be-
drawn from China. He backed Matra’s scheme of bring-
ing women from Otaheite and neighboring islands for the
men at the settlement. Had he adhered to the plan of
colonisation first approved by him, no convict would
have been discharged to Australian shores. That
plan contemplated the transfer to Botany Bay of
those Americans who had been on the losing
side in the war of American independence. Thou-
sands of these found it difficult to settle down-
in America at the close of the war, and were willing to-
found a new settlement in Australia, if the British Govern-
ment would assist. James Maria Matra played Moses to-
these people, and was at first supported by Banks.
But the English Government wanted to reduce the horror
of overcrowding in its gaols, and fixed on Botany Bay as-
an eligible dumping-ground and no sooner did Banks find
the humor of Government than he abandoned Matra.
and pushed ahead his convict-settlement idea. Capital
executions were horrifyingly numerous in the early years
of the settlement. They were not sufficiently numerous-
for the flower-nursing Banks. Phillip and Hunter re-
prieved many. Banks had served King on previous
occasions, and hoped to serve him still by friendly
admonitions when he became Governor over as wretched,
a lot of human beings as was ever huddled together.
His advice was against mercy, and for hanging.
As a public man Banks was to Australia good and bad A
more bad than good —and unless on the ground that worse-
men are already statued in Australia, he has no claim to-
bronze or marble existence in a public place.

Aboriginalities.
“M. 8.”: “Hamer” (8., 26/l/’O5) writes
absolute rot about snake-bitten persons mostly
dying from fright or whisky. It’s not fright or
whisky that kills bitten animals. I have seen
two dogs bitten—one by a black, the other by a
brown, snake, and each dog died within half an
hour. Also, has “Hamer” never heard of a
horse dying from snake-bite ? I should feel sorry
for the relatives of those who should follow the
advice “Hamer” (inferentially) gives—simply not
to worry and let things take their course.
* * V *
“Gil”: Agree that most snake-bitten
victims would recover if simply left to themselves
and did not worry. Knew a swaggie who, being
bitten, walked six miles to an hotel, where he
arrived in a semi-dazed condition. Some good
people gave him a little whisky and walked him
about for best part of 24 hours. The man
recovered, though nothing more was done for him.
“Alex M.”: To “Itan” (8., 16/2/’O5).
The insect inquired about is a Brachinus, or
“ Bombardier ’’-beetle. The whole family (cara-
bidae, or ground-beetle) to which it belongs have
the power of expelling an intensely acrid fluid,
which in the Bombardier’s case is so volatile that
upon contact with the air it explodes into a smoke-
like vapor. Some of them can thus fire 20 suc-
cessive “rounds.”

He was a shearer—“ Adelaide Bill ” they
called him—and he had but one eye :
“ Strike me fat! ”he exclaimed, when I asked him what
had become of the other ; “ yer not onlike the that
corsed the loss of it. ’Old on till I tell yer about it. I
cums ’ere this time two years ago, and I gits the blight
and the ‘ barcoo.’ I’d done-in me ’orses at Kynuna play-
in’ euchre for drinks, an’ I had to walk ’ere, an’ had
nuthin’ to eat but doughboys. Well, along cums a bloke,
the first day we started to ’ook ’em out of the pen, an’,
lookin’ at me, ’e says : ‘ Would you like your eyes cured ? ’
‘My oath ! ’ says I. He gives me a bottle of somethin’
like red ink, an’ on it was writ: ‘ Place 10 drops in each
eye after every meal and at bed-time. Keep the eves
wide open and blink a lot.’ I tried it on one eye, and in
three days out drops the blessed peeper. Oh, the other
one’s orlright, and I ’ope to Gord to ’ave me sight wen I
strike that cow of a doctor, as he called hisself. He giv
me a perscription afore ’e went away in case the one
bottle wasn’t enough. ’Ere it is.” I took it, and read :
“To enny chemmiss. Nine (ix) granes of strike-nin, 4
granes of armonium, dilooted and mixt together well,
after which take a corosiv suplement.” “ I ’ear,” said
Bill, in conclusion, “ that the blanker is over at Hamilton
Downs. Gord ’elp ’im wen I get a ’orse.”

“L.L.D.” : Talk about tired men ! On a
blazing hot day in a God forsaken part of Wes-
tralia I came to cross-roads, and didn’t know
which to take. Spotted an aborigine lying in
the dust, and woke him. Rising on one elbow, he
opened one eye. “ Which track goes to ? ” I
asked. He slowly sank again ; and, as he closed
his eye, he said, “ Too plurry ’ot to yabber.”

Some people in North Queensland have
become large property-owners out of the wages of
Tommy Tanna. In the old days, when our
colored brother had finished his term, he often
had from £lO to £25 to draw. This sum he
usually handed over to the storekeeper to mind
for him, and in return received a receipt (un-
dated), which he also handed to the storekeeper
to mind. There was one storekeeper who had
a sum of £9OO to mind, and he built three houses
and let them at 12s. 6d. a week each. He knew
that Tanna would only want about ss. or 10s. a
time to spend, and on an average he (the store-
keeper) only had to have about £3 or £4 a week
handy. If Tanna required anything in the way
of goods (including rum), he got the same from
the storekeeper, and it went off his money. If he
wanted a large sum to gamble with and insisted,
he was given grog (doctored), and he gave no
further trouble for 24 hours ; then it was time for
him to get back to work. The storekeeper was
a banker who gave no interest, and often annexed
about half the deposit. During the last few
years Tanna has got more sense, and the last
Government Savings Bank report showed a credit
to the niggers of £15,000.

“Jan.” : Be domestication of the wombat
(“Harry 5.,” 8., 26/l/’O5). This animal is very
subject to a filthy skin-disease, which is con-
tagious to humans, and often causes much suffer-
ing to men who have skinned or otherwise handled
the brutes. It is believed in Gippsland that the
disease may also be contracted by eating the flesh,
and I do not know of any authenticated cure.
“J.E.8.”: Be ants (B. 2/2/’O5). Darwin
(“ Origin of Species ”) says of a common European
ant (Formica refuseus) : “It is absolutely
dependent on its slaves. Without their aid the
species would certainly become extinct in a single
year. The males and fertile females do not work.
The workers and sterile females, though most
energetic and courageous in capturing slaves, do
no other work. They are incapable of making
their own nests, or of feeding their own larvae.
When the old nest is found inconvenient, and
they have to migrate, it is the slaves which deter-
mine the migration, and actually carry their
masters in their jaws.” Of another ant, Formica
sanguinea, Darwin says: “ The slaves are black
and not above half the size of their red masters.
They are household slaves. ” In Switzerland the
ant-slaves habitually work with their masters in
making the nest, and they alone open and close
the doors in the morning and evening. Their
principal work is to search for aphides, which are
to the ant what cows are to man. In a recently-
Sublished “ Romance of the Animal World,” by
Idmund Selons, we are told of ants which plant
seed, from which they harvest grass, and strip and
store grain ; and of others which grow mushrooms,
march in armies, and put wild animals to flight.
* * * *
“ Polstan ” : Hares are great fighters.
When in captivity they will scratch like demons.
Saw a young one lay a cockatoo out with a slap
on the head with its paw, recently, for giving
cheek. Also know of several cases where ferrets,
put into logs to get puss out, have had to retreat,
badly punished ; in one instance, the ferret never
came out again. Hare laid him out. Yet ferrets
can, and generally do, move foxes. By the way,
it has often been said that hares will not seek
cover. A badly-harassed hare likes a hollow log
as much as a rabbit does, and he is a terror to get
out—chopping is about the only way to do it.
* * ♦
*
A recent telegram to M.L. metropolitan
newspapers, told that the Premier, travelling
through the West Coast, had visited “The Lost
Tribes.” This strange name for a settlement was
curiously gained. Nearly 40 years ago a number
of diggers and their families crossed the coastal
ranges and disappeared from civilisation for nearly
three decades. They re-acquired many primaeval
habits, and when the fringe of spreading popula-
tion at length reached “the tribes,” the members
resented the intrusion, drew their welta robes over
their eyes, and stated something equivalent to
“git hon ! ” In present days the methods of “the
tribes” remain archaiac, and there is record in the
printed reports of a Government Railway Commis-
sion that a “Lost Tribe” witness who appeared
before the Commission knew nothing of his sur-
name. He gave evidence as plain Tom, and in the
report he was written down as “ Thomas Nemo.”
* * * *
“H.W.V.” : Re “early birds.” Magpies
often warble at midnight when there is a moon.
Have also heard them flying in the night time.
“ Ghost ” : I think shearing isn’t the
game it is cracked up to be. Looked over a
Both well (Tas.) shed recently; four shearers
at work, and in 15 minutes they shore four
sheep ! They seemed to be sharpening their
shears, and eating hard cake, and drinking
cold tea most of the time. Perhaps they had
the job by the day ? Most rememberable
features of the occasion were the girl showing
me round and the click, click, click—clack,
clack, clack of an ancient, demented-looking
wool-press at the far end of the shed. Nobody
swore, or yelled “tar,” or anything like
that; the game wasn’t as lively as counter-
jumping or Christian Endeavoring.

“Yin”: Re price upon dingoes. Com-
mon thing in New England district for a
dingo’s scalp to fetch £l5 or even £2O. I know
a case where a trapper worked for a couple of
months and eventually trapped a “special
lot,” scalp which brought £25.
* * * *
“ Itan ” : Re Australia’s “lack of in-
digenous fruit and grain ” (Bulletin,
26/l/’O5). In the scrub of the Brunswick
and Tweed Rivers, there is a wild plum
which makes excellent jam. In same dis-
tricts are two or three native limes, or
lemons, rather small, which might prove
valuable for grafting. They are scaleless and
blightless. The Botanic Gardens people in
Sydney would do good service if they paid
some attention to the preservation of the
few edible fruits which are indigenous,
because, like the blacks and the emus, they
do not “play chanies” with civilisation.
Along the river banks of the Clarence a berry
grows on a vine, known locally as the “ wild
grapes,” which is of nice flavor, but punishes
afterwards with nausea. Cultivation and
grafting would no doubt improve it.
“Big Mary”: Pooncarie (generally called
Pun-carie by the Darlings) is not “a sand-heap
about 100 miles above Wentworth, on the Dar-
ling” (“Poverty Point,” 8., 16/2/’O5). It is,
according to its leading residents, one of the best
known ports on the river. Nothing pleases the
inhabitants more than to hear the place alluded to
by visitors as Port Pooncarie. I have seen as many
as one boat tied to a tree there at a time. It is
in such desolations that the “ weird expectancy of
Outback ” is seen at its worst; for there is, on
the faces of the half-forgotten Civil servants, the
“past earin’” look of those who despair of escape
from exile. When State seivants have been,
immured in these soul-strangling remotenesses for
10 years or so, their eyes take on an uncanny
gleam, due to brooding over the apathy of their
departmental heads, who live in luxurious capitals.
God or a good Government should release many
of these prisoners of Very-Out-Back.
* * * *
“Yarrum”: When a fence was being
run along the N.S.W. border on account of the
Queensland tick, a parson, who travelled that way
with the stock inspector, “couldn’t understand
how any fence could be constructed to block so
small a creature as a tick,” and waxed very wroth
over the “ silly waste of public money.”
“ V.D.” : Concerning the speed of flies,
they may often be seen to fly in one window of a
railway carriage and out of the other and then
unconcernedly keep up with, or even shoot past
the carriage. But then they are probably helped
along by the wind made by the train.

“ Ed.” : Some greyhounds get very expert
at throwing a kangaroo. I krjow of a light little
dog that can throw the biggest of ’roos when
running. The faster the pace, the more sudden
the fall. The dog runs alongside, reaches across
and grips the off-hind-leg, and with a sudden pull
sideways the Too comes down. A forked stick,
by-the-way, is the only thing that will keep a Too
off if one attacks you. If no stick is available,
lie down ; the Too can’t then use his dangerous
hind-claws. With a stick you can t hit a Too with
a side stroke—he ducks under it; but a downward
hit will always floor him.

“ Wongin ” : A footnote in the journal
of Landsborough’s explorations gives the origin of
billabong as from “ billy,” meaning pitcher, and
“bong,” meaning empty (literally dead). This
would give to both “billy” and “bong” an
aboriginal source.

“Flabby Paul”: Know a cocky up here
(North Queensland) who has had very hard luck.
He applied for 160 acres. His
application wasgranted. Started
to work, and cleared 15 acres
of scrub, when the Government
surveyor came along and dis-
covered that the cocky was on
a State reserve. The surveyor
explained to him, on the map,
where his land was situated.
The cocky shifted, and carefully
started clearing the selection
adjoining his ovvn—the owner
of it being away. He (the
owner) turned up later (after
10 acres were cleared) and
ordered the cocky off. The
cocky refused—saying it was
his own land. He was con-
vinced after a lot of trouble, and
then he consulted a solicitor to
know whether he could get
compensation from the man
whose land he had cleared, or
whether he could get the sur-
veyor the sack, or would it be
better to sue the Government.
The solicitor told him he was very fortunate not
to be prosecuted for trespass, and took his bit
of land in payment for the information. Conse-
quently the cocky starts afresh without any en-
cumbrances.
* » * *
“Jasper”: Be “What the White can
do ” (B. 19/l/’O5). On the Lower Burdekin cane-
cutting is finished, and some of the men are grub-
bing, fencing, &c., on piece-work. For grubbing,
Is. 6d. a tree is paid, timber under 6in. not count-
ing. Very few make more at this than tucker.
Same conditions prevail in fencing-contracts, men
wearing out what few rags they had when they
came on the job, and departing nearly in the
nude. This work is to prepare the ground for
cane intended to be registered for the white labor
bonus. If men can do this work in mid-summer,
they can surely do cane-cutting, which is over
before the hot weather really commences, and, for
a sweaty job, is not to be compared with many
other kinds of work the white performs without
thinking he is doing anything out of the ordinary.
And yet the planters continue to bemoan the im-
pending loss of their darling savages, and would
stop at nothing to discourage white men from
getting a footing among them.
“ Wobbegong ” : Sydney Harbor is laying
claim to its “Pelorus Jack.” There is an old
porpoise cruising about in Port Jackson, who at
some period of his career lost the extreme part of
his tail, bitten off, presumably, by a shark. How-
ever, every time he comes to the surface, prepara-
tory to diving again, he gives the water a tremen-
dous smack with his stump-ended
tail, making a sound like the
beating of a carpet. This seems
to give the old fellow no little
satisfaction, as he doe 3 it with
great vim. He does not con-
sort with the other porpoises,
but follows at a respectful
distance, as if, in losing his
tail, he had lost caste, and
had the deformity line
drawn against him.

“W. J. S.” :
There is a large,
sooty-grey moth
which at night
takes possession
(after flying
about all day) of
wilga, native-
orange, and
other whitewood
trees. It builds nests,
not unlike a “chignon”
in shape and structure, in
the branches, well out of the
reach of predatory enemies.
Often when, after walking a long, hot
stage, I have thrown myself down for the night,
after lighting a fire under one of these leafy
trees, these moths would start to swoop down on
my fire in scores. No circling round the flames,
but deliberate suicide. In the morning my camp
would look like a battlefield, with dead and
wounded lying all round, and giving forth a
sickening smell.
* * * *
“Walg” : Have recently been obliged to
use “bore” water, and I only wish the gentle-
man who wrote about “ drinking artesian water
from a thousand feet below ” was condemned to
“ use no other ” for six months or so. His enthu-
siasm would be effectually slain. The water
carries about 20 per cent, of soda, and wherever it
passes it leaves a thick white coat of the deposit.
It kills vegetables, corrodes iron pipes, and makes
the unfortunate drinker thereof look like a ring-
barked sapling.
* * * *
They are spelling kangaroo in different
ways in some of the English periodicals. ‘'Kan-
garoo ” receives favor. That is Captain Cook’s
way, and he said he had the word from the natives.
Tt is obvious, though, he hadn’t the spelling from
them. And aborigines in general d on ’t know the
beast as kangaroo or anything similar. “Patago-
rang” was a common native name, and “baggary”
another. Governor Phillip wrote it “kangurroo,”
but he mis-spelt common English words. G. B.
Barton’s notes on the word leave the spelling
doubtful.
FIGHTING THE HEAT.
Another hot spell prophesied. Preparations up

“A.P.R.”: Somebody asks, why don’t
Australians “siest” (new verb)? In towns like
Coonamble and Walgett, where the mercury
remains at over 100° for weeks, it seems absurd to
do the chief business in sweltering afternoons,
instead of, say, 7 p.m. (Memo. —Our midsummer
daylight lasts 15 hours.) The mid-day camp is
popular with all bushmen, especially drovers, who
siest three or four hours; jackeroos have tried
hard to inaugurate the siesta, but the usual result
of the boss finding Hawhaw, Esq., under a shady
tree at 11 a.m, is that the latter is next day seen
“ taking his hook. ” # #
“ Whaffor” : Witnessed a native “ burial ”
recently in Kimberley (W. A.). After cutting the
body and smearing their own bodies with the blood,
the mourners placed the corpse in the boughs of a
tree. Then a painted, howling crowd danced
round the trunk. They gashed themselves with
sharp stone and shells, and threw themselves
violently on the ground.

9/2/ , 0o) concerning the indifference of Horsham
(Vic.) farmers to scientific agriculture as con-
trasted with those of Nhill, in similar dis-
trict. One reason is the idiotic way in
which Longerenong Agricultural College
(eight miles from Horsham) was managed.
Writer came from the Cold Country with
the idea of going on the Australian land ;
thought to gain a few ideas at an
agricultural college, and by
ill-luck chose Longerenong.
Proved to be a hotbed of
political interference, crass
ignorance of farming con-
ditions, and inseparable
red-tape. The princi-
pal, to demonstrate
the value of rape
as a crop to fat-
ten lambs for early
market, sowed
some, and got a
small mob of lambs
ready for sale in a
very short time, but
was kept waiting six
weeks forpermission
to sell. Result, local
ridicule. Later, tiro
college was closed
“on account of the
drought,” although, by
surface cultivation
only, ninety-day maize
was brought to per-
fection, and peaches
lOoz. weight were
gathered from trees
which had neither rain
nor water for five clear
months. With the claims
and results of science thus
flouted by the authorities, no wonder the Horsham
farmers follow suit.
* * * •
“Janor”: In drawing the bark-roof,
The Bulletin artists are always wrong about
the logs which “anchor” the roof—known as
riders ’ and “jockeys.” The first-named are
perpendicular, in pairs ; and the ends overlapping
the ridge are loosely held together with wooden
pins, to allow their spread to conform to the roof-
angle and lie close to the bark when the weight of
the jockey coineson their loose ends—those nearest
the eaves. The jockeys are laid horizontally across
these lower ends, and are held in position by
wooden pegs driven into the underlying rider.
But the artist always draws the rider on top of the
jockey, “ which is absurd,” as the geometry-books
say.

belief that the awful disease follows the eating of
Muscovy flesh is general amongst the natives of
Indui. _ Anybody know on what grounds the
belief is based ?

“Alex. M.”: To “ Nymsie ” (8., 9/2/’O5).
There was never any serious question about the
hearing powers of fish. With the single excep-
tion of Amphioxus (the lancelet)—the lowest form
of vertebrate—all fishes have well-devolop ;d
auditory organs.
LIGHT FROM ISRAEL.
INTERVIEWER: “From your experience of the
passage of the Red Sea, sir, do you think that
colored laborers are necessary in those waters! ”
MOSES : Well, the colored element certainly did
expedite our trip through, but I did not deem
it judicious to take it on with us.”
Port Darwin district claims to have the
most remarkable ants’ nests in the world. They
are long and narrow, and every nest is built in a
due north ;vnd south direction—why, no one seems
to know. They are known as “magnetic nests,”
any one of them being an infallible compass to
travellers.

“Eel.”: “ W.E.’a ” self-closing gate (8.,
9/2/’O5) is no novelty, but this is : A sapling with
a heavy root was fastened on a gate-post by a bolt
sft. from the ground, the root on one side of the
post, sapling on the other. The whole moved
round this bolt, the bolt-hole being 3ft. from the
foot end. Hanging downwards from the sapling
was light lattice-work. A cord, with a handle in
a conyenient position, released the spring-catch,
and with the same movement the gate was jerked
upwards. There was ample time for a buggy to
pass through before the gate descended slowly
back to closed position.
* * * *
“ Buzz ”: He the rough-and-ready methods
of the blacks. I once rescued a young lubra (while
bathing in Wide Bay) from a shark. She got away
with her foot, bitten clean off at the ankle. After
I got the girl ashore (not a white man within
1G miles), the blacks made a paste of mangrove
ashes and clay and plastered it on to the stump.
The wound was thereafter left so for 12 days, and
was then re-covered with the same stuff. It was
never washed. In less than two months the
girl was going about with the aid of a stick, and
never seemed to suffer much discomfort or pain
after the first week.
• * * *
“War E”: “ Nardoo ” (B. 26/l/’O5) de-
fends phosphorus and maintains that it is keeping
the rabbits in check. It is doing nothing of the
kind ; despite the 2000 distributors at work in
N.S.W., bunny is more numerous to-day than 10
years ago, and the fact that there is such ah out-
cry for more drastic means of checking the pest
(there’s no such word as ‘ ‘ eradicating ’’ when speak-
ing of bunny) isn’t too good an “ad” for phos-
phorus. My experience, extending over many
years, is decidedly in favor of poisoned water, it
makes short work of the rabbits. There is, I
admit, much damage dona to natural fauna by its
use, but in the hands of a careful man far less than
can be laid to the account of phosphorus.

“ Hodge ” : “ War E.” (B. 26/l/’O5)
asserts that sheep will, if left to themselves,
escape from a bush fire ; and that the boundary-
rider ought not to interfere with them in such
circumstances. Well, my experience is that sheep
will run before a bush fire until—if not previously
overtaken by it —brought up by a fence, which, if
left to themselves, they will follow to a Corner,
where they will remain till roasted to death.
Pure nonsense to allege that only the outside
sheep will be scorched. The fire would run
through, under, and over them. As if greasy
wool wouldn’t burn !

“Ess”: The beetle industry in Queens-
land provides pocket-money for a host of people-
including hard-up tramps—during the cane season.
In one month recently the Cairns Beetle Board
paid for two tons of borers at Is. per pound—now
dropped to Gd. per pound. When the bonus for
beetle-scalps was started a few seasons back, the
insects were counted by the receivers, but the col-
lectors brought them in such vast numbers that the
expedient of paying by weight was adopted. Then
the nippers soaked the catch over nignt, and the
increase of expenditure on beetle-destruction was
so marked that soon the old method of counting
was reverted to.

“ Alone ” : Many dogs in Vic. have
become chronic fruit - eaters. Seem to have
acquired the habit through poking about in vine-
yards in the grape season ; and now they have to
be counted among numerous pests of the grape-
grower. I myself have a collie that waits philo-
sophically under his favorite pear tree till the
wind blows the ripe fruit down to him. [Dogs
are great vineyard depredators in the claret-
making districts of France.—Ed. B.]
ANSWERS to CORRESPONDENTS.
No notice (except of a hostile character) will be taken of
communications insufficiently stamped. Name and
address must appear on MS. No liability is under-
, taken in any case re contributions voluntarily sub-
mitted, whether sent by post or handed in ; and in
no case will MS. be returned unless stamps (of any
State) accompany.
Vulcan: The United States are by no means
“ unified ”; their Federal Constitution leaves more to the
States and less to the Federation than that of Australia.. .
Tit-for-Tat: But it’s a bad time to start making friends
when the storm bursts. Make your friends in fair
weather ; test them in f0u1... .Curlew : Alas, yes we do
import lemons in the off-season—mainly because the
Australian fruit-grower won’t grow late thin-skinned
varieties, or take the trouble to store lemons for
keeping. Australia imports poultry, eggs, cake, jam,
jelly, fruit and other trifles as well as machinery.

TO ARTISTIC (AND INARTISTIC) CONTRIBUTORS.
Harold G.: Very crude as yet; hardly possible to sav
how you would develop with tuition.
A photo, of Jap. General Nogi and staff
at table is going the rounds of pro-Monkey sheets
(Australian and other) at present. It shows
fodder in plenty, palatable drink in abundance,
but not the least sign of knives or forks. Nippon’s
“ New Civilisation ” hasn’t yet climbed up to the
fork level.
A Melb. lift-expert got something of a
shock, the other day. After effecting repairs in a
fashionable hotel he sent the cage to the top of
the shaft, and almost immediately pulled the rope
to bring it down again. His hair nearly turned
grey when two ladies stepped out as the lift
reached the ground-floor. Melbourne is the one
civilised city in the world where it is quite usual
to leave a lift without an attendant, to be worked
by whomsoever listeth.
James Edmond from Bombay :
Asia has no “ weird expectancy ”as Australia has. It
doesn’t expect anything except to remain the same old
Asia. It is waiting all the time, but for nothing in
particular. An immense proportion of Ceylon is still
jungle, and an immense proportion of the people stand
around so that you would almost expect jungle to grow
on them. Everywhere you meet Asia walking six miles
in his bare feet, and carrying £lb. of something in his
hand ; or he will stand three-ciuarters-of-an-liour urging
you to buy some trash for a rupee, or five cents, or some-
thing equally ridiculous ; or you will see four of him
cutting one square foot of grass ; or eight of him
hollering to one bullock about the sine of a cock-
roach, or three of him cackling wildly to three more
to come and help them to lift a box that an Aus-
tralian lumper would pick up with one hand.
There are about 180 servants in a big hotel —one waiting
at each table, and one hanging about at almost every
door in case he should be wanted, and one on each side of
the front door, and two on the steps, and 50 buzzing
around generally, doing nothing visible to the naked eye.
There are brown men in the streets who want a com-
mission for helping you into the shop into which you
would have gone in any case. If you are going into
a Colombo tobacconist’s, some irresponsible brown per-
son fastens on you outside, says “Good cigars, master,”
buzzes in with you, helps you to call" out to the
tobacconist, gives you his moral support during the trans-
action, and—doesn’t ask for any money. lie only walks
alongside you afterwai Is till you give him some. At a
railway station about eight men struggle for your bag',
and four more holler hr a rickshaw for you. At Mount
Lavinia hotel, near Colombo, a native camped outside the
hotel verandah with his eye on me for three hours. When
at last I came off the verandah I found that he wanted
25 cents to show me a house where a Boer captain lived
when a prisoner in Ceylon. Three more natives dropped
from the skies to help him show me the house. 1 found
the first one was an official guide, so I gave him 25 cents
to go away. . The other three wouldn’t depart, so I
walked back into Colombo—about eight miles—to tire
them out. They gave up after about three miles. There
is an immensity of work waiting to be done here, but
Asia is so busy buzzing round trying to sell you a toy
elephant half an inch long that it hasn’t time to do the
work. It hasn’t even the least idea that the work needs
doing. The Cingalese laborer makes about 33 cents (say
6d., or a third of a rupee) daily, and he isn’t worth more.
So far, in short, Asia strikes me as the ant which is
always carrying the leg of a dead grasshopper up one side
of a stone and down the other.
Concerning a matter of little consequence :
A while ago, Dill Macky’s Botciiman, out after sectarian
strife, pulled a R.O. temperance preacher into its mischief-
making, and said that most of the Bung business was in
the hands of the Mickies. The Bulletin, which doesn’t
care one empty beer-bottle more for the Micky than the
Macky, but does want to keep sectarian bitterness out of
Australia, told the little mischievous insect to look
after the beer in its own eye and let the whisky in
the other fellow’s alone ; and it remarked, to prove
that there was considerable beer in that eye, that
in the Bung and Narcotic business (which it was
mentioned couldn’t be separated) there were something
over 800 Methodists employed in N.S. Wales, The
Methodists were chosen for example because they
happen to be the sect which puts about the heaviest foot
down on the Curse ; yet in spite of that, there was this
large amount of “ beer and bacca ” in their eye, requ : ring
attention. Thereupon parsons have
thrown about large quantities of angry denial, and wan t
proof. The Spectator wants the names of 10 persons
“ responsibly engaged in the hotel or liquor business who
are members of the Methodist Church.” But The
Bulletin isn’t going to be limited like that. It doesn’t
know who are T< members ” of the Methodist Church ; it
only knows those who described themselves as Methodists
in the Census papers, and who said they dealt in or handled
liquors or narcotics. Of the population engaged in or
manufacturing stimulants and dealing in and manufac-
turing narcotics tha Census office says there are about 200
Methodists ; the exact figures cannot be got out, but that
is the estimated maximum. Of those keeping and
working in hotels there are 500 —estimated in the same
way. In addition to these there are persons engaged in
clubs, in caf6s supplying liquor, and a large number in
groceries which hold bottle licenses, besides vignerons.
The number of Methodists engaged in grocery stores, and
also there, handle liquor, cannot be definitely arrived at,
but is estimated to be greater than the number of those
who are employed in hotels. So the total of those in the
“ beer and tobacco ” business is greater than was first men-
tioned by The Bulletin, in spite of the whirling words of
the parsons who characteristically said it was untrue first
and asked for proof afterwards. And even then there
haven’t been |included the names of the very big Method-
ists who are the landlords of pubs and and wine and spirit
stores. Would the Spectator like some of those names
for a start —or is It sinful to sell whisky, but not sinful to
take some of the proceeds from the man who sells it, for
providing him with a place to sell it in?
IDENTIFYING BEER.
Melbourne, Sunday.—How is beer to be identified?
riiis question has arisen in a purely legal sense. The
Chief Commissioner of Police points out that it is a
question of fact for magistrates to decide, and even if it
should be held necessary for a constable to taste beer in
order to identify it—well, the police could even meet that
difficulty. —News Item.

• LEADER OF MERCHANT TAILORS.
R. C. HAOON’S ENTERPRISE.
THERE is, at least, one establishment in
Sydney that never has time to do anything
but look ahead, go ahead, and keep ahead.
It has never been allowed a “ tired feel-
ing ” or a timorous inter-
val to pause and reflect on
past achievements. It
knows that in order to be
first favorite it must not
rely on a reputation, but
must keep that reputa-
tion all the time and
right up to the living
present. That establish-
ment is R. 0. Hagon’s,
12!) King-street, Sydney,
l't is just 20 years since
Mr. Hagon launched his
enterprise with one
cutter and 20 employees
who produced 100 gar-
ments a week. How
great has been the ex-
pansion is shown by to-
day’s figures : There are
now five cutters and 150
employees—some of the
hands dating their con-
nection with the house
since its inception, a
score of years ago —and
the garments, (>OO weekly.
This represents the
largest turn-over of any
tailoring establishment
in the States.
It will interest the
public to know that every
piece of material, before
being made into a
garment, is shrunk
on the new London
principle, and inspection
of the process is invited
at the buildings, which
are illumined throughout
with electric light.
It has been Mr. Hagon’s
aim to give his patrons
the beat of material, the cleverest of cuts, and the
most perfect of finishes at the lowest of rates
compatible with quality and skill- the essentials
to success in a discriminating Commonwealth.
A characteristic of Australians is their demand

Business, Robbery, etc.
Dookie Agricultural College (Vic.) is conducting
useful hybridising experiments in regard to wheat and
oats and barley. Principal Pye has over 150 plots, hand-
sown, with different grains. The grains are hybridised
carefully by hand, and all details of each experiment duly
noted. The outcome is quaint, just as the life of a wheat-
grain is interesting ; for of all Nature’s gifts wheat is one
of the few self-inoculating. In some rows there are not
two heads to be found alike—some stalks holding heads a
foot long, others but a few inches. Some grains are
covered with heavy, coarse husks ; others the reverse.
Over fifty beardless types of barley are under observation,
and oats receive due attention also. To protect these
valuable plots the sparrow has to be catered for, and a
large plot of barley has been planted handy to keep his
attentions off the experimental area. Next to the
sparrow pest, principal Pye classes the petticoat searcher
after knowledge, and gives an example. A lady of a
visiting party pulled up a bunch of wheat growing handy,
and asked innocently what he called that? Without
moving a muscle, Pye replied that he called it six years of
hard research thrown away ! It was his pet bunch of
hybridised bread-stuff. All these experiments are being
carried out under any unfavorable condition that may
present itself to the mind of the experimenter, that the
types may be evolved to meet the peculiarities of climate
wherever they may be adopted eventually.
* • * *
“ Flabby Paul ”: Struck the meanest firm. It
has branches and agencies all over the place. A little
widow vainly tried to make a living in a hotel “ tied ” to
the firm. She was unsuccessful, and ended up about £lOO
in debt. The firm, in an overwhelming rush of gene-
rosity, gave her daughter (a girl of 17) a billet in its
drapery department, at the wage of 7s. 6d. a week, on
condition that 2s. fid. a week be deducted towards the
liquidation of the mother’s debt.
• * * *
“Jack Shay” : Remember taking part in a rob-
bery—at least, another man called it a robbery. Swagging
it once, struck a N.S.W. country town on the annual race
day. Being stiff, mate and self left our money on the
fence, and went into the races—the man watching the
fence was otherwise engaged and didn’t see us. Put in
he day, knocked about the town at night, and camped
on the course. When day broke we set to work and
rooted round the “ring” and the bough-shed
booth; result, half-a-sovereign and 4s. 6d. in silver.
Met the caretaker of the course as we were
leaving, and told him we had camped under
the stand. He rode over to the stand, but ten
minutes after caught up to us on the road. “Watcher
been doing over there?” he yelled. “Only camping,”
we answered. “ D’yer pick any money up ? ” “ Money ! ”
—our astonishment was sublime. “ Yes, yes, yous
dashed robbers ! Give it to me, or I’ll put the police on
yer." We advised him to try jumping at himself, or to
get hold of himself by one ear and throw himself at him-
self. How he did foam and roar ! The sergeant of
police came along in time to join in. We admitted we
had money—what was left of our last wages. The
sergeant shook his head. “ Can’t do anything, Bill,” he
said, and rode off. Bill followed us for about a mile out
of town, and from saying, “ Yes, yous did, you blanky
robbers,” he came down at last to asking us what we
thought of ourselves, and how we expected a poor man to
live. We advised him to get work, and live honestly, and
give up trying to cadge from two poor but honorable lads
swagging it in search of graft. That settled him.
* * * »
“Drifter” tells how he didn’t have a drink:
Went into a wine bar t’other night and called for half-a
pint of claret. An unsavory-looking woman searched for
the mixture among bottles on a shelf, and not finding it,
told me in thick, greasy accents she would get some from
“on draught.” Then she dived beneath the bar. Seized
with curiosity, I slipped round unobserved to watch the
process. First she sorted out a length of gutta percha
tubing from amongst the dead marines, old cigarette
boxes, and other rubbish. Then, after inserting an end
of the tube through a hole in the top of a cask, she held
the wine glass close down, put the tube in her mouth,
and gave it a suck. The mouthful she received she spat
out over the cigarette boxes. Then, with the stream of
wine coming down this rough syphon, she filled my glass.
I fled.
* * * *
“ Stev ” : It was a few years ago, when Honolulu
was experimenting with a view to establishing an export
trade in cucumbers to Barcelona. The other fellow was a
carpenter, who followed shipping work—building horse-
stalls, fitting up cold chambers and freezing compart-
ments. He may have lied, but this is what he told me
“ We worked day and night, and fitted up one of the holds
as a cold chamber for a trial shipment of asparagus the
Yokohama Government was sending to Kamstchatka. We
finished, and all the men but myself went home. I had
to stand by. The Government inspector was to pass the
chamber, and, if he required any little alteration, I was to
do it. He came aboard, and adjourned to the saloon for
an hour before he inspected. He passed everything as
0.K., and retreated to the saloon again. When he went
ashore, the lamp-trimmer or some other officer came to
me and said, ‘ Get too, and pull all that flaming lumber
out of the way ! ’ With the assistance of some of the crew,
it was down and stacked away in one-twentieth of the
time it took a dozen carpenters to build it. As soon as it
was cleared the lumpers rushed in the cases of tomatoes
and stacked them up as if the contents were blue-metal.
I afterwards saw that those horse-radishes arrived in Peru
in a rotten condition ; and that the Government of
Sumatra had abandoned its attempt to help its fanners by
proving that strawberries could be successfully exported
to Devonshire.

“ T.D.” : Even some of the steamer companies
trading Englandwards via Chow Africa have lately deve-
loped “ derry ” on the Australian : passengers going right
through notice a marked difference between the treat-
ment from Sydney to the Cape and from Capetown on-
wards. For the first half of the \ oyage poor provisions
are used, the water is bad, bread is made from sour flour,
and dinner runs the limited gamut of sea pie, Irish stew,
potato pie, and hot-pot, which all turn-out to be one and
the same thing. But after passing Capetown the Sydney-
side poultry and the Victorian rabbit and the Tasmanian
apple are allowed to walk out of the refrigerator much
more frequently, and the sour flour is stowed away for
the next lot of Australian passengers.

“G.H.” : Saw three alleged Stradivarius violins
advertised, the other day, at prices ranging from 30s. to
555. One advt. spelt it “ Stradivarious ” ; and it was pro-
bably right. A genuine Strad. costs about as much as a
brick house; and it is doubtful if there are more than
one or two of them in Australia altogether. Judging by
the wanted-to-sell advts., one would think that Mr.
Stradivarius chopped his violins out with an axe at the
rate of one a minute.
* * * *
Protectionist Maoriland’s latest is the dried-
milk industry. First factory for its manufacture was
opened near Palmerston recently. Europe has several
factories where the stuff is run as a by-product on a small
scale ; but M.L. is the first to go the whole hog with the
new product. Process is simple enough : After test, the
milk is filtered, then sent to the drying room, where it is
run over heated rollers till only the solids are left in
large, crinkled cream sheets. This is taken to a sifting
room, sifted at the rate of a ton per hour, and then
packed for sale. Packing is done by hand at present, but
an automatic packer, with a 12,000-tins-per-day capacity,
is on its way from America. Already the local demand
for the new product is strong, and the enterprising
initiators expect to knock out “ condensed milk ” forth-
with m the local market at least. Well, you ten-times-
as-big, half-Protected Australia?
