Just published in the U.S.A., an Authors’ Year Book,
giving hints on how to live by writing. The hints are
mostly good, and applicable to Australia: Authors
should read assiduously the mags, and newspapers to
which they expect to contribute. The “timely” article
is a good dish when served hot; what will be rushed this
week will be shunted next month. The taste for serials
is fading, and for verse increasing. Type-write
clearly your MS. Never address a MS. 'to any
person by name. One well-known author’s advice is
“ When I have a manuscript to offer, I offer it in the
usual way, without even an accompanying note, unless it
is something timely, requiring immediate decision. Nor
lias my acquaintance the slightest influence upon its for-
tune. Indeed, I sometimes think that mjr warmest edi-
torial friends have taken the keenest pleasure in turning
me down. They might, perhaps, have had some delicacy
in the matter had I been a stranger. As it is, they have
not hesitated to tell me that a certain poem or story was
‘ pretty bad stuff.’ ”

E. W. Carey, just now mentioned in Melbourne,
had several jaunts along the inky way With Labor-
ite Prendergast and some Bohemian artists and
writers, he ran the Boomerang, a little Labor
paper started in Premier Patterson’s time. But
the Puritanical sensibilities of Carey and Prendergast
caused friction with the sta'f, which culminated in a
deliberately strong leader on Dean Macartney, entitled,
“ The Dear Dead Dean,” being served up for censorship.
M’Kain, later of Adelaide Critic, was editor, and passed
the article. Carey forbade its insertion. In the row that
followed, Carey, Prendergast and the comps withdrew.
Other comps were got to bring out the paper for the
staff, but the scheme fell through, and the Boomerang
fell to earth. Carey was also in with H. H. Champion in
the Champion ; but once again Carey’s prudery irritated
the writers. Mixed up with the concern were a flock of
“ new ” women, all clamoring for “an equal standard of
morality.” One writer slyly slipped into the paper that
what they were really after was “ an equal standard of
immorality.”. Carey and his chickens were furious,
the writer got slops, and the Champion also went bung.
Finally a quite different kind of Carey, a Carey who had
quarrelled with Champion—a Carey without prudery, and
with liberal purse—ran the Outpost entirely on his own.
The writers and artists with whom formerly he had
quarrelled. were enrolled on his staff, and brought out
what was, at its best, a really smart little paper. It was
the only Melbourne journal which persistently and
thoroughly championed Marshall Hall against Fitchett
and his muckrakes. But the Outpost, too, went bung ;
and then Carey decided to stick to his building society.

The irony of the joke entered the imprisoned pressman’s
soul when the gaoler remarked, as he turned the key,
“ You fellows have a soft snap, getting into everything
free.”
* * * *
Randolph Bedford for a while edited Korumburra
(Gippsland) Southern Mail. The monotonous lying
which constitutes country journalism got on his nerves,
and he broke out into occasional sprees of candor. Once
a church concert didn’t advertise with the Mail. Bed-
ford wnt and paid his fare in, which was ominous. Wet
night, poor house, and performers (imported from Melb.)
didn’t care a continental. R.B. reported that concert-
discussed it as nuisances are seldom discussed. The
several oldest inhabitants still speak in subdued accents
of the shock of that excursion of trutnful reporting into
the soapy realms of country-town journalism. Bedford
left soon afterwards.

“Muru” from Maoriland:—Met the editor of The
Bulletin in a bush pub. t’other day. Gazed with reve-
rence upon the unclassical brow and luminous nose of
this arbiter of contributors’ destinies. He was getting
outside a mug of beer at the time. “ They don’t serve
it in buckets ’ere,” he explained, apologetically, noting
my deferential look of inquiry. “Y’ may ’a’ noticed the
paper ain’t been up to standard the las’ few weeks,” he
continued, between acts of suction. “I’m travellin’ fer
me ’ealth, see; and a-eollectin’ hinformation about the
great natural resources of this here glorious country.”
He went on to tell me a number of remarkable secrets
about the paper. “ Come to think of it,” he remarked,
“I ’as most trouble with ther picture blokes. Artists is
that cantankerous. W’y, Norman Lindsay’s wrecked the
premises frequent; an’ McCrae’s bin known to chase the
’ole staff down the street with a gun w’en ’e wasn’t
satisfied with the way ’is picter came out. An’ ’Op !
Anybody ’as seen ’Op’s drorin’ would n’t ’a’ dreamt ’e was
brought up fer a missionary—but he was ” (in a fierce
whisper). “ I alone know the dark secret. Yes ; we
gives George Reid a bad time of it; but, lorbless.ver, ’e
don’t mind. Keeps 'im in the public hye, ’e tells me,
confidential-like. You wouldn’t ’a’ thort me an’George
was cousins. No ; nor anybody else. Fact is, me an’
George orfen ’as a beer together—an’ Reid, ’e never lets
me shout. Yes ;I do n’t mind if I do.”
* * * *
“ R.” : Conscience-stricken, I herewith confess my
transgressions as “ local correspondent ” of a Sydney daily.
For some time the journal had sullenly refused to print
my wires stating that “ the crops are looking well.”
Lucrative murders were scarce, and my daily income of
sd. receded to zero. In despsir, I sent in a lying descrip-
tion of a “ cross between kangaroo-grass and wheat, per-
fected by a local farmer.” This earned me 2s. ; then,
launched on the ocean of mendacity, I proved that the
drought was ending, because the local divining-rods were
all pointing skywards. Imaginary fires and thunder-
storms followed in quick succession ; and a local man,
nick-named “Tiger,” having visited a neighboring town,
I wired that a tiger had been seen near that place. This
was reprinted throughout N.S.W. and gained great kudos
for the local rum ; even The Bulletin commented upon
it. Fearing that the ill-gotten riches thus amassed
would bring me ill luck, I resigned, to put myself out of
temptation.
* * » *
“ Stev.”: There is one phase of modern journalism which
calls for censure—the columns of stuff which appear with
scare heads whenever a new discovery in chemistry or phy-
sics is made. “Consumption Conquered,” “A Cure for
Cancer,” “ Hope for the Blind,” and so forth run the head-
ings and cross-headings. Then follows a slab of matter,
more or less imaginary or prophetic, based sometimes on
scientists’ incautious talk, sometimes on a fool’s maunder-
ings. I wonder do the ghouls who print this stuff ever give
a thought to the vain hopes they raise in the minds of the
afflicted. I know of two cancer victims, practically con-
demned to death, and resigned to their fate, whom the
booming of the “ molasses cure ” spurred to fresh fights
for life. Cruel disappointment ensued, and both are since
dead. They would have died anyhow, but in peace.
“ J.P.” : How pars travel. Know a par, written by a
journalist on a Maoriland daily, concerning the frequency
with which vessels whose names bear the initial “ T ” go to
pieces on the Maoriland coast. It was originally published
in Thb Bulletin, was copied (unacknowledged, of course)
by London Daily News, and was then printed (acknow-
ledged to latter) in the opposition paper two streets from
where the author had written it. That par travelled
32,000 miles to get 600 yards.

Adelaide J’s.P. talk of a new journal expressly for the

Melb. morning papers agree to agree on many matters
in the arrangement of their sheets, but they persist In
differing about others. The Age always spells it Lilly-
dale, the Argus “ Lilydale ” (David Syme ought to know ;
he owns a good slice of that part of Victoria). Some
other regular differences are “ Thibet ” (Argus) and
“ Tibet ” (Age) .; “ Lhassa ” (Argus) and “Lhasa” (Age);
“ Peking ” (Argus) and “ Pekin ” (Age).

English papers announce the death of W. L. Lewis, pro-
prietor of the Suffolk and Essex Free Press. In the
middle fifties he was a member of the reporting staff of
Melbourne Argus ; later on he started the Ararat Adver-
tiser, and returned to the Cold Country in ’62.
A SCRIBE’S LAMENT.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
There is nothing so hollow as pens,
There is nothing so gloomy as ink,
When a writer’s obliged to think of something,
And doesn’t know what to think.
There is nothing so blank as paper,
There is nothing so void as a brain,
W.ien a scribe has an hour to think up a thought,
And has thunk for an hour in vain.
I know how a ghost must feel,
As he tries with his fingers of air
To convey a jugful of foaming beer
To a mouth that isn’t there.
VIC.

POLITICAL
POINTS
The best immigrant Australia can have is
the little Australian.
A policy for the Commonwealth in a nut-
shell—Down with the fences, up with the factories.
The politician who demands that the
country should be “run on business principles”
sometimes means for his business principals.
Sydney’s Lord Mayor, Allen Taylor, has
formed his timber business into a limited liability
oompany, which can now sell timber to whom it-
pleases, corporation contractors or not.
Who got the commission that Mr. John
See says was so liberally bestowed over the pur-
chase of Myall Creek station by the N.S.W.
Government? It is alleged that one of Mr.
See’s own colleagues got a share.
What a fearful hullabooloo the dailies would
have made about “secret caucuses,” “freedom of
conscience, ” “ the right of the people to know what
their representatives are doing,” and so forth, had
J. 0. Watson been running the Hobart Confer-
ences !
Icicle Irvine isn’t going to have any
absurd walk-over in his Wimmera electorate.
Solly, Labor man, has been up there organising,
and striking unexpected success.
A carriage tax, a footman tax, and an
armorial bearings tax are levied in England. If
levied in Australia they would help the Treasury
a little, and hurt no one who doesn’t deserve to be
hurt.
Encyclical Kelly has resigned his mem-
bership of the Ashfield branch of the N.S.W.
P.L.L. He and Mugwump, of the L.0.L.,
should now form a Cave of Adullam for discon-
tented Laborites.

WINE SHOP ITEM.
LIDY : “Ho! don’t serve no tidies, don’t per?
Wait till I see Billy Maloney. He'll reform
yer.”
Postmaster-General Smith points out for
the edification of the anti-Australian press that
while perishable produce is carried on the Orient
Co.’s boats for £7 a ton, it asks, on the basis of
£150,000 (its first tender), £3OOO a ton for carrying
mail matter.
The agreement between N.S.W. and Vic-
torian Railway Commissioners as to the Riverina
trade ends a long-standing rivalry. As far back
as the days when John Robertson was Premier
the N.S.W. Government was heckled “because
Victoria was pushing her railways to the Murray
faster than N.S. Wales, and would secure the
Riverina trade.” Exasperated at the heckling one
night, Robertson jumped to his feet and protested :
“Mr. Speaker, God Almighty made the country.
I object to being made responsible for the Murray
being further from Sydney than it is from Mel-
bourne.”
N.S.W. Premier Carruthers is not likely
to ask N.S.W. Parliament to amend the Arbitra-
tion Act. Some of his supporters would amend it
out of existence, and the balance of the House
would increase the Court’s powers in favor of
unionism. Between the two stools Joseph would
be mangled.
U.S.A. President Roosevelt, in his first
Presidential message to Congress after his elec-
tion, inferentially upholds the preference-to-
unionists principle of N.S.W. arbitration law.
Unionists, he says, have always a legal, and some-
times also a moral, right to refuse to work side by
side with non-unionists.
N.S.W. Employers’ Federation, which
doesn’t want the Arbitration Act, has decided to
attempt its repeal on the ground that its decisions
can’t be enforced. Which suggests the Employers’
Federation asking for six-penn’orth of prussic acid
to poison a dog, on the ground that the animal is
dead and otherwise harmless.
In evidence before the Navigation Com-
mittee, Mr. Boner, Australian manager for the
North German Shipping Cos., stated that not only
were his subsidised boats forbidden to carry Aus-
tralian butter, milk, grain and meat to Germany,
but the ships themselves must be built of German
materials, and must use German coal exclusively
when available.
“If,” says the Owe’Sullivan, “we build
the railway engines in N.S.W. we shall have the
money and the engines as well.” But, if we stop
at that, neither the money nor the engine will be
ours. The money will belong to Cohen, who lent
it to us; and the engine simply replaces a worn-
out engine that was built with borrowed money,
and therefore will also belong to Cohen. Pro-
tection is good ; but Protection and No More
Borrowing are better.
All Australian political bodies seem unable
to present a series of resolutions to the public in
uniform style. Even the first 14 resolutions
carried at the Hobart Conference are eccentric in
their phraseology. They begin by saying that
certain things “ought to be” done; then they
said that several other things “must” be done;
further, that several others “may” or “might”
be done ; and so on. In every case, by the altera-
tion of two or three words, these mere recommenda-
tions would have assumed proper shape.
S.M. Herald, girding at the Arbitration
Act and the like :
The private employer has no fictitious wag-e-fund to
fall back on. He must pay his men out of the value they
produce. Labor parties might fill the statute-books to-
morrow as they pleased, and in the long run their
statutes would be inoperative, for economics is stronger
than politics.
That’s just where
arbitration pro-
tects the employer.
Workmen often
don’t know what
the employer can
afford. In the
strike days they
sometimes, there-
fore, made impos-
sible demands, and
if they won they
shut that employer
up. Now all that
an employer has to
do is to satisfy a
Supreme Court
Judge that he can’t
afford what is
asked, and there is
an end of it. In-
stead of the deci-
sion resting with a
body of workmen
with no means of
finding out how
matters really
stand, it rests with a Court which has the means
of finding out.
Maoriland has a political cat-in-the-bag
just now, and parties will remain undecided until
it has jumped. The uncertain animal is the land
question, on which a Royal Commission has been
appointed. Said that Mr. Joseph Ward may yet
champion the freehold. He is reported to be
rapidly making a pile out of his grain business,
and is looking forward to acquiring an estate.
Though Westralia will have to import over
a quarter of a million bushels of wheat this year
to feed its handful of people, it has just shipped
250 tons of wheat by the Norfolk to London.
This is to impress the world with the magnificent
resources of Groperland. This year it is esti-
mated that Westralia will import from the Eastern
States eggs, butter, bacon, cheese, fruit, onions,
potatoes, and other nroduce to the value of over
£500,000.
The Liberals in S. A. are in a very awkward
position, for in several districts both the Conser-
vatives and the Labor party are attempting to
win all the seats. Among men thus opposed by
the Labor party are Denny (Kingston’s late col-
league), Scherk, and Cummins, who have been
consistent Democrats. Kirkpatrick, M.L.C., and
Labor leader Price, it is said, both pleaded with
the party not to put up men in Adelaide against
Denny and Scherk (there are four seats).
S. Australia has a bad habit of running its
trains through its principal streets; Adelaide,
Pt. Adelaide, Pt. Pirie and Glenelg all have the
excitement and the soot of this habit. The Vic-
toria Square line, which runs through the long
principal street of Glenelg and through half a mile
of King William-street, Adelaide, is the worst
example. Needless to say, it was Private Enter-
prise that built the line.
“Exile”: Why this terrible outcry for
absolute commercialism in the building of railway
engines ? I seem to remember long reports from
the Commissioners and their officials giving com-
mercial reasons against many railway proposals.
Yet the Government, supported by Parliament,
authorised their construction in the public interest.
Does not the public interest apply to engines
equally with the lines on which they run ?
A LITTLE LEARNING.
“ Now, Mr. Smarty, what do you know about
this race? ”
“ Not much. Enough not to back the favorite,
but not enough to pick the winner.”
England’s suggestion that Canada should
contribute an annual £250,000 for the defence of
Halifax (Nova Scotia) and Esquimault (Britisli
Columbia), leaving the British Govt, full control,
has been promptly snubbed. The Dominion re-
plies that it prefers paying the total cost and re-
taining full control. Compare this dignified and
independent attitude with Australia’s servile
Naval Subsidy !
N.S.W. ex-Premier John See charges that
the Carruthers Government paid far too much for
Myall Creek—the estate resumed recently for
closer settlement. In reply the Lands Minister
gives a flat denial. But there will always be an
atmosphere of suspicion about closer settlement
effected by land purchase. The equitable and
natural way to closer-settle is by a progressive tax
on land values sufficiently high to make it unprofit-
able to hold big estates.
Something is to be said against the pro-
posed settling of Austrians on the sugar lands of
North Queensland if the scheme means that a
colony of Austrians is to be segregated in a
certain area. Europeans who come to blend are
welcome, but not those who want to found a
community within a community. At one time
the preponderance of Germans in one Queensland
electorate caused the election of a member to turn
on German instead of Australian questions.
There was a crowded indignation meeting
of South Melbourne ratepayers the other day to
protest against the local council’s determination to
spend £5OO on a jag in honor of the city’s jubilee.
South Melbourne has a public debt of nearly
£195,000. It should celebrate its jubilee in
humiliation and prayer.
N.S.W. People’s Reform League is said to
be so disgusted with Mr. Carruthers that it is
busily gathering funds to send a couple of
organisers to the country to organise against the
Government. Everything points to the conclu-
sion that when Joseph goes to the country next it
will be as the friend of the Labor Party.
Surely even the Carruthers Government
would not permit a syndicate to run steam inotor-
’busses through the streets of Sydney and suburbs in
competition with the State trams ? To say nothing
of the politically suicidal character of such an act, it
would also be murderous, with the proposed speed
of 30 miles an hour. The tram is a fixed quantity
so far as the rails are concerned—people are safe
when clear of the rails. But the motor-’busses
would be free to skim corners and dodge in and
out everywhere.
S.M. Herald, too, has gathered up its
skirts for a campaign against Socialism :
Perhaps, in the long' run, it does not matter very much
how others see us. We shall work out our own destiny.
But in the meanwhile the stigma of Socialism is bound to
affect our foreign credit.
The old “ pop-shop ” argument again. Australia,
in the eyes of the non-Australian press published
in Australia, has already lost its independence,
and must consider every move in the light of the
foreign creditor.
Melbourne Customs wrestled with a new
undesirable-immigrants problem recently. The
sailing vessel Men nock arrived with her articles
expired, which meant that every member of the
crew was entitled to his discharge. There was no
trouble over the mixed white crew; but there
were two negroes, besides, and if the captain dis-
charged them he would be liable to be fined for
landing prohibited immigrants ; while if he did n’t
discharge them, they had an action against him
under the Merchant Shipping Act—penalty, £5O
in each case. Finally, the negroes solved the
difficulty by deciding on re-shipping in the
Mennock.
The Queensland Register has some new
and entirely original information concerning the
political customs of New South Wales. It asserts
that, until Hogue put his foot down, it was usual
to give each N.S. Wales M.L.A. £lO at Christmas
time to spend in his electorate. Dismal Jimmy,
says the Register, does the spending himself
now, having discovered that last year the member
for Geebung Creek gave his tenner to the foreign
missions; that the member for Ti-tree Elat
knocked his little lot down in a wild tear; that
the man from Mopoke Gully paid his life assur-
ance premium with his bit; that another member
put his divvy into shares in Adams’ sweeps, and
so on.
“ Lak ” : If the post office
is under Federal control, why—
when I recently bought (in
Queensland) a couple of N.S.W.
stamps—did the Department
charge me 25 per cent, on the
4d. ? I’m a whooper for Federa-
tion, but its practical evolution is
wretchedly slow.
Sydney D.T. talks horse-
sense when it advises N.S. Wales
to “take a lesson from what
has been done in Canada,
to encourage rather than
to tax primary production.”
It is monstrous that the
primary producer should
have to hump his bulky
goods from one end of the
earth to the other before
he finds a market. Canada
teaches the lesson—put on
heavy Customs duties, bring
in a host of consumers, give
the farmer a local market, and
so save him all this unnecessary
and expensive dragging of wheat
and dead cow across the earth.
That decent political dunderhead, David
Storey, tells the people of Rand wick (N.S.W.)
that he has discovered a great secret —that the
philosopher’s stone of government is to Let the
People Alone. Mr. Storey is a little late in
arriving at his conclusion—the doctrine of laissez-
faire is nearly a century old in its revival, and
dates originally back to Aristotle. But when Mr.
Storey’s further remarks are studied, it will be
found that his scheme does not mean really that
the people should be let alone, but that the mono-
polists, sweaters and boodlers should not be
interfered with.
S.M. Herald says, concerning the mail
contract—“lt is hardly consistent in those who
want white labor to be unwilling to pay for it.”
This, in spite of the fact that Orient manager
Anderson has said that the cost of labor has
nothing to do with the matter, and that his co.
wants the higher figure simply because the busi-
ness hasn’t paid. As a matter of fact, black labor
doesn’t seem to be cheaper than white on the mail
boats. Its recommendation is that it is more
servile. The mail lines which serve Australia
serve also India, and the Nabob passenger from
India with the curry-colored face and the hob-
nailed liver, likes servile surroundings.
There seems to be a little coolness between
the Argus and Private Enterprise just now ; so
the paper is saying things like this :
The value of the sites in the centre of the city nd in its
chief thoroughfares is greatly increased by their position
and those who reap this unearned increment ought to be
prepared to accept the responsibility and expense whioh
the occupying of such a site entails. The general appear-
ance of the city cannot be allowed to be ruined by the
greed of individual occupiers who desire either to erect
mean and unworthy buildings, or, on the other hand, to
advertise themselves by pretentiousness and vulgarity.
“Unearned increment”! Then what has the
Argus to say about a land tax, which would get
back for the people some of their own ?
Another instance of the Tory’s anxiety to
let the State run the non-dividend-paying con-
cern comes from south-west Victoria. Under
private enterprise Kenilworth has left a trail of
broken hopes and fortunes behind it. Now there
is great booming to foist it on to the State for
closer settlement. And when the estate (which
lies between Hamilton and Horsham, and only
reaches civilisation occasionally per bullock-wag-
gon) has proved a failure under closer settlement,
the Tory will point with great solemnity to another
State failure. But good estates, that will pay
handsomely under any management, are held
securely for private enterprise to prosper upon.
Thus the Argus on Russia’s “Red
Sunday”
They (the people) were simply seeking to make their
wishes known in a peaceful manner to the head of the
State. What is to be thought of the strength or self-
confidence of a Government which cannot control an un-
armed crowd without pouring volleys of bullets into it ?
Yet what did this Argus say when a Victorian
Government planted in the Melbourne morgue-
yard Tom Price and some hundreds of mounted
riflemen, provided with ball cartridges, awaiting
the peaceful crowd which assembled in Flinders
Park to express an opinion on the maritime strike ?
Like the hypocrite-papers that Russell-Lowell
placarded, the Argus “believes in freedom’s
laws—as far away as Paris is.”
“ War E.,” on the way they settle people
on the land in N.S.W. :
The Ganmain “Forest” Reserve case has advanced
another stage ; the Wagga Land Board has recommended
the application of the Scottish-Australian Investment 00.
to lease nearly 13,000 acres. All this land is within five
or six miles of Ganmain railway station, on the non-pay-
ing S.W. line. It’s in the belt of the finest wheat-growing
land in N.S.W., and doesn’t carry timber good enough to
build a goat-bail. Even granting the possibility of timber
on it within the next 30 years, the question is whether it
would not pay the State better to establish, say, 20 homes
on the alleged timber reserve, rather than lock up agri-
cultural land for 28 years, leasing it to a foreign “ shindy -
kat ”at sd. per acre ? The Board’s decision now goes to
the Minister for Lands, and N.S.W. will await with
interest Mr. Ashton’s verdict whether it’s better to raise
pine or men.
“ F.H.R.” : Barrenjoey Carruthers might
see if he can’t economise in the N.S.W Forest. De-
partment. Rangers may do good in some districts,
where piles are being cut, but why are three of
them kept in the Dubbo district ? Sleeper chop-
pers work there, and they won’t cut a young tree
for sleepers, because they would not be passed.
The fees the rangers collect do not nearly pay
salary and expenses, which are heavy.
Wolfe’s Schnapps as good for women as
for men. Buy bottles with wrappers intact. Get
what has been known for 70 years. Don’t be
deceived. Get Wolfe’s Schnapps.*

Legal Notice.
pURSUANT to an Order of the High Court of Justice
Chancery Division, dated the sth day of October,
1904, made in the matter of the ESTATE of GEORGE
MARSDEN, Deceased, and in an action Church against
Marsden, 1904, M., No. 2,007, the persons claiming to be
next-of-kin according to the statutes for the distribution
of Intestates’ estates of George Marsden, formerly of West
Mount, Uttoxeter New-road, Derby, in the countv of
Derby, but late of the Coppice Hospital, in the city'and
county of Nottingham, Gentleman, who died at The
Coppice Hospital aforesaid, on the 22nd February, 1904,
living' at the time of his death, or to be the legal personal
representative of such of the said next-of-kin as are now
dead, and particularly Will am John Marsden, formerly of
the town and county of Derby', and Henry Alarsden,
formerly of Derby, aforesaid, and Nottingham, brothers
of the said George Marsden, who, if living on the said 22nd
February', 1904, would have been among the said next-of-
kin, or if dead since that date, the persons claiming to be
their respective legal personal representatives, are, by
their solicitors, on or before the Ist. day of June, 1905, to
come in and prove their claims at the ‘Chambers of Air.
Justice Buckley and Air. Justice Warrington, at the
Royal Courts of Justice, Strand, London, or in default
thereof they will be peremptorily excluded from the
benefit of the said order. Thursday, the Bth day of June,
1905, at eleven o’clock in the forenoon, at the said
Chambers, is appointed for hearing and adjudicating upon
the claims.
Dated this 12th day of January, 1905.
RICHARD WHITE, Master.

England, Agents for Eddowes and Sons, Derby,

AS SAFE AS fl HOUSE
is an old-fashioned saying that has passed muster these
many years. But it is not true as regards present-day
conditions; for a house is not safe unless protected from
damp by a thoroughly reliable and permanent Dampcourse.
MALTHOID Dampcourse used in the foundations,
over window and door openings, under window-sills and
parapets, is a perfect protection from the evil effects
of Damp. It would be more true to say—As safe as a
house with
Malthoid Dampcourse
~ N°t the same as others.-

The Coal-Pit Horse.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
GETTING the horse into the cage for the
pit is the first difficulty. He is first
blindfolded some distance away from the
shaft fence. Sometimes the horse gets suspicious,
and there is a bolt. Ultimately, by force or moral
suasion, the horse’s feet are planted on the wet
bridge of a horrible greasy swaying thing; the
cage-doors are slung on and bolted, and “ Horse
in cage” is rapped down. “ All clear ” rings out
the pit-top hammer, and the startled brute is
shivering at the cold pit-bottom before he has
time to think of kicking, even if that were possible
in a ten-man cage.
Some horses buck into the swing of pit life from
the jump; others, more imaginative, conjure up
dread visions from the darkness, and will not
budge till an old horse takes lead with the first
set. Often for weeks and weelcs the wheeler must
trudge on ahead with the light. Sometimes a
poor brute takes fright and blinds himself against
a prop; the light of others’ lives go out from no
apparent cause. A blind horse, even if new to the
work, seldom gives trouble ; he generally has a
don’t-give-a-hang air. Bright meadow or dark
pit—it is all the same to him.
When the stables are below ground, regular
feeds, shorter time from stalls, and decreased
travelling would appear to counteract the lack of
a little sunshine. Yet you sometimes see a liigh-
spinted brute die after being kept down below a
week plain case of broken heart. Horses drawn
from mines, during the Newcastle colliers’ nine
months’ strike, stampeded to the darkest gullies
at the first flush of dawn, only returning to the old
feeding-grounds after sunset. A horse born below
ground gives the wheeler a lovely time breaking
him in. Once let him get away, and lie scampers
through and through the mine galleries till the
safeties burn dim.
Most old pit-horses are demons for tricks.
I hey can unfasten a tucker-tin or empty a bottle
without removing it from the nail. They will
fight like trapper boys to jump one another’s turn
for the cage at knock-off. Knew one that could
breast a skip into the cage as well as any man ;
one that ate tobacco and cigarettes; one that
chased with his mouth open everyone who couldn’t
talk ‘ wheeler ” to him ; and another that, instead
of breeching the skips down the incline, used to
place his feet on the rails and slide. 11 was
amusing to watch an old stager, pulling small coal
and ashes from the furnace, prick his ears as each
skip was coupled-up, and stand on his dignity if
the wheeler shoved on an. extra one. Then the
wheeler would pretend to uncouple by rattling
the chain on the last skip, whereupon the old boy
would throw himself into the collar quite
contented.
JIMMY S.
Melbourne Hospital bakes its own bread
and manages to supply itself—using between
seven and eight bags of flour a week—with the
pure unadulterated, full-weight article at 3Ad. the
41b. loaf, what time the suburban baker is char ing
sd. cash over the counter and 5Ad. and 6d. if
delivered, for a mostly inferior article.
Expenses.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
A SYDNEY newspaper-office has lately
been troubled over “expenses.” A re-
porter is not supposed to spend his own
money on fares, hotel-bills, and other charges
incurred whilst engaged in the work of the office.
He bills the proprietary for such out-goings. In the
case of the “ rounds-man ’’—who goes the rounds
of the police-stations, hospitals, fire-stations, and
so on, for news—some offices pay a fixed sum
weekly, say £l, for trams and cabs. That is
supposed to cover everything, and anything
saved is a legitimate perquisite. The ordinary
staff-member sent on an up-country trip is in-
structed to stay at a good hotel, and his expenses
are met by the office. The sub-editor usually
supervises expense-accounts, and is supposed to
look after the interests of the proprietary and of
the staff, calling attention to under-charging or
over-charging.
* * * *
. the palmy days of the Australian press,
10 to 20 years ago, expenses were allowed on
a generous scale. If a reporter went away
vvith a Minister of the Crown and did his
share of “shouting” when champagne was
bi (niched, the office paid without & murmur.
i^ n R P ee ’ a l’ a°ing to M.L. once, entertained
the Ministry at dinner—a very expensive dinner,
lhe Aug us didn’t question the item. The same
man for the same paper once went up-country
with a Minister of the Crown, commissioned to
get from him, somehow, certain information. He
got the news, but his expenses account was enor-
mous. It included one item, “Lost at 100, £40.”
lhe sub-editor boggled at that sum. “Really,
Mr. ——, is it fair to charge the office with that ?”
he asked. “ Well, you told me to get the
news, and I had to play 100 with the old man to
keep him sweet, and I lost.” “Supposing you
had won, blandly inquired the sub., “ would you
have paid your winnings into the office ? ” “ Oh,
certainly,” said the reporter, as he winked the
other eye. The sub-editor winked too, and the
account went through.
The serenely respectable Sydney Morning
Herald had an hilarious expenses incident once.
A reporter who had made up his mind to leave the
wu , • , ( '. en sent on a special mining inquiry.
When his bill of expenses came in it read some-
thing like this: —
Railway fare £3 5 4
Coach 4 0 0
Monday, present to lady.’. " " 010 n
Hotel bill (four days) 1 10 0
Wednesday, present to lady .. 010 0
Incidentals 076
A shock of horror went through that moral office
when the bill reached the manager, and there was
sune discussion among various “heads” as to
whether the man ought not to be dismissed with-
out being called upon for an explanation. “ There
is not the slightest doubt as to its meaning ” said
one official gravely. “He glories in his miscon-
duct, and has the effrontery to bring the records
of it within the portals of the Sydney Morning
Herald office.” And he breathed as heavily
as it he had just run from viewing the dese-
cration of the Holy Sepulchre. Finally it was
decided to sentence the criminal .to be cast from
the Herald, to gnash his teeth in the outer dark-
ness, after an exhortation from the heavy-weight
exhorter of the office.
“Sir, your scandalous offence has shocked
me deeply began the official when the un-
abashed delinquent faced him, and ten minutes
of scathing eloquence followed. The reporter
professed am izament. What had he done ’ He
was conscious of no offence. The official reluc-
tantly c. me down from the heights of his moral
i i(.agnation to point a scandalised, tremulous
pencil at the item—-
Present to lady .. .. 10 0
“Oh, that s all right,” remarked the reporter
cherubically. “ You see, the mine was closed-
clown, arid the caretaker’s wife was in charge I
had to give her a tip to open up the mine the* two
days ! examined it. What did you think it was
sir.' -No explanation was tendered as to what
he thought. The reporter was, as he expected
asked to resign. ’
Nowadays, expense-accounts are very closely
checked. I here is no chance of charging-up
champagne to the oftioe. Indeed, a reporter who
ruined a suit of clothes, doing a wreck for a daily
paper, was only allowed the cost of a new suit
after some haggling. Which recalls a Sydney
Pf IL J Telegraph joke. A fledgling reporter had
joined the staff, and was persuaded by a humorist
that, since the office required its representative to
attend some functions in evening dress, a dress-
suit was looked upon as a sort of uniform, and
was provided by the proprietary. “ All you need
to do is to go to your tailor, order a suit, and
charge it to the office.” The victim did so, and
had a rough five minutes with the accountant in
consequence.
One great daily has lately come down to the
point of instructing its special representatives “to
stay at boarding-houses when possible, as they
are less expensive.” J

The solar eclipse due on Monday next tvjll
appear annular (narrow rim of sun bordering dark
disc of intervening moon) along a line runnmg
N.E. from a point in the Great Bight to the
Queensland coast, N. of Rockhampton. At
Sydney, the obscuration —lasting from 2.50 p-m-
to 5.30 p.m.—will, at its greatest, leave visible, as
a crescent, about l-4th of the sun’s disc.

SPORTING NOTIONS.
The most interesting sporting fixture of the
week was the Dunlop Motor Reliability Contest for the
Buchanan Cup, between Sydney and Melbourne. The
motor* left Sydney on Tuesday morning', in glorious
weather. Among the starters was Mrs. B. Thompson, of
South Australia, driving her own car, a Wolseley, a one-
cylindered 6-h.p. vehicle of the light order, weighing about
half a ton and costing £250. There was a fine assembly of cars,
and even the motor bicycles showed a bewildering variety.
Among them was one of local build, and called the Illawarra.
All the rest were foreign made. The lightest machines
were the Motosacoche, which looked very like an ordinary
bicycle of the roadster pattern with an engine fitted on.
They weighed 80 lbs., were of 1J horse-power and the
market value was put down as £45. The big motor cars
aroused intense interest. The big Darracq (French) cars
looked like line-of-battle ships, and so did the Decauvilles.
They seemed altogether too heavy, and the cars that
seemed the most road-worthy were two elegantly finished
Argyll*. They glided away almost noiselessly, while the
French cars made a fearful row. A weird looking little
machine was Mark Foy’s “ Flying Fox,” which, by the
way, was the first motor car to accomplish the journey
between the two capitals. It was a 7h.p. Pan hard, and
was covered with honorable scars. The French cars were
all more costly than their English and American rivals.
The big Decauville and Darracq cars cost £750; the
Argylls £495 ; and the cheapest of the heavy class cars
was a £3OO Oldsmobile.
The first day’s run was from Sydney to Goul-
burn, a distance of 129 miles, over roads styled second and
first-class. The quickest time made by the motor-cycles
was that of B. James on a Minerva. V. H. Gard was next
on a Brown, and the locally-built Illawarra was close up.
The light cars were led in by Kellow on a Talbot; a
Da Dion, a Swift, and an Oldsmobile scored equal points.
In the heavy class the only cars which did not score the
full 500 points were Mark Foy’s Flying Fox (a Panhard), a
Talbot, and another Talbot. The fastest time was made by
Victorian Yankee Stevens in a huge Darracq. He went for
speed and got over the 129 miles in shr. 45mm. The second
day’s run of 121 miles was over more severe roads, and
only three motor bikes scored the 500 points —the Illa-
warra, a Minerva, and a Brown. Four light cars (Olds-
mobile, Swift, and two De Dions) scored full 600. Among
the heavy ears Langford’s Darracq was wrecked, and
Rand’s Decauville went bung. The other failures were
Foy’s Flying Fox, Nicholls’ Darracq, and Holmes’ Olds-
mobile. The 121 miles between Gundagai and Albury
proved bad for the motor bikes, only James and Gard on
the Minerva and Brown machines getting full score. The
same four light cars got through, and six Talbot,
the two Argylls, two Darracqs, and a Decauville —made
top score. The run from Gundagai to Albury had proved
the crisis of the journey. From Albury to Euroa they all
picked up again, and full points were scored by the nine
heavy cars left in, and five of the eight light cars that
were still going, while three motor-cycles failed to score.
Langford’s Darracq was progressing quietly behind the
tail of a despised moke en route for a railway station.
From start to finish, H. Li. Stevens in his big
Darracq had thundered in the very front. He ran oyer
everything—plunging through watercourses, jumping
logs, and flying at break-neck speed where others crawled.
His actual time for the whole journey was 23 hours 42sec.,
and his average speed 24 l-7th miles per hour. Behind
him were the two Argylls, driven by Messrs. Tarrant and
Ross, who had averaged 17 7-Bth and 17J miles per hour
respectively. Sydney Stott in his Decauville made 16J
miles per hour for the whole journey. These four
cars were the only ones which scored the possible
2500 marks. The best light cars were Mr. Coleman’s
Swift (14J miles per hour) and Messrs. Day and Craven’s
De Dions, which made 15J and 14J miles per hour respec-
tively for the trip. The breakdown in the motor-bikes
was very complete, Card’s Brown and B. James’ Minerva
being the only ones to score the possible. They made an
average speed of 19J and 20 3-sth miles per hour for
the whole trip. Mrs. Thompson, the lady who drove her
own oar, was right out of it; still she did better than
many of the men, having scored 1982 points out of the
possible 2500.
The final run-off to decide the destination of
the trophies will take place between Melbourne and
Ballarat, on a day to be fixed. The winners in every
class are Victorians.
* • * •
Just now there is in Melbourne a “world-
girdling motorist ” —Charles J. Gliddon. He is travelling
round the world in the Arctic Circle, 24 h.p. Napier. He
crossed Canada on the rails of the C.P. line, having special
flanged wheels fitted to his car for the purpose. He is
using ordinary pneumatic Dunlops for the rest of his
journey. #
The numerousness and cheerfulness of the crowd
at Caulfield last Saturday promised a good send-off for
the V.R.C. Autumn Meeting, when the truth, or other-
wise, of Caulfield “form” will be tested in the New-
market Handicap. Probably the winner of the popular
sprint has been seen among recent battlers; neverthe-
less, the race looks more open than in many previous
years. Gladsome, Abington, Medallist, and (in a lesser
degree) Bullion are worth backing on their late public
trials, whilst Playaway, Haloya, and Lachlan, a
dark stable companion of Bullion, have a threaten-
ing appearance. Also “ the field ” is a notoriously
dangerous horse. Last year Old Mairp, a 25 to I chance,
beat the favorites. This time a turn-up for the books is
not so likely as a close struggle between two or three of
the well-backed ones, with two or three other public fanoies
following them home. Gladsome given a favorable runnutsf
go near to victory if she doesn’t actually win. On paper
the flying mare looks better able to give 21 lbs. to Bullion
than 18 lbs. to Abington, or 36 lbs. to Medallist. The ques-
tion whether any reasonable weight can stop Gladsome is
answered in a cocksure negative by her warm admirers,
yet the “ Racing Chronicle ” laughs at their enthusiasm ;
9st. 71b. is the highest weight ever humped by a New-
market winner thus far, and this is 101 b. less than Glad-
some’s burden. Between Abington and Medallist there is
little to choose ; Haloya, spoken of as a good thing, is not
a highly-proved performer ; Lachlan, the unknown, has
the advantage and disadvantage of a light weight (7st.),
which means a very small jockey for so large a colt;
Playaway is open to the objections that she failed last
year, when punters laid even money on her, and is not an
up-to-date “star” like Gladsome, Abington, or even
All the same, this ecribe likes Playaway. She
carries only 4lb. more than the weic-ht with which she
was placed third last year. Assum.ng her to have im-
proved with age, her chance is undeniable. Playaway
may beat Medallist, Gladsome and Abington.
'• * • *
The Australian Cup, at time of writing, appears
to be at the meroy of the 3-year-old division, notwith-
standing Lamrock, Green Mountain, Bright Beauty, Elvo,
Merrion, Emir, and other seasoned horses. Blinker, if he
has been prepared for the 2J miles, should beat Billings-
gate and Dividend, though the latter is much respected
for the workmanlike way in which he outstayed Canteen
last week. Emir’s chance of carrying 9.7 in front of
the young and old opposition may be judged from
his running in a w.f.a. race on Saturday. As regards
his temperament, Emir is the same beast now as
heretofore, but it must be remembered that he galloped
beautifully at Flemington 12 months ago, when even
Lord Cardigan couldn’t trouble him. In a field of com-
parative commoners Emir must always be as dangerous
as he is untrustworthy. The big son of Wallace and
Emmie performed in an absurdly “off-and-on” style
behind Gladsome when that honest mare was getting
away with the Futurity Stakes. First ho wouldn't, and
then he would gallop, and when he did choose to go he
travelled faster than any of them, not excepting the
winner.
• * * *
Well-known N.S.W. squatter and sport, Mr.
H. C. White, died at Hobart on Friday night of internal
cancer, aged about 70. Mr. White was breeding horses
on the Castlereagh 46 years ago, and owned at the time
of his death one of the best studs in Australia, built up
principally by well-judged importations. His first big
venture in connection with breeding racehorses was the
purchase, 24 years ago, of famous English mare Valetta
and three of her female progeny for £lOOO. Paris (winner
ef two successive Caulfield Cups and Doncaster Handicap)
and Bungebah (Newmarket winner) were of Valetta
descent, and bred by Mr. White, who was a younger
brother of the late Mr. James White.

Comes from a reliable source that Sydney Turf
Club is shortly to be absorbed by A.J.0., which body will
also secure S.T.O.’s race days.

Maoriland-bred Moifaa, winner of last year’s
Liverpool (Eng.) Grand National Steeplechase, and
favorite for this year’s ditto, has been bought by King
Ned. Moifaa is by Natator out of Debigh.
* * • *
A commission for Gladsome was foreshadowed
in the minds of suspicious observers when Melbourne
Argus made the announcement that Sol Green’s cham-
pion would be a non-starter for the Newmarket if her
stable-companion, Independence, continued to go well on
the track. Last Thursday morning Independence did a
first-class gallop as evidence that she was keeping
well,” nevertheless the other mare was backed later in
the day for all the money in the market. The Argus
evidently had been used for the dissemination of a bogus
“ warning.” . * ♦
Latest N.S.W. 2-year-old winner, Grafton Lass
(who scored at Warwick Farm on Saturday), promises
excellently for the future, as she won in capital style.
Though an awkward galloper, the filly has size and reach,
and plenty of pace. She is a well-bred one indeed—by
Grafton-Miss Melos, by Melos from Hirondelle, by Lochiel.
* * * *
Phil Newland was married in Adelaide last
week, and has gone off to overtake the Australian XI. in
Maoriland. Joe Darling, who sails to London direct, has
another month before starting, and goes on quietly look-
ing after his Adelaide sports-outfit business. His latest
innings was a level 100 in electorate cricket.
If no more interest is shown in champion
athletes Shrubb and Duffey in other capitals than in
Melbourne their visit will not be a success financially.
Last Saturday’s attendance on the M.C.O. outer ground
would scarcely pay for advertising, while the reserves
were but a quarter filled, mostly with dead-heads.
Duffey is still unable to strike the form he is supposed to
possess, losing his heat in the 100 yd. handicap to Morgan,
of Essendon, on 4Jyd., and though the judges gave him
the verdict in the 100 Invitation Handicap, it was a
doubtful honor, many feeling certain that Shea,
2£yd., was first, with Mueller, Widmer (N.S.W.), and
Duffey himself disputing the other places. Shrubb started
in the Open Mile, and though he won by 20yd. from Blake,
didn’t attempt anything sensational, and the Austra-
lasian amateur record of 4.25, held by J. F. Dalrymple,
still holds good, though he chipped 12-5 sec. off the Vic-
torian record of 4.804-5. In the 4-mile—giving, among
others, Blake 160yds. and a hefty youngster, C. J. Fraser,
of Malvern, 500yds.—he tackled the Victorian records of
4.304-5, 10.5 4-6,15.30 and 21min., and lowered the three
last to 9.47 4-5,14.58 4-5 and 20.15 2-5, winning, with a fine
■print, by half a lap from Blake. Writer has doubts about
Duffey, but he would like to see Shrubb extended. He fills
the eye as a runner. Moving like a machine, the thin, brown,
wiry figure paces round. Ashe passes, j r ou notice the wide,
deep chest, the well-set head, and square jaw ; then the
wide hipkand good thighs strike the eye, though the legs
are thin and the stride seems a trifle short. By the way,
the local runners make tremendous fuss over themselves.
They canter a quarter in 53 or 54, touch the tape, then
collapse and lie on the grass for a space while someone
rubs and fans them strenuously. Shrubb broke a 4-nule
record, and then walked easily to the pavilion, and writer
has, at Lillie Bridge, seen little L. E. Myers do his quarter
in 49see., and W. G. George his record mile, and then
stroll to the dressing-room as though nothing unusual
had occurred.

Strange what a lot of people affect to dis-
believe that Duffey ever put up that 9 3-ssec., and charac-
terise it as a “Yankee lie.” But considering that Duffey
has repeatedly done the same distance in 9 4-ssec., and
about ten others share the time with him, and that once
he did it on grass, there is nothing remarkable about him
dropping. l-sth of a second on a first-class track, such
as one would expect them to have in a city like New York.
* * * *
When the distinguished British and Yankee
runners arrive in Sydney it is just possible that
they will meet foemen more worthy of their
legs. Sydney has taken the matter very seriously,
and been hard at work trying to undo the evil
perpetrated by the cronk flat-racers who have ruined
the game. For weeks a number of very speedy dark
horses have been undergoing a course of special training,
A. A. CAMERON, a Scottish athlete now in
M.L., who has challenged Hackenschmidt to
a wrestling match.
and it is said that some hair-raising times have been made
by two short and a pair of long-distance men. One thing
is certain—that if this State puts its ears right hack
and seriously gets its head down to bringing out some
runners who will race Duffey and Shrubb very close, it
can do it. We’ve produced some champions in the past,
and our great athletic resources are not played out yet.
A 20-year-old son of ancient M.L. athletic
champion Bill Burk wants to toe the mark for a mile run
with Englishman Shrubb when latter visits Dunedin.
T’other day, Burk ran a half-mile against the wind in
2min. 3sec. Coming out 30min. later, and under adverse
conditions, he loped over the mile in 4min. 35 2-ssec. The
Horsham wonder’s best time is 4min. 22sec., and the
young Maorilander can do it in something under 4min.
30sec.

“Alex. M.”: If I didn’t know that “Alba”
(8., 16/2/’O5) frequently writes at raw-random, I should
“laugh consumedly” at his dogfulness. He is right
about the bull-terrier being the prince of fighters, by
reason of his quickness and of the “ punishing ” length of
jaw which has been evolved in him by selection in
breeding; but, all the same, if the pure “bull” gets
an effective hold, it’s long odds against the other
tyke. Then, as to that “ huge kangaroo-dog ” affair,
“ Alba ” should know that for anything like a
“ fair go,” dogs are matched carefully by weight
—a difference of, say, 351 b. against 401 b. being almost pro-
hibitory. Therefore 301 b. against 60 or 70 could, bar
accidents, end only one way. Further, if a “ bull ” gets a
hold on a kangaroo-dog that allows the latter free use of
his teeth, the former will hold on till he is practically cut
to pieces by the other’s slaughtering snap-bites. 1 once
saw a bulldog thus killed by a large and savage
greyhound. The “bull” had got a breast-hold, and
suffered himself to be slashed to ribbons by the grey-
hound’s trenchant snaps. He died, on the hold, from
sheer loss of blood ; but not before he had “ worked-in ”
almost to his antagonist’s heart; said antagonist also ex-
piring half-an-hour later. Again, a dog that is a cocktail
at bottom may fight splendidly as long as he’s getting the
best of it; but I have known a battle won by the mangled
remnant that crawled indomitably up to “ scratch ” after
a two hours Struggle, from an opponent that then put hie
tail down and fled the pit. And, finally, there is no
authenticated instance of absolutely pure bull-blood
showing the our. When that happens there is always
strong suspicion of a “ throw-back ” to a non-bull strain.
When Dick Cavill carried his cargo of ginger-
beer and peanuts over 300yds. in 3min. 34 3-ssec. at Liver-
pool Baths, on September 27th, 1902, and thereby
smashed through his own world record by 6 l-ssec., and
left Lane’s record an additional 2-sths behind, the
swimming world wondered. It was considered a mar-
vellous performance, but of late Sydney has seen so many
world’s records go to pieces that when B. B. Reran did
the same distance in 3min. 31 4-ssec. in Farmer’s Domain
Bath, Sydney, on Saturday, nobody was a bit surprised.
The laps were 45yds. Ift., and it took Dick Cavill 3min.
40 4-ssec. to cover the same distance in the self-same
bath when he broke the then world’s record on Feb.
23rd, 1901.

Not to be outdone by women long-distance swi ai-
mers of Victoria and Queensland, Miss Massey, daughter
of a Claremont (W.A.) bath proprietor, recently swam
from Claremont jetty to Point Walter jetty and back
(4 miles) in 3hr. lmin. Miss Massey expects to do a lot
better next time she tackles the task.
• * * *
The running of Ellegaard is a thing which even
the Dane himself does not understand. Like the wrath of
Heaven, the ability to suddenly dash off at top speed, to
jump from dead slow to somewhere about 20 miles an
hour all in a twirl of the pedals, falls on him without
warning. Sometimes it falls on him at the wrong part of
the race, and then he dashes away only to be overtaken
and beaten horribly—for it never falls on him twice in the
same race. However, it struck him at the right moment
in the team races between himself and Rutt on the one
part, and Farley and Ivor Lawson on the other part. The
’Murkan world champion won the first mile, but the
second, third, fourth and fifth were won by the Dane in a
most decisive manner. Neither Lawson nor Farley got a
look in.
The apparent honesty of the man-to-man test
racing on Melbourne Exhibition track last week compen-
sated in a measure for its slowness. Ellegaard crawled
for } mile, with Farley crawling behind him, or vice verm,
and then they sprinted home, the Dane proving himself the
better tactician, or the better sprinter. Anyway, he won
two successive heats. Lawson and Rutt likewise crawled
and sprinted, the American’s two wins being more dis-
tinctly masterful than Ellegaard s. Had the men raced
all the way in every heat, one of the results might have
been different, and the public would certainly have got
more fun for its money ; but if they were all doing their
best to beat one another the public cannot complain.
Honest wheel-racing between two men, neither of whom
can “donkey lick” the other, is the very antithesis of
boat-racing. In the one case the early pacer gains an
advantage, in the other case he gives it to his opponent.
A champion sculler can win all the way, like a champion
foot-racer ; hut a champion cyclist can’t win all the way
if the way is any considerable distance. If he cannot afford
to make the pace from the jump, is it likely that an
honest solitary opponent will attempt the impossible.
Bike-racing- lacks the warlike spirit of other manly sports.

N.S.W. League of Wheelmen last week threw
out Larry Corbett’s appeal for whitewash, not on the
jrrounds that he must obey the League s ruling 1 as to the
disposal of last year’s Sydney Thousand prize before any
appeal can be considered, but because it was in his
brother’s handwriting. When Larry comes back he is
going to appeal personally. Stated on good authority
that the voting of the Council will be the same as m the
MacFarland case. They’re generous with the whitewash
in Sydney.

Said that Stanbury will meet Towns next week
over a friendly cup of tea to arrange the date for their
race. Stanbury is getting down the flesh somewhat,
having reduced his weight to somewhere about 1.5 stone
—and a great struggle it has been. _ Possible Stanbury
will have an even bigger contract in putting up the
money than in gettingjiown the fat.
Dull and warm, with a faint nor’easter, Satur-
day afternoon found Sydney Harbor crowded with racing
craft, no less than seven events being almost simul-
taneously contested. The grey-blue water was flecked
everywhere with white sails. The yachts of the Prince
Alfred Club were racing for the Marshall Plate, a trophy
which has to be won four times by an ownerin his own yacht
to become his permanent possession. This was the second
race for it, and last year it was won by one Brookhoff in
the Fleetwing. Fleetwing was present to get in a second
leg if possible, and started with a 1 min. handicap, and
five ahead of her, the limit boat receiving as much as
25min. After a first class and well contested race, A. E.
Muston’s Janet, a 3min. boat, got home first, with
the Sunbeam from scratch second and Magic, another
3 minuter, third. Last year’s winner was a long way out
of it.
• * * *
Sydney Sailing Club’s handicap on Saturday as
a race was anything, but exciting. Desdemona, with a
10 minutes handicap, got a good start, with only two
beside her and the same number ahead of her, and had an
easy win. The feature of the race was the masterly way
Chris. Webb saved the Australian from a capsize and
luffed clean out of danger and, incidentally, of all hope of
winning the race.
* * • *
As suggested herein recently, when it was
announced that Turner and Jessop would have to box
till one proved superior, there was no difficult}' in picking
the winner. Both men were just as well as ever, and
displayed the same amount of skill and caution as usual,
but even in the second round Turner showed that he was
the better, and gave the Newcastle man a lot more than
that pug could return. From that on Turner led on
points, but after the 15th round it became evident that
Jessop was hopelessly beaten. He would not use his
right, the one on which he had relied all through the
battle, and after the scrap it was found that he had
broken it. The eternal Turner-Jessop draw grew
very wearisome ; five referees had declared that they
could not decide which was the winner. It was a most
profitable hit of inability for the bruisers, and now it has
come to an end.
» * >* *
Much-boomed S’African heavy-weight, Pat
Farley, came a cropper in Melbourne last week at hands
of welter pug., Ed. Williams, thrice beaten by Jack Black-
more in Sydney. Farley’s ways suited Williams nicely,
and he was punched out half through the first round. A
week before succumbing to Williams, Farley challenged
the winner of the Moir-Felix fight for £lOO aside. Lucky
for Farley, Moir didn’t take up the gage. Williams will
make some of the best sit up if he is given plenty of fight.
* * * *
“ Fedder” boxing champion Ted Murphy, in
defeating Victorian Sam Nathan at Sydney National S.C.
last week, demonstrated one or two things he had not
previously been credited with—he punched hard enough
to badly fracture a couple of his opponent’s ribs, and
out-generalled a much older and considerably more
•xperienced boxer. Murphy is a quiet youngster (cer-
tainly not moreithan 18), with a good-looking face.

A witness before the Tariff Commission
showed that Freetrade (?) Britain pays a bounty
on exported spirits, and produced “ Ridley on the
British Tariff ” (1903) to prove that excise duty on
exported spirits was repaid, plus 3d. and sd. per
gallon ! It is this bounty-fed competition which
has killed the Australian industry.
T. Bent’s faithful colleagues never miss an
opportunity of cracking-up that immense man—
indeed, several of them seem to exist for no other
purpose. At South Melbourne, the other night,
Cameron and M'Leod, Ministers of Works and
Mines, relieved a municipal banquet with frantic
eulogiums of the abounding Bent, reminding the
suburb that it had once refused Tommy, the
Cabbage King, with such scorn that the £5O de-
posit of this “wonderful, straight-forward, and
genuinely honest man ” was commandeered by the
State. “But,” declared M‘Leod, in a rhapsody,
“like the late Lord Beaconsfield, Mr. Bent’s time
has come, and now South Melbourne must hear
him. ” Cameron went even further, and beslavered
Bent so free'y that even the fatmen present began
to feel ill. This kind of thing reminds one very
forcibly of the parasites in the proverb that
pleaded so eloquently for the life of the unwhole-
some dog.

letin should publish the fact. We do so.
The result of the inquiry into the affair-
Wodonga is satisfactory to all but the genera!
public. About Christmas time the Wodong;,
going down Port Phillip, was scared nearly to
death by a shell from one of the Bay forts passing-
through her r igging. The gunners, when remo.i-
strated with, called the people on board clumsy
liars. The vessel was half a mile from the line of
fire, and to say that one of their shots went in her
direction was nothing but a studied insult. The
collective testimony of passengers and crew was
too strong, and the inquiry ordered in conse-
quence discovered that the shot, aimed with
uncommon accuracy, had struck the stanchion of
the target, and must have thence ricocheted over
(luckily, not through) the Wodonga. The soldiers
are hugely delighted at this testimonial to their
professional skill, and the sailors are pleased to
prove that they were not spinning sensational
yarns. The travelling public, finding themselves
not safe, even when the gunners fire straight, will
have to hold their heads on pretty tight whenever
gun practice is going on. When Mr. Jack Want’s
yacht Miranda had her topmast shot away between
Sydney Heads, some years ago, he was the com-
mission of inquiry himself on that occasion, and
his finding was couched in fine, vigorous language.

Adelaide is a proud city to-day. There
was a tram accident last week. The S.A. horse
trams have never before been credited with such
capacity.
‘ ‘ Elmore ” : Met a white hawker, t’other
day. While the billy boiled, he talked. “Women
are the best to sell to ; the old ones much better
than the girls. If you see a cat or two sitting out
on the doorstep, you needn’t knock ; it’s a sure
sign the missus is not at home. Men are the worst
customers. If you get talking to a man, turn the
conversation on infectious diseases ; then you can
always sell him a bottle of disinfectant. A man is
in a great funk when there is fever about. The
cockies are no good this year. They had a good
harvest, so now they can sport a buggy and drive
into the towns to the storekeepers. Last year,
they had to turn to us. If ever you go hawking,
sonny, start in a small way. Blacken your face
and tie half-a-dozen towels round your head, and
you’ll soon raise a waggon and pair. Only about
one in six Hindu hawkers walk now.”

Fashion, once set, of celebrating your arrival in
this, that or t’other ship, 50 years ago, has vast
possibilities about it. Luckily, “historic” arrivals
prior to ’55 aren’t liable to enthuse on the subject
unduly.
“ Hamer ” tells how he and himself had a
very good time :
When I came home late, the other night or early the
other morning, I found I had a tremendous fund of won-
derfully funny stories to tell, and a splendid listener to
enjoy them. Then somebody told me to go to bed, and !
started to undress. My boots were hard to get off, so,
seeing the difficulty, I offered to drag them off, for which
I thanked myself very much. When I sat down and ex-
tended my leg I found I could not do much dragging, and
accused myself of beingdrunk ; to which I retorted, “You’re
drunk yourself,” and laughed when I saw the humor of the
situation, until again urgently requested to “go to bed.”
After that I kicked my boots off with a bang that made
somebody scream, which set me laughing afresh, and
made me apologise in a far-reaching voice for waking any-
body up. “Go to bed,” sternly said the same voices. I
explained to the one nearest me that I was going to bed,
and stated the causes of the delay, the time it would take
for me to get to sleep, and other details. Then I wan-
dered into general observations about the weather, and
started on an extremely funny story (which I forget);
this caused a lady to exclaim, “Oh ! ” ; and a man,
“ Shut up—go to bed.” This last expression got on my
nerves. It seemed I had a noble, poetic nature, which
was misunderstood. In melancholy vein I conjured up
love and other reminiscences, which I (the other I)
remembered. That relieved me of the impression of my
loneliness. I recollected that I had known myself for
some time, and found myself a trusty friend. “ You ’re
a good old boy,” I said, affectionately ; “ come and have
a drink.” “Go to bed,” said an urgent voice. “I’m
going,” I said, “ I’m going ; only he’s drunk ; we ’re
going to have another drink—join us.” The voice told us
to go—not to bed this time. I looked for the door, opened
it, and walked through the doorway. To my surprise I
tumbled into a bed. When thirst woke me I was lying
across my bed. Going out to the bathroom for a drink,
I found my hat on the stair-head, my boots and clothes
decorating various parts of the stairs, and my landlady in a
state of nearly speechless indignation regarding them.
“Who was that man you brought in with you last night?”
she said, severely. “Oh, a grand fellow,” I said ; “ one of
the best. The best friend I have in the world.” Some-
times I have doubts ; but probably I was right.

From Goombo Lodge, Peckham, London, S.E.,
They tell this story in Wellington, M.L.,
where the electric tram service is new and the cars
are chronically crowded. A well-known man,
who had got used to travelling in crowded cars,
was going home one night after a birthday cele-
bration. He was a lone passenger until a lady
boarded the car; then, rising and bowing grace-
fully, he said, “ Madam, you take my seat —I will
stand.” And he hung on to a strap for the rest of
the ride, beaming with whisky and politeness.
“Alba” : Many Australian writers have
broken their pens striving for the word that will
explain the great oneness of London’s chief smell
—London, the sepulchre of six million breaths.
It flows up from dank cellars and alleys in germ-
laden streams; it is the smell of a small, over-
crowded bedroom. Listen a moment, and you
will hear the Englishman coughing in the midst of
it. At midnight, in winter, the cough is loudest
—up in the garrets, in waiting-rooms, and in the
recesses of wet and mouldy arches. The London
smell and the London cough are almost as deadly
as the London sneeze. If some enterprising syn-
dicate of electricians could harness the jolts from
each individual sneeze, there would be enough
power generated to illuminate the Strand. The
criminal waste of London's sneeze-power is quite
overlooked by modern scientific writers.
“Ku Klux ”: We have a family of
Smiths in our street, and about once a week
they have a row that reminds me of Sheol or
Mafeking day. Then the neighborhood gathers
together, and the word goes round: “The
Smiths are at it agen.” The women look at
each other and say, “Shocking!” “Dreadful!”
“ Scand’less ! ” but they listen with all their ears
to the compromising family disclosures made
during a Smith row. Occasionally the police
intervene—to everybody’s disappointment. They
arrived the other day just when Mrs. Smith
was pursuing Smith into the street with a
kettle of hot water. After the gathering had
departed to escort Mrs. Smith to the lock-up,
I heard a man ask Smith’s son what started it.
“Well,” said the lad, “dad got a ticket in
Tatt.’s. ’E ain’t got any result yet, but he
reckoned ’e’d get first ’orse, and ’e said ’e’d take
a trip to the old country ; and when mother arst
’im if ’e’d go without her, ’e said ‘ Wot in ’ell would
I take you for ? ’ An’ the nex’ thing I knoo the
sugar-basin ’it me on the bloomin’ lug. Mother
never could aim straight.”
“ Mars ” writes :
Re “F.J.B.’s” article (2/2/’O5). Educational faddism
began to run rampant over Australia about a decade ago.
South Australia started first. Then in Victoria there was
a sort of blind groping. Afterwards the chaos was
organised into a definite programme by that whale for
theory and mental development, Director F. Tate, at the
head of several imported educationalists. The old result
system, which considered a child an expansible bladder
to be stuffed with odd and sundry facts, was superseded
by a new system. This system was built upon the theory
that it is better to teach a child to use his natural faculties
for himself than fill him with facts without the power of
using them. For instance, Nature-study does not attempt
to teach biology or botany, but to train children in the
use of their eyes, &c. Drawing, brush work and manual
training do not teach painting or carpentry, but manual
dexterity, &c., and so on. These subjects win their place
if they develop these qualities, though they do no prac-
tical good. So much for the theory, which is beautiful.
But, the subjects are so many, and the special skill re-
quired to get the most good out of them so great, that
much of the good is lost. Only a few T , comparatively, of
the most enlightened teachers have grasped the spirit of
the new work, and so the old cram system is continued,
but with new subjects. It takes an educated brain of no
mean calibre to get order out of all this jumble, and
where is Victoria going to get such for the £ll2 10s. to
£165 per annum that rewards the rank and file of the
teachers ?
This is a startling allegation that comes so
late—that there is no record of the baptism of the
late Queen Victoria, and that it is quite probable
she was not christened at all. Reason, her
parents could not afford the costly splash neces-
sary for a royal infant. The parents of the late
Queen were so poor that they would not have
been able to get to England in time to make her a
little English infant if it had not been that their de-
voted friend, Alderman Wood (grand-uncle of Mr.
Charles Stuart Parnell and of Field Marshal Sir
Evelyn Wood) advanced them money for the ex-
penses of the journey. The yarn comes from
Modkkn Society.
Kate Carew, the American caricaturist, who
is always mentioned as “ the only woman carica-
turist,” gives an interesting account of herself in a
current English maga. She is, it seems, the wife
of a younger brother of Haddon Chambers.

A HEALTHY LIVER— A HEALTHY LIFE
Professor F. H. Griffin,
M.D., wrote:
“ I only wish every soul in the world used
Kutnow’s Powder; they would experience
great benefit. Four of my family besides
myself use it regularly.” It is a splendid
corrective for liver disorders. It is as mild
as the purest water, but as certain as the
rising of the sun. It is delightfully refresh-
ing as a health-draught, and removes the
severest headache almost instanter.
Professor /. N. Love,
M.D,, wrote:
“ The great surgeon, Professor Lawson
Tait, M.D., himself a sufferer from a gouty
diathesis, was not only one of the world s
greatest surgeons, but an able physician and
a skilful therapeutist. For years he found
Kutnow’s Powder of great value to him per-
sonally and in his practice, and this remedy
is one of the few he conspicuously endorsed
over his own signature. He realised, as all
careful, observing practitioners do, that it is
necessary to cleanse the system frequently,
to remove the debris of undigested food, and
to hasten the elimination of uric acid to
properly guard against serious disease. Kut-
now’s Powder stimulates the activities of the
bowels and kidneys. A dessert-spoonful in
a tumblerful of water taken immediately on
rising in the morning cleans out the bowels,
acts as a diluent, and flushes out the kidneys
in a most satisfactory way. Thorough
elimination is salvation, and there is no
better eliminator than Kutnow’s Powder.”
Late Professor of Clinical Medical Diseases of Children
and Hygiene, Marion-Sims College of Medicine, and
College of Physicians and Surgeons.
How to Secure Both.
How many people are there to-day in the British Isles who could
answer the greeting—“ How are you?" by saying, “ Very well,
thanks ! ’’
The percentage who could truthfully say, “ Very well, thanks,”
is very small, but really (with care) everyone ought to be always well.
Good health is your birthright, but, unfortunately, you have an
organ to keep in order called the liver, an unruly member.
If you have a strong, active liver, you will lead a strong, active
life, but you must keep the liver up to the mark.
If you find a difficulty with the liver, be careful to avoid drugs,
mercurial pills, and all drastic remedies —they hurt.
Adopt Kutuow’s Ponder, the liver encouraging remedy ; it acts
gently, yet, nevertheless, does its work with extraordinary efficiency.
If you wish to excel in brain-work you will find a dessert-spoon-
ful of Kutnow’s Powder of immense benefit. The same applies to
every action of our daily life—work, play, sleep—no matter what
you do, if you would do it well you must keep the great organs of the
body in perfect working order. The stomach needs cleansing, the
liver stimulating, the kidneys flushing, and the bowels clearing.
Herein lies the whole secret of health, and Kutnow’s Powder does
this for you, and does it perfectly.
IMPORTANT CAUTION!
Kutnow’s Powder is sold everywhere, but should you be shown a bottle
similar to the genuine Kutnow's Powder, bear in mind that to be
genuine every bottle must have the fac-simile signature, “ S. Kutnow
and Co., Ld.,” and the registered trade-mark, “ Hirschensprung, or
Deer Leap,” on the carton and label on the bottle. This signature
and trade-mark alone ensure your obtaining the
Genuine KUTNOW’S POWDER

We sell nearly three times as many Beale Australian Pianos in Australia as
are sold by any other maker, foreign or British.
We fought for and won our right to existence against extreme opposition by
foreign agents and those amongst musical “professors'’ who are their willing
satellites. All that in the teeth of freetrade, falsely so-called, which admitted
pianos free and placed duties on our materials.
There are seven times as many German Pianos imported as there are of
British and American added together. But only Pianos made in the climate are
fit to stand the climate.
Our name is on every instrument, whereas on imported pianos the names are
usually of unknown foreign makers or are wholly fictitious.
We sell direct from factory to user, and thus save you two or three heavy
loadings of profit besides freight, duty and charges.
Our prices are not more than half those of the leading makers in London
itself, and the Beale Pianos are in all respects demonstrably superior. Please
examine them and trust your own eyes, ears and intelligence.
If you send your money abroad you strengthen your commer ial and per-
haps your national enemies. If you spend it at home on an Australian piano
some of it must return to you, whilst your neighbours, anyhow, benefit. We dis-
tribute £9OO per week—say £45,000 per year —amongst Australians. In the
Arbitration Court last month (December) in the case of the Furniture Trade
Society and A Hordern <fc Sons (which case would affect us under the Common
llule) it was proved that Beale & Co.’s wages rates are higher than full union
rates. It was proved that we pay in the four branches embraced by our busi-
ness at the least ten percent, higher than union rates, and actually up to forty
per cent, higher on the average to cabinet makers than the Court award, which is
fixed for two years. Naturally we have the best men in the trade and pay them
from double to treble the wages paid to piano workmen in Germany. Of the
latter, at this moment, in the depth of the dread winter, thousands are on strike
in Berlin and Leipzig against their harsh conditions. The pianos imported from
Germany at £l5 (300 marks) are sold in Australia at £45. Why not obtain
real value from your own fellow-citizens and refuse to back the German sweater?
We offer you a Beale Australian Piano, full size, “College” Model, at £39
spot cash, ten times better in every respect than the £45 German article, and
guaranted for 25 years.
Advance Australia! And the only way to advance is along the lines of
industry all the time.
“The Most Advanced Industry of Australia.”

No firm of piano-builders in Europe calculates its own scales, designs, makes the patterns, makes its
own foundry-flasks and apparatus, moulds and casts its own iron frames.
No other firm designs, moulds, casts, machines, electroplates, and finishes its own brass and bronze work.
Even the castors (rollers), which it is impossible to buy of reliable strength and positiv e working, we
design and make ourselves in Australia.
We designed and built the traversing drilling machines which drill the patent iron frames each with 300
holes. We designed and built the tapping machines, with semi-automatic action, which make the screw-
threads in the patent frames.
We designed and built in our own shops the spinning-machines which turn out the patent hexagonal
bass-strings which alone can be guaranteed to remain true.
The entire installation of machinery, electrically driven, was erected by our own staff.
We have made and sold more than

Beale Pianos in Australia, and these, placed end to end, would reach five miles.
