TERMS.
REPAIRS and REPLACEHENTS.
We have all facilities at hand. All parts at all times in stoclc. When a
foreign piano is injured or requires restoration in any way, there are no parts ob-
tainable, for the name of the foreign makers is legion, and no importer reckons
to keep all parts in stock. When from rust, carelessness, or any cause strings in
foreign pianos have to be replaced—a frequent occurrence—no one here knows
the maker of the wire, consequently the new wire will not match or keep in tune
with the old. It cannot be helped, and buyers of foreign pianos must submit, yet
no piano-builder mixes up wires of different makers. Any restoration is and
must be a “ fake ” under such circmumstances, but the original builder can
always restore or replace as at first. Have an eye to the future and buy a Beale
Australian Piano.
TESTIHONIALS.
The recommendations of travelling artists are notoriously purchasable, and
must often be dearly bought. The leading trade periodical is Music Trades of
New York, which recently, in denouncing the practice, narrated the extreme case
of an old-established organ-making firm which began the manufacture of pianos
and proceeded to buy such recommendations. It was a fatuous policy, the firm
was unable to stand the cost, its business was taken over last year by a well-
known Chicago music-house, and the name alone remains. The testimonials we
offer proceed from the generosity of the ladies and gentlemen sending them. We
could not if we would, and we would not if we could, attempt to purchase any of
them. Thousan sof people, in all walks of life, have shown us the great kind-
ness to forward their favorable opinions, founded upon actual inspection. Others,
as from Calcutta, Fiji, and New Caledonia, give their expressions as to the con-
struction of our p'anos. You cannot have stronger assurance than the verdict of
the thousands who have bought and paid for Beale Pianos.
If you are not a possible buyer yourself, you will favor us with the names of
any of your acquaintances who may be intending to purchase a piano?
We make allowances for old pianos and supply our instruments for cash or
upon easy terms of payment. Remember, they are supplied direct from factory
to user, and are within the reach of all.
The Beale factories are fitted throughout with the most modern plant, elec-
trically lighted and driven. No patents for any parts of pianos have been taken
out in Australia from abroad, but we have many pitented improvements of our
own. Hence the entire field of discovery and practice is open to us, which is not
open to any other manufacturer in the world. Foreign traders cannot rob us of
those and other advantages.

A TORPID LIVER.
A CATALOGUE OF SYMPTOM
The Gao of Mr?. M. WING.
(By a Sydney Reporter.)
Events which havo evidently left an
impression upon the mind never to be
obliterated—events which at one timo
were beheld with grief and dismay—
are here related by Mrs. Maria Wing,
of Dora-street, Hurstville, upon whom
a pressman recently called, and was
courteously accorded an interview.
“It is twelve years ago now," ex-
plained Mrs. Wing, “since I took Cle-
ments Tonic for indigestion and neu-
ralgia, but if it were twice that time I
should never forget how much I owe
to that remedy for its powerful cura-
tive influence."
“Had your afflictions been in exis-
tence Jong ?"
“Quite seven years, so you can ga-
ther from that what a wreck I was.
Even from childhood I had been a
great sufferer from nervousness, and
each year found that complaint more
fully developed then ever. When neu-
ralgia started, the pains in my jaws,
all about my head, I ehind mv eyes,
and at the back of my neck and right
down my shoulders were unendurable.
Might after night passed by and not
an hour's sleep, with tl e result that,
by-and-bve. I felt almost da/ed for the
want of rest. 'J his, however, was not
the full extent of my ad .a rsities, for 1
daresay you know what indigestion is
like ? It meant that whenever I took
any food—and J did not have much at
a time, my ap| etile being so poor—[
got a feeling in the chest like a tight-
ness, as if had overeaten, and a
nasty, full, tir-comfortable feeling also
affected me in the stomach. But the
bilious attacks l had were horrible.
Some days, from the time I got up in
the morning till the sun went down in
the evening, I could do nothing but
retch, and on those particular occa-
sions I had such a fearful sensation in
mv head that I could not hold it up.
Talk’ about being depressed—why, that
was no name for it ; I was absolutely
melancholy, and what also helped me
to be so was a continual presentiment
that something was going to happen.
Try how I would L could not < t out
of my head the idea that some ! ind of
trouble was awaiting me, and if it
didn't come to-day I was sure it would
arrive to-morrow. Oh ! it was a life
that one should be pitied for leading,
and I'm sure I’ll do all I can to help
other poor women out of similar pre-
dicaments. Now T know that an infal-
lible cure is within the reach of every-
body, I consider it but light that I
fhould make known to the world what
that cure is, and for this reason alone
1 am actuated in going into full de-
tails. Yes, when those bilious attacks

I could not oven take a drink of water.
Nothing whatever would stay on the
stomach, so it’s nothing to marvel at
that I got as thin as .a shadow and as

that 1 had to contend with. It used to

“Those are symptoms arising from

“Kxactlv, and further evidence in

symptoms cither.”
“What medicine had you taken pre-
viously ?”
“Nearly every kind of pills and me-
dicines ever heard of, and there was
not one of them that was worth mv
while to refer to. Such useless trash J j
didn't think possible could exist, and
it just shows you how advisable it is
to relate such experiences as so l
that people’s money should not be
thrown away. Doctors, too, they used
to attend me and prescribe medicine,
but it never came to anything. I felt
no better after taking it, and that is
why, to tell you the truth, I started
to lose ho[ e, and think that L was
never going to get rid of my com-
plaints again. One thing that caused

down at night, but Clements Tonic

was entirely absent. So far so good, l
ihought ; but there was a lot for Cle-

“And was it equal to all your re-
quirements ?"
“Yes; every one of them, and what

Uric Acid. —The presence of this acid in the blood is the cause of people suffering from Rheu-
matism, Gout, Neuralgia, Backache, Lumbago, Sciatica, Gravel and Bladder Troubles. The accumu-
lation of uric acid in the blood is a certain indication that the kidneys are acting inefficiently.
Warner’s Safe Cure acts specifically upon the kidneys, and invariably restores those vital organs to
health and activity, when all suffering due to uric poisoning ceases. So potent is Warner’s Safe Cure
that it will even cure Bright’s Disease of the kidneys.
A simple test to make as to the condition of the kidneys is to put some of the urine, passed the
first thing in the morning, into a bottle or covered glass, and let it stand until next morning. If it is
then cloudy, or if it contains a sediment like brick-dust, or if particles float about in it, or it is of ap
unnatural color, the kidneys are not healthy, and no time should be lost iu commencing to take
Warner’s Safe Cure. [6].
The Joyful Truant.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
GLORIA BURNS TO DOROTHY FITZPATRICK.
MY DEAR OLD FITZIE,—I’m writing to
you with a tear in one eye, and a wicked,
wicked wink in the other. Just think of
it! I have been expelled, and the cause was
nothing more nor less than stopping out all night
with a man. There was no public expulsion. I
was ordered to the lecture-room, where the Old
Dame —she didn’t have the necessary pluck herself
—had sent the “ Little Minister ” to deal with me.
(Oh ! hut he did look as though he would have
liked to have been the man.)
“The full extent of your—er—escapade is
known to the authorities,” he said ; “ but, for ob-
vious reasons, the particulars have been kept from
the other girls.” I was requested to announce m /
withdrawal from the school, and so on, and so on.
You can understand just how I received him, but
the little fool affected an air of martyrdom, and,
plunking down on his knees, offered up a prayer.
Fitzie, if you want to know the utter depravity of
man, catch a parson praying for your soul’s salva-
tion. the while leering out of the tail of his eye at
the lips of soft lace that kiss a woman’s ankle. I
was never so ashamed in my life. I wanted to
shake the life out of him, but —I was crying.
I only remember wishing, with a great big sob,
that Jack were there to catch the sanctimonious
little prig up in his arms, and throw him out of
the window ; then I slipped out at the door, leav-
ing him on his knees.
But, dear Fitzie, you must be dying to know all
about my awful, yet interesting crime.
Well, last Wednesday afternoon, our class went
to Fern Tree Gully, with the Old Dame and the
old Professor, for botany ; and there we camped.
I fancy I can hear the old Professor droning away
now. Good old Bladder o’ Lard ! Do you re-
member the day a spider started out to traverse
that spotlesss head ? There was the sweep of the
hand time after time, and I know you laughed.
But the spider kept on and on, until, in a moment
of mental aberration —splash ! —and it was no
more.
Well, Fitzie, the old Professor kept mono-
tonously on. The Old Dame snored peacefully in
her folding-chair. There was the hum of bees
and the droning of many insects. An impulse
seized me to wander away and stretch myself in
the shade of some hidden tree. A little while,
and I struck the main road. Climbing over a
fence to it, I sat for a moment or two upon the
top rail, watching a buggy coming from the
distance. In that buggy was the cause of all my
trouble—Jack !
“Hullo, Glorious! You here? How glad I
am,” he shouted.
He was invited to keep silent in his gladness.
Then I told him in an undertone how I was play-
ing truant.
He laughed at first, but after a while became
grave—he always does when meaning mischief.
Then he said : “I don’t suppose you’re game to
come for a drive, little girl ? ”
“Little girl” and “game.” Ah. Fitzie! he
told me afterwards that he knew my weakness.
In a moment we were off, bound for Auntie
Ruth’s, where the fine mulberries grow.
I don’t think I shall ever forget that drive,
Fitzie; for, after I had finished making fun of
Jack, and he had grown almost savagely silent,
and as we drove along the narrow, hilly, winding
road I became dreamy, and—and—ah ! the sense
of it is in my brain, but the word—there is no
word.
I spoke to Jack as in a dream. In an instant
he had an arm around me, and I was awakened.
I thrust him away almost fiercely, but I know that
he understood me, for, after a pause, he asked,
blushing still:
“ Do you know what you called me ? ”
I put my hands to my ears. I had a faint recol-
lection.
“ You called me ‘ Jack, dear.’ Didn’t you mean
it, Gloria ? ” he asked.
But we had pulled up at his Aunt Ruth’s place,
so I jumped clear, saying, “ I’m not quite certain
that it should not have been ‘Jack, darling’!”
You should have seen his face then, even though
he knew I was laughing at him.
Dear old Auntie Ruth ! She called me “My
Morning Glory,’’ just as she used to call me when
I was quite a little girl; and oh ! I was ever so
much in love with her then.
But the mulberries, Fitzie ! My lips are red
with them yet, and I have a one-time white dress
that I shall not wear again until the day after I
am married, for there is a huge stain in the lap of
it where I upset a bowl of berries when a lad
came, saying, “Mr. Jack has met with an acci-
dent, Miss Burns.” But it wasn’t all mulberry-
juice, Fitzie. Jack’s head lay there, and he was
bleeding. (You used to call me “a girl of the
grotesque.” How does mulberry-juice and lover’s
blood go ?)
The accident occurred very simply. Uncle Ned
was out in one of the far paddocks. Jack had to
see him, and saddled a horse to ride out. He
tried to jump the horse over the first gate, but it
swerved and tangled itself up in the wire fence.
Jack was thrown and the horse rolled on him,
crushing out all appearance of life.
I forgot truancy then. I had Jack to look
after. He was unconscious for over an hour, and,
Fitzie, I prayed then, for the first time in my life,
willingly. Perhaps some wandering angel took
pity on the little love-smitten maiden in her grief ;
perhaps Jack’s great strength and his youth
worked the wonder, but all I know is that, before
there was time for a doctor to arrive, Jack was
sitting up in a chair, white and very shaky, but
smiling, and protesting that he wasn’t hurt
seriously.
“ I’ll have to take you back at once, or you’ll
catch it,” he said to me, when he learned what
time it was ; but the doctor came then, and there
was nearly two hours’work bandaging contusions
and anointing bruises, and when Jack was delivered
to us, bound-up and plastered, and with his right
arm hanging almost useless by his side, tea was on
the table.
“ We’ve done it now, Gloria,” he said. “ Shall
we stay ? ”
“Might as well be hanged for a sheep as a
lamb,” I answered —there were mulberries on the
board. Besides, Auntie Ruth had been led to
believe that her “ Morning Glory ” was having a
holiday, and had no need to return to the school
until 10 o’clock; so, for the sake of appearances, we
couldn’t rush away supperless.
Well, Fitzie, Jack and I didn’t get a start back
to town until nearly 8 o’clock ; but it was a beauti-
ful evening, and I didn’t trouble much about the
Old Dame, for as we drove along “ we two” con-
cocted a perfectly lovely tale to tell her, and I
reckoned I would only have to answer for leaving
the class without permission.
H was. Jack’s intention to drive me to his
s place, where I could stay all night, and
then for him to take me into school next morning;
out, alas, Fitzie, just after we had left the Gully,
the effects of the poor boy’s fall overpowered him,
and he almost collapsed. We had been having
such a good time driving along, too. I felt him
shiver, heard his breath coming and going in little
short gasps, and, looking up quickly, saw his face
was strained with pain, and white.
“Shall I take the reins, Jack?” I asked him.
He braced himself up. “No, it’s all-right—
Gloria! ” but, oh, Fitzie, I don’t think there is any-
thing so horrible as watching a man—the man you
love—fighting against a faint. He swayed about
with the movement of the buggy like one without
power.
“ Can’t I do something for you, Jack ?”
He shook his head, but, in a moment or two—
“lf you don’t mind, Gloria, just put your —your
arm around me, and steady me a bit, will you ? ”
“If I didn’t mind ! Oh, Fitzie, I held him close
enough—not but that he will hold me closer some
time, I hope—and then—and then, just as we were
fetting along famously—smack !—Jack and I
ung to the ground—the horses dashing along the
track with a portion of battered buggy banging
their heels, and working them into frenzy !
I was stunned for a moment or two. Jack lay
motionless. His right arm was twisted under his
chest in a way that made me shudder.
“ Jack,” I called to him. “Jack ! ” The sough-
ing of the wind in the leaves answered me —a
seeming song of death.
I leaned over him, fearful of all things; yet, ere
I comprehended, I had turned him on his back.
There was a film of dust across his eyes, and his
lips were bleeding.
“ Jack,” I cried again, “ Jack, darling, won’t
you speak to me ? ”
Ah, Fitzie, you will wonder how I managed it,
but I dragged Jack clear of the track. Then I
looked to see if there were any lights near or in
the distance. There was darkness only. I went
in search of water. There was none near the
roadside. I wondered if there were any houses
near, and set up a great cry; not even an echo
came to me. I felt tears in my eyes and blamed
God that they were salt. I rushed along the road
looking for succour. There was nothing but trees,
trees, trees ! I dashed into the scrub, glancing
fearfully behind me at every step lest I should
miss the way. “Water!” I cried; “water!”
and Jack’s bleeding face looked at me wherever I
went. But it was no use, the couritry was dry. I
longed for one of those cool, crystal creeks in the
ranges. Then suddenly a picture came to me ; the
remains of a swagman’s camp alongside the road
as we were driving. Perhaps ! —perhaps!—as I ran
the “perhaps” seemed to grow into certainty.
I reached the camp. There were ashes, a few
ends of wooden matches, a crust of cheese, a crust
of bread. These things seemed to be all, and, in
bitter anger, I turned away. But, ah ! Under
a tree close by was a meat-tin ; beside it the marks
of a dog’s paws. The swagman had given his
dog a drink there, and the tin was half full of
water. Jack hadn’t moved, but his lips were still
bleeding, and I knew that was a sign of life. I
had undone his collar before, and had taken his
coat off and made him a pillow of it. Now I
washed his face and neck and hands, propping
him up that the blood from his lips should not
clot in his throat. I forced some of the water
down his throat, too, and that the dust in his eyes
might not cause him pain, I squirted little tiny
sprays of water beneath the lids.
He came to himself after a weary, weary while,
saying suddenly :
“ Are the horses all right? ”
The horses. How like a man !
I told him they were, and he said in a wander-
ing sort of way : “ And Gloria, dear little Gloria,
where is she ? ”
Of course, I cried out asking him if he did not
know me. Then, with a great light in his eyes,
he seemed to remember. “ You're not hurt, little
girl—are you ? ” But I saw him wince and look
down to his right arm. I knew it was broken.
“Give’s a hand to get up,” he said. I did, but
he fell back, groaning.
There was a pause, then, “ Gloria, Gloria ! ” he
cried ; but I was standing away from him, behind
a tree, taking off an underskirt. I returned, tear-
ing it into strips.
“ What’s that for ? ” he asked ; but for answer
I tore off the sleeve of his shirt, and took gentle
hold of his arm. The bones of the forearm were
broken, and the broken ends grated together. I
cried out myself at the very sound of it, but Jack
stood the pain like an Australian.
“ Broken,” he simply said.
“I am going to bind it,” I replied; and, as
best I could, I set the broken bones, end to end,
and twisted the strips of skirt about it evenly and
close. It did not answer as well as I thought, the
weight of his hand seeming to give the arm an
ugly, sickening bend. So I went behind the
tree for more bandages. A thought had come to
me of my weak ankle, and of the elastic bandage I
was forced to wear. It was like the thought of
sleeping with one’s stocking round one’s neck to
cure a sore throat; but Jack says he will never
part with that bandage now, and the doctor who
set the broken arm reckons that I could have
thought of nothing better.
Well, Fitzie, when I had fixed Jack’s arm in
a sling he wanted to look after the horses, but his
legs wouldn’t work and he was forced to lie down
again.
“ They’ll keep on down the road till somebody
checks them,” I said. “Then we’re sure to be
looked for, ” I added hopefully.
“But we’re not on the main road now,” he
replied. “ I was taking you a short-cut, and
people are not likely to come along here at night.
There ought to be a gate not more than half-a-
mile down the track, though.”
I went to that gate. No horses were there, not
even a sign of them. I went back.
“ Isn’t there a house near, Jack?” I asked him.
“ There’s one about three miles further on, and
there’s a township about two-and-a-half miles
back.”
I shivered ; all that way alone, and in the dark.
But I said, “Do you think you will be all right
whilst lam away, Jack ? You can’t stop here all
night, you know.”
“ By Jove, if you go I’ll go with you,” he said,
striving to get up. But he strove in vain, and at
last I was forced to say, “ Well, Jack, if you won’t
keep still, I shall run straight away at once.”
And what do you think he said, Fitzie. Why—
“ You brave little Glorious!” Then with a
twinkle in his eyes. “ I tell you what, little ’un;
I ’ll keep as still as a church if you ’ll tell me one
thing.”
“ Well,” I answered.
“ Promise,” he said.
I promised.
“ What was it you called me when I was deaf
just now ? ”
“Jack,” I said, saucily.
“ Anything else? ”
“Jack—darling ! ”
He tried to draw my lips down to his, but I
drew back.
“Won’t you let me kiss you, Gloria?” he
pleaded.
“ I’ve not long washed your lips with water that
a swagman’s dog had drunk of,” I answered ; and,
as he still pressed me, “No, no!” I oried. But
IT T,-. • , „ . T
all the same, Fitzieyou may be very sure that I
was on the left-hand side of him, so that at last,
he might draw me to him, for I was shaking like
a leaf, was almost crying, and my throat was full
of sobs. After all that had happened I wanted to
be petted and kissed and made much of, and J ack
just kept on kissing till I had cried out all my
fears and pain and trouble on his shoulder. Oh !
but it took a long time.
I sat by him all that night, dear, and—you know
what is coming—some day within two years—l
promised to wait that long—l’ve given my word
to become Mrs. Jack. You should lust see my
engagement-ring.
But the worst comes now. Dear old Auntie
Ruth was in Melbourne next morning, and,
thinking of Jack’s accident at the farm, called at
the school to get a direction to his place from. me.
I wasn’t there, and, of course, the dear old soul
told the Old Dame all about the joyful truant,
right up to the time of leaving .the farm at 8
o’clock with Jack. The Old Dame said nothing
to Aunt, but made enquiries of address, and
posted off to Jack’s place. I wasn’t there, neither
was he. She hammered two and two together,
and clinched it with her worst construction. When
she came back I was at the school. She didn’t
even frown, just asked me what I had to say for
myself. Now, if I had told the truth things might
have been fixed up without much trouble. But, the
truth to her ! Even you found that irksome at
times. So she cornered me without showing her
hand, then left me to consult with the “Little
Minister.”
I ve been stopping at Jack s place since, looking
after him. Dad came down for me yesterday,
He was very indignant at first with the Old
Dame, but when I told him everything he laughed
and said it was all my own fault and Jack s.
And now, Fitzie, I have told you all. So T
finish up with a kiss for you, dear—three, in fact,
one for each cheek, and one for the lips of the
sweetest mouth in the world, though, like the
mouth of Lorna Doone, it is large enough to speak
the truth with. —Yours ever, Gloria.

MILKY WHITE.
A family of Norwegians, brought out by a
relative at Cairns (Q.) under the immigration
regulations, arrived in Brisbane to find that they
still had a journey to cover which would cost £2O
in passage money. Two members of the Labor
party came to their rescue by guaranteeing fares.
The New York “tough” is up-to-date in
his methods. Twice within last Christmas week
a woman was found, on a quiet riverside drive in
New York, with emptied pockets and a broken
neck. In each case the robbers had used the new
Japanese jiu-jitsu method of homicide,
“H.W.”: I haven’t seen any referenee
made to the striking resemblance between George
Reid’s face, as depicted by “Hop,” and the
markings on the moon. One evening, Luna
being three days off the full, I left the pub after
closing hour, and, making my uncertain way
along the bush track, pondered from State Par-
liament to Federal, and from Federal to George
Reid. Then, happening to look up, I saw that
individual gazing at me with happy smile. Part
of his face was invisible, which led me to the
conclusion that George was on a wild “jag,” and
had tilted his plug hat over his right optic.
Next evening, to satisfy myself that I had not
imagined all this, I looked at the moon again.
George was still there. A little less real, cer-
tainly; but. judging from appearances, on a
wilder “jag” than ever.

Kidneys
Consider what it means to neglect the kidneys.
They are the great blood filters of the body. All the
blood passes through them once every three minutes, day
and night, to be filtered and purified. Thus the kidneys
remove about 500 grains of impure matter every 24 hours.
So the kidneys are working day and night to keep you
in health. But when they get sick they do not do this
filtering process properly, and some of the uric acid and
waste matters stay in the system. These impurities are
the cause of most diseases.
sdf

KIDNEY
PILLS
are made to heal and help the kidneys in their great work.
They take out the uric acid (the acid that causes rneuma-
tism, gravel, gout, heart disease, sciatica, and neuralgia);
they take out the water that causes the dropsical
swellings; they relieve backaches, correct the flow from
the kidneys, &c.
But your neighbor’s word, given here, tells the whole
story.
SYDNEY PROOF.
Mr. George Woodroffe, Roseby-street, Drummoyne, writes : —“I am pleased
to be able to tell you of the very great benefit I have derived from the use of
Doan’s Backache Kidney Pills. I have been a great sufferer with the kidneys for
the past 17 years. It started with a cold, and eventually inflammation of the
kidneys and bladder set in.
“ I have been treated by some of the cleverest and best-known Sydney
doctors, and was for seventeen and a half weeks in Sydney Hospital, and also two
months in the Coast Hospital, where I had one kidney removed, in order to save
my life, the doctor said ; and though the various treatments eased me for a time,
I was not cured, and would soon relapse into my former condition ; in fact, I
seemed to get worse each time. I also tried all kinds of patent medicines adver-
tised to cure kidney complaint, but they did not give me even temporary relief.
One doctor said I could never be cured ; and after so many failures to get a cure,
and the years of suffering, I began to think he was right.
“I was so bad that to work was almost impossible. My brother kept on
worrying me to try Doan’s Pills, if I only took one box. To please him, I got a
box and took a few doses. They seemed to make me worse, and I said I would
take no more. He prevailed on me to go on with them. I commenced taking
them again, and before I had finished the box I felt extraordinary well. I had
not felt so well for years ; the pills were working wonders for me. I continued
taking them, and am now cured of the headaches and backache, and I sleep well
at night. These pills have been a Godsend to me, and I feel that it is a pleasure
to live. My friends tell me the improvement in me already is positively wonder-
ful.”
Seven months later Mr. Woodroffe states :—“ I have not taken the pills for
some time. lam perfectly cured. I can do heavier work than I have been able
to do for years. lam not troubled with the secretions or pains in my back, and
have gained a stone in weight during the past three months.”

Hygeia.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
ODDESS, blithe and young and fair,
I jj" With the brow so broad and noble,
And the eyes undimmed by trouble,
And the lips that laugh at care,
And the brown limbs fleet and free —
Hast thou quite forgotten me ?
Maid divine, dost thou not mind
When we raced the streams together,
In the mad, glad winter-weather,
While thy hair streamed on the wind
Like a flying flame of gold ?
Ah, the vanished days of old !
Lady, bright, dost thou forget
When we wandered, we two only,
By the side of waters lonely,
From sunrising to sunset,
And I made a Rhyme for thee
Full of magic melody?
When the morn’s flag was unfurled,
Thou wert with me, rapture bringing,
While my heart a song was singing
Of the Beauty of the World—
Does the morn no longer glow ?
Was it all so long ago ?
On the purple hills afar
Are thy swift feet gleaming, gliding ?
Or art thou o’er grey plains riding
Underneath the Morning Star?
Maid divine, my fear is sore
That I ne’er shall see thee more.
The Goddess of Health dwells in far-off places.
If you are living by the sea, her feet are beautiful
upon the mountains; if your home is on the
mountains, she wanders oy the shore of the
many-laughing main. The one thing certain
is that she never lives in your immediate
neighborhood—supposing, of course, that you
have reached a stage in life when you need her
help badly. If you have not reached that stage,
give thanks, but do not boast. The arrow fiieth at
noonday.
« Dr « *
Three eminent medical authorities informed me
that, so far as I was concerned, there was no salu-
brity in Sydney. The kind of health I wanted could
only be found on the other side of the Mountains,
upon some high table-land, beyond the reach of
the sea-breeze, where the air is pure and dry, and
there falls not rain nor hail nor any snow. Or
words to that effect. An enthusiastic friend ad-
vised me to go to Orange, in the vicinity of which,
he said, he had spent the happy years of his boy-
hood. “ All the best people know me,” he added.
“ You have only to mention my name, and you
will be treated like a prince. Nothing will be too
good for you.” He offered me letters of introduc-
tion, but I thought that they would be unneces-
sary in the circumstances. Orange is, I learnt from
other sources, the true home of Hygeia. She has
favored haunts elsewhere, as Yenus had, but Orange
is her Cythera. Once I arrived there, I might
safely throw physic to the dogs. Stimulants also.
The local air is a delicious combination of balm and
ambrosia, and the fortunate inhabitants walk about
the streets wreathed in perpetual smiles —the
result of subtle atmospheric intoxication. Orange
was evidently the place for me. I went there.
• * * *
It was night when I arrived, and I had to stay
at an hotel. The hotel was full of Christmas
visitors, but a room was found for me with a nar-
row slit of a window at one end of it, and space
enough inside for a bed, washstand, and a chair.
I went to sleep with a sick-headache, but with the
hope of making glad discoveries, and verifying all
the fine things I had heard, in the morning. . . .
The morning rushed in on me with a roar. Just
outside my slit of window, which opened on the
verandah, two Christmas visitors were struggling
up and down in the fierce embrace of battle. It
was the pounding of their heavy boots on the
boards, and their howls of rage that aroused me.
They exhaled an odor which was not that of am-
brosia. It was the smell of whisky. I knew it
again. Suddenly they disengaged, and lumbered
down to the bar—to take in a fresh stock of their
warlike motive power, I suppose. I got up,
and also went downstairs. The landlord, a stout,
florid person, with a mildly-humorous eye, asked
me if I had slept well.
“ I thought you told me that this was a quiet
hotel,” I said by way of reply.
“So it is,” he remarked ; “ too quiet, worse
luck. But things will be livelier next week, when
the races are on.”
# * * #
The first observation I made, after leaving the
landlord, gave me a favorable impression of the
climate of Orange. There were several servant-girls
in the hotel, and they were all fine, big, strapping
wenches, with broad shoulders, and arms fit to
pillow the heads of giants. I noticed subsequently
that most of the women of Orange are of larger
build than their sisters in Sydney. The former-
speaking of them as vessels, which is an old poetical
licence —have a nobler “displacement” than the
latter. There must be 3ome curious forcing influence
in the air of Orange. The boarders staying at the
hotel were mostly men from the country—big, sun-
baked young men who had come into the town to
have a good time. They were not, with some ex-
ceptions, riotous, or even noisy, but their look of
superfluous strength irritated me. Fortunately I
met with another invalid who was also staying at
the hojfcel—a small, thin man who had led a most
varied and picturesque life all over the world. He
was an Irishman and had been, amongst other
things, a trusted official of the Boer Government.
As a companion he was a God-send, and we
agreed with regard to most questions; but he would
have it that his malady was worse than mine,
which was absurd. I had often smiled at this
pathetic vanity of invalids. Now I know it has
a reason in nature. A man must be proud of
something. One day he is reduced to being proud
of his decrepitude.
• • * *
I triumphed over my friend very soon. I had a
relapse. A doctor was called in. He wrote a
prescription and told me that he could do nothing
more for me, and that, although I might take a
turn for the worse, I might also be better in the
morning. I was scared, but not so badly as I
expected to be. There was no time. The trouble
was not coming ; it was in the room. None of the
thoughts supposed to be usual on such occasions
passed through my brain. I had a feeling of
vague remorse for a misspent life, but it was not
strong enough to force itself on my consciousness.
A hurried half-conjecture was all I had to give to
the Unknown Future, The thought that held me
in its grip was— lsat coming now ? I shall never
again believe in oeath-bed repentances—except,
perhaps, in the cage of sinners who die of old age.
It was a long, long lonesome night. But I fell
asleep after all.
• • • •
After some days I was able to walk slowly up the
main street and inhale the boasted air of Orange.
It was hot and full of dust. I sat down in a small
rectangular public park, through which runs a
noble waste-water channel, dug out by the muni-
cipality, and, no doubt, a solace to persons of
leisure who walk upon its banks. There seemed
to be quite a number of these fortunate individuals
in the park. All the seats were occupied by them.
I noticed that they were mostly old, which also
speaks well for the climate. I got into conversa-
tion with one, and mentioned two or three names
of residents supplied to me by my enthusiastic-
friend in Sydney. The ancient man laughed till
the tears ran down his ancient nose.
“Why,” he said, “X went insolvent and left
Orange years ago, Y died of drink, and Z —that old
man with the red nose and long grey beard, on the
seat yonder—is supported by his grandchildren.”
I didn’t ask about any of the others.
» * * •
The fetish of Orange is its thermometer, which
is kept at the post-office, in a nice, coo’,
shady place. The official record of heat
is—so I was informed by a man who has no
chance of making money out of visitors—
never allowed to get as high as lOOdeg. “ They’d
smash the thermometer first,” he observed. One
day during the heat-wave I was talking for a
moment with a blacksmith who had come to the
door of his smithy to get a breath of air. “My
oath,” he said, “it’s a scorcher out here.” And
he went back to his glowing forge with a sigh of
relief.
• * * *
I am now staying at a quiet boarding-house.
There are several young men, two or three
daughters of the house, a piano, almost a new
baby, belonging to a lady-lodger, and seventy
fowls in the establishment. The landlady is a
nice, motherly person, who seems to take an
interest in me. _ She says I am the living image
of her brother-in-law Tom, who died of drink.
On the second evening after I arrived she ap-
peared to think I was in low spirits, and (dear,
kindly soul!) told me a story to cheer me up.
“ I had a young fellow staying with me once,
and he had just the same thing the matter with
him that you have. His people were rich, and he
had a doctor with him in the house to look after
him. The doctor occupied the very room you are
in. There was nothing spared. The best port-
wine, and sprays, and tabloids for killing germs,
and scented pastilles to burn in the room, and
everything you could think of, and more, that
young man had. He was getting on well, too,
and after a few months could run up and down
the stairs as well as anybody. Then he left me,
and I heard no more of him for several weeks.”
“ And then ? ”
“He got low-spirited about his weight, and shot
himself, poor young fellow.”
And this was the person who had just the same
thing the matter with him that I have !
V. J. DALEY.
When Willie was Dead.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“% M J E’VE ’ad our tea,” Mother said, as
\ \ Father entered the kitchen “ aven’t
T T we?”
The two children nodded; and Father, after
glancing round doubtfully, sat down at the table,
half of which was set for the meal. The other
half was bare, save for the lamp, a penny bottle of
ink, and several pieces of paper. At that end
Tommy, the boy, sat writing.
“Y’ must eat, y’ know, Dad,” said Mother.
“ It oarn’t make no difference, now the little chap’s
,” and she put a dish of stew before her
husband.
Father ate stolidly for some moments; then,
with his fork, he made a gesture towards Tommy.
“What’s Tom doin’ ?” he asked.
“Never you mind now,” replied Mother.
“ Wait till y’ve finished tea. What’s the use of
upsettin’ yourself ? ”
Father finished his meal in silence, whilst Tom
wrote upon and tore up sheet after sheet of paper.
Mother went behind him, and looked over his
shoulder.
“ Cam’t you get it, my son ? ” she asked.
“ The larst line beats me,” replied Tommy.
“ It’s a bit of poetry for the paper, about Willie,”
Mother explained. “ We’re goin’ to take it to the
office to-night, Father. Tommy’s writin’ it.”
Tommy blushed. Father nodded.
“ I thought about that,” he said. “I thought
we ought to put somethin’ in, though it carn’t do
the pore boy no good now.”
Mother wiped away a tear.
“Cheer-up, Mary,” Father said, patting her
arm. “ Carn’t you get it, Tommy ? ”
“It’s the larst line,” said Tommy. “I want
somethin’ to rhyme with ‘best’.”
“ Read it out,” said Father, and Tommy read :
Little Willie, we have missed you,
But we know it’s for the best,
For we know you are an angel bright—
“ Somethin’ to go with ‘ best,’ he said anxiously.
Father took the paper and read the lines over
and over again, muttering them under his breath.
He scratched his head. “ Guest, lest, chest, pes—
rest,” he murmured. Then he said aloud, “And
have gone to the Heavenly rest. That’ll do.”
Tommy took the paper, wrote the words down,
and read:
“ Little Willie, we have missed you,
But we know it’s for the best,
For we know you are an angel bright,
And have gone to the Heavenly rest.”
“ My pore, pore, dead son !” Mother cried, and
burst into tears as Father wrote on the paper :
“Inserted by his sorrowing father, mother, sister
and brother.”
The angel Consolation comes in strange guises.
S.A.
A. E. MARTIN.

The 40 State scholarships competed for by
the youths of Victoria have been duly announced,
and 60 per cent, of the passes were obtained by
the sons of teachers of State schools. When the
head-teacher of a school is coaching half-a-dozen
youths, including his son, for a scholarship, it seems
fair to presume that the best of the tuition will go
to the teacher’s son. That is unfair; yet to bar
teachers’ sons would also be unfair. There does
not seem an easy way out, except to do away with
the need of the scholarships by making secondary
education free.
